Pro Private Markets
Venture Firms Bet Big on Star Biotech Entrepreneurs

By Brian Gormley 
644 words
17 August 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Health-care venture capitalists are placing some of their largest bets on startups formed by star entrepreneurs able to win investor confidence despite being years away from putting a drug on the market.

Top health-care venture rounds in the first half included Human Longevity Inc .'s $220 million Series B, Aptinyx Inc.'s $65 million Series A, and a $51.5 million Series A for Morphic Therapeutic . Well-known entrepreneurs formed all three and amassed the funds despite a general slowdown in health-care venture investment.

J. Craig Venter , who sequenced the human genome early last decade through Celera Genomics , which he formed in 1998, now runs a large-scale DNA-sequencing operation at Human Longevity. Combining genomic, clinical and other data with large-scale computing and artificial intelligence, Human Longevity aims to tackle cancer and other diseases amid a quest to extend the human lifespan. Investors such as GE Ventures and Celgene Corp . are bankrolling this vision, supplying the company with $300 million in Series A and B financing since its formation in 2013.

Venture firms also took aggressive approaches with Morphic Therapeutic and Aptinyx, betting it was best to enable them to concentrate on drug development instead of fundraising. Morphic Chief Executive Praveen Tipirneni said some investors wouldn't discuss a deal unless he was willing to raise a large round, one that would enable it to prove the concept of its approach in clinical trials.

Morphic, which aims to treat inflammatory-bowel disease and other conditions through drugs targeting integrin receptors, was founded by integrin-research pioneer Timothy Springer. Drug companies have brought six integrin medicines to market but have also seen several failures in clinical trials. Morphic formed last year to use Dr. Springer's research to clear technical hurdles that felled previous efforts.

The company, whose backers include Polaris Partners and venture arms of Pfizer Inc ., GlaxoSmithKline PLC and AbbVie Inc ., intends to develop an oral version of the Entyvio, an infused inflammatory-bowel disease therapy from Takeda Pharmaceutical Co . that earned U.S. regulatory approval in May 2014.

VCs spun Aptinyx out of venture-backed Naurex Inc . after selling the neurological drug developer to Allergan PLC in a $560 million deal in August 2015. Allergan bought Naurex to secure antidepressants that may deliver rapid relief by modulating a receptor known as NMDA. Aptinyx, led by former Naurex CEO Norbert Riedel , plans to develop NMDA modulators for other conditions with the $65 million round he raised in May. Aptinyx, which launched initial clinical trials in July in healthy volunteers, could pursue conditions such as PTSD and traumatic brain injury.

Aptinyx was somewhat of a departure for investor Longitude Capital , which typically finances clinical-stage drug companies. But the firm was confident the Aptinyx management could use the same technology platform from Naurex to develop new products quickly, Managing Director Patrick Enright said.

Raw startups weren't the only ones closing large rounds in the first half. Acutus Medical Inc . rounded up $75 million in March from Deerfield Management , Xeraya Capital and others to fund the launch of tools to enable better detection and treatment of atrial fibrillation, an irregular heartbeat that can lead to stroke and other illnesses.

Similarly, PanTheryx Inc . collected $53 million from Pegasus Capital Advisors in March to release a medical-nutrition product for traveler's diarrhea and infectious diarrhea. The product, DiaResQ, delivers nutrients to the small intestine to promote the repair of damage caused bacteria or viruses. Although traveler's diarrhea is a good-sized market, the larger opportunity lies with infectious diarrhea, according to Chief Executive Mark Braman . Diarrheal disease is the second-leading cause of death in children under age 5, according to the World Health Organization .

ANALYSIS
Rise of the DIY algo traders; FT BIG READ: INVESTMENT

By Robin Wigglesworth 
2,110 words
17 August 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
09
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Online platforms that 'crowdsource' trading strategies are attracting tech-savvy amateurs keen to develop winning algorithms. But will they be able to consistently beat the market and take on the hedge funds? By Robin Wigglesworth

By day Dan Houghton helps run Chilango , a Mexican restaurant chain in London that he co-founded. By night, he is an algorithmic stock trader, coding complex, automated investment strategies once his children are in bed.

In 2013 Mr Houghton sold an online texting company that he founded while studying mathematics at Cambridge university. The proceeds were ploughed into his restaurant and a new family home, but rather than deposit the remainder in a staid mutual fund he decided to use his maths skills to try to beat the market.

After reading books on finance and scouring the internet for articles on "quantitative" investing, he stumbled over a company tailor-made for people like him: Quantopian, an online trading platform and "crowdsourced" hedge fund. The company is designed for a new breed of do-it-yourself algorithmic traders who develop automated investment strategies. Each month, the best "algos" win trading money from Quantopian and a select few are included in its embryonic hedge fund.

"My wife thought I was crazy," Mr Houghton says. He had to learn Python - a programming language popular among quantitative analysts, or "quants" - from scratch, and after experimenting with the platform for a few months is trying to get one of his algorithms included in Quantopian's hedge fund.

So far he has made $7,971 after trading commissions, but making money is not the main driver. "It's primarily intellectual curiosity but I'm fairly competitive and the chance of getting included [in Quantopian's fund] makes me stay up for the extra hour working on a strategy," he says.

"Burrito-Dan", as he is known at Quantopian, is one of a swelling number of mathematicians, programmers, data engineers and physicists who are taking advantage of cheap, powerful computers and the availability of financial data to code their own trading strategies. These DIY algo traders are an updated version of the army of day-traders that emerged during the 1990s stock market boom.

Some analysts believe this new wave of amateur algo traders can be harnessed using crowdsourcing techniques to disrupt one of Wall Street's elite professions - the hedge fund manager.

"If our model is successful there will be no need for hedge funds any more," says Martin Froehler, an Austrian mathematician who created Quantiacs , one of several online platforms for DIY algo traders. "A smart guy with a laptop will be able to start his own hedge fund. It will be very challenging to the big incumbents. A very simple idea can prove very powerful."

Most day traders are ultimately unsuccessful - many were wiped out when the dotcom bubble burst - and are often derided by professional money managers as "dumb money". The new DIY algo traders may be smarter and more computer-savvy than the average day trader but many experts warn they will find it just as hard to consistently beat the market. They scoff at the idea that their industry will face any threat from nerds coding in their basement.

But Quantiacs and its rivals are emerging as the classic hedge fund model is showing signs of strain . Investors have been pulling billions of dollars from hedge funds after a long period of underwhelming performance. And the fact that some technologists scent an opening underscores the sense of a looming industry shift, as more scientific, computer-driven approaches to investing gain favour.

Monetising pure data

Khaled Sharif, a young Jordanian computer engineer, arrived in the US in June to work at Nasa's Ames Research Center, the space agency's Silicon Valley outpost. Mr Sharif is a research assistant who specialises in deploying artificial intelligence techniques such as "machine learning" to crunch data on satellite images, weather patterns and earthquakes. But on the side he has an avid interest in another data-intensive area: the stock market.

He started using Quantopian a few years ago, attracted by a platform that allowed him to deepen his interest in using computer-based forecasting methods on the stock market. More recently, he found something that suited his expertise even more: Numerai, a small San Francisco-based hedge fund founded by Richard Craib, a South African mathematician.

Numerai provides users with encrypted, raw financial data to develop models using approaches like machine learning - Mr Sharif's field - to help them make stock market predictions. They submit their predictions to Numerai , which awards prize money for the best models and incorporates them into automated trades for its hedge fund.

The prize money idea is similar to Quantopian's but Numerai differs in that it offers a "pure" data challenge, leaving how to solve it up to users.

In this respect it is similar to Kaggle, a data science website popular among computer scientists, where people post challenges and offer rewards for solutions.

The concept is esoteric and ambitious but in April Numerai raised $1.5m from investors led by Howard Morgan, one of the founders of Renaissance Technologies, a computer-powered hedge fund group. Numerai represents an intriguing return to his past and a pointer to a possible hedge fund model of the future, Mr Morgan says.

"Numerai could be disruptive because you can get a lot of people doing this part-time at home," he told the Financial Times. "We're very excited about the democratisation of problem-solving."

Institutional pressures

The hedge fund industry could be ripe for disruption, as investor dissatisfaction is rife. While the money trickling out is modest compared with the industry's $3tn size, even insiders admit that the old band of iconoclasts, including people such as George Soros, Michael Steinhardt and Julian Robertson, has been replaced by a far larger, institutional industry where the sheer number and size of hedge funds hit returns.

Tony James, president of Blackstone, the US asset management group, has predicted "a day of reckoning" that will contract the hedge fund industry by as much as a quarter. "There will be a shrinkage in the industry and it will be painful," he told a conference in May .

However, quantitative hedge funds such as Two Sigma, Renaissance, DE Shaw and Winton Capital, which eschew the gut feelings of human traders in favour of the logic of computer programs and mathematical models, have generally performed much better. As a whole, the quant hedge fund industry is on its seventh straight year of client inflows, with total assets going from $407.7bn in 2009 to $878.7bn this year, according to data from Hedge Fund Research, accounting for almost a third of the overall industry.

Talent has emerged as the leading challenge, with an intense battle among funds to recruit from rivals and Silicon Valley. But many people with the requisite skills have little interest in working for a big, established hedge fund in New York or London. Some merely want to dabble on the side of a full-time job.

This is where the new platforms - like Numerai, Quantopian, Quantiacs and QuantConnect - come in. They want to enable a new generation of iconoclasts who will help upturn the traditional hedge fund model.

Quantiacs is a good example of this vision. Like Quantopian and Numerai, it attracts computer scientists to its platform by awarding trading funds to strategies that perform best in its tests. The Silicon Valley company invests some of its own money in the competition winners, but Quantiacs is primarily focused on becoming a "marketplace" for automated investment strategies, and plans to open up the platform to individual and institutional investors to invest in algos directly.

While hedge funds traditionally charge "2 and 20" - a 2 per cent management fee and 20 per cent of profits - Quantiacs does not charge management fees and the 20 per cent of profits are split equally between the company and the quant. It hopes to funnel millions of dollars directly from investors to quants in their basements, dorms or research departments.

"There are a lot of people that don't want to work at a big hedge fund," Mr Froehler argues. He set up Quantiacs after working for a hedge fund in Switzerland and becoming disillusioned with the industry. "It is elitist, closed to outsiders and wastes a lot of talent. I would have loved to do this myself when I was studying mathematics."

These attempts at harnessing the talents of these DIY traders are nascent, and the new platforms may never attain the scale of the quant industry, let alone the broader hedge fund world. Most money is invested by pension funds, endowments and insurers that want a more traditional, institutional set-up.

Many hedge fund managers are also sceptical, arguing that DIY algos often underestimate the differences between financial theory and practice. An algorithm that might do well in a simulated test environment can swiftly unravel when exposed to the chaos of real markets. Even strategies that work with a modest amount of money can turn into duds when they are scaled up.

"We've seen very little evidence that lone geniuses sitting in their living room will be successful. There are some examples but very few," argues one quant hedge fund manager. Even superficially successful ones are vulnerable to sudden reversals. "These guys might not understand the risks that they're taking. They might be doing well but they might be exposed to a huge tail risk they're just not seeing."

Powerful tools

However, some of the industry's leading names think the idea has potential. This year Steven Cohen, the head of Point72 - a family office set up after SAC Capital, his former hedge fund, pleaded guilty to insider trading, and the Securities and Exchange Commission banned Mr Cohen from managing client money until 2018 - invested in Quantopian and promised up to $250m for its embryonic crowdsourced hedge fund.

"The tools can now be put into anyone's hands around the world to do very sophisticated quantitative investing. That is very different, and very powerful," says Matthew Granade, chief market intelligence officer at Point72 and a board member of Quantopian.

Numerai's Mr Craib admits that the concept is "hard to get your head around but I think this is the future". He is convinced that the crowdsourced alternative hedge fund models will supplant the dinosaurs that have lumbered around markets.

"Will the hedge fund of the future really be lots of people sitting in a big office? I think that in the post-artificial intelligence, post-internet world it will be far more democratised and diverse."

Recruitment

Mainstream asset managers step up hunt for tech talent

As banks, asset managers and hedge funds become dependent on technology, the demand for data scientists and programmers has become intense. Many hedge funds have been scouring Silicon Valley to attract some of the best minds in tech.

This year, Citadel, a Chicago hedge fund run by Ken Griffin, poached Microsoft's chief operating officer Kevin Turner to be the chief executive of its securities arm, while Bridgewater Associates, the world's biggest hedge fund manager, hired Apple's Jon Rubinstein to be co-chief executive.

Bridgewater had already hired IBM's David Ferrucci - the man behind the Watson supercomputer - to lead its new artificial intelligence unit, and Two Sigma, a quantitative hedge fund, last year hired Alfred Spector, formerly Google's vice-president of research and special initiatives, as its chief scientist.

"What is an absolute certainty is that the asset management sector is set to become even more of a technology industry than it already is," Rick Lacaille, chief investment officer at State Street Global Advisors, wrote this year . "Those firms that have made the necessary investments in data and technology will have the edge."

This is a prime driver behind the establishment of online platforms for amateur or part-time quantitative traders. While a top quant hedge fund might employ several hundred mathematicians, physicists and programmers, these start-ups hope they can gain an edge by harnessing the skills of potentially tens of thousands of tech-savvy people from the US, Europe and Asia.

Speed read

Quant hub Online platforms can attract talent by awarding trading funds to strategies that perform best in its tests

'Day of reckoning' Blackstone's Tony James predicts that the hedge fund industry could contract by up to 25%

Market judge Algos that do well in tests can unravel in real trading, or turn into duds when they are scaled up

Markets
Withdrawals Plague Once-Mighty Hedge-Fund Firms Brevan Howard and Tudor; Brevan Howard fund suffers first-half outflow of more than $3 billion; Tudor's head count falls by 15%

By Laurence Fletcher and Gregory Zuckerman 
1,074 words
17 August 2016
02:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A growing exodus from hedge funds extended to two of the biggest names in the industry Tuesday, Tudor Investment Corp. and Brevan Howard, as disenchanted investors increasingly shun what was once the hottest place to put money.

The funds' problem is clear: They just aren't performing.

Hedge funds and actively managed mutual funds have been underperforming since financial markets began their rebound in early 2009. The average hedge fund is up 3% this year through the end of July, according to researcher HFR Inc., less than half the S&P 500's rise, including dividends.

Funds in the $2.9 trillion hedge-fund sector have now experienced three consecutive quarters of withdrawals for the first time since 2009, according to HFR.

Brevan Howard's master fund was one of the star performers during the credit crisis, but is now on pace for its third straight calendar year of losses. Investors withdrew more than $3 billion from its flagship fund in the first half of this year, according to letters sent to investors and calculations by The Wall Street Journal based on the fund's asset levels and performance.

For the year through August, two key Tudor hedge funds were down about 3%, according to investors, well below the overall market. On Tuesday, the firm reduced its workforce by about 15%, or about 60 employees, including money managers as well as other staff, according to people familiar with the matter.

Faced with the declining performance, Paul Tudor Jones's firm had been contemplating for several months the shift to quantitative strategies, which rely on algorithms to make investing decisions, according to people close to the matter.

The move toward computer-driven investing and away from traditional investing is a sign of how even hedge-fund giants are feeling pain lately amid a growing shift to these so-called quant strategies.

The money-management business is increasingly becoming bifurcated with institutional investors choosing low-cost exchange-traded and index funds, then adding hedge funds that focus on quant trading, such as such as Renaissance Technologies and Two Sigma Investments LLC.

One Two Sigma fund was up almost 14% this year through the end of July, though another was down a bit.

Mr. Jones, who became a billionaire thanks to prescient "macro'" trades that anticipated moves across financial markets, has watched Tudor's assets shrink to approximately $11 billion from $13 billion last year and $14 billion in 2014, a person said. Now the firm is cutting underperforming traders who rely on a traditional, fundamental approach and focusing resources on its best moneymakers.

In a meeting last September with the firm's portfolio managers, he tried to motivate and prepare his team for the new era, according to the people close to the matter.

"No man is better than a machine," he told the team, "and no machine is better than a man with a machine."

The new quants won't replace departing traders. Instead, Tudor hopes to emphasize so-called big-data strategies those that use powerful computers and the internet to crunch mountains of raw data to uncover previously invisible insights in the market, an approach already emphasized at a number of hedge funds that are performing well lately, the people said.

Bloomberg News first reported the staff reduction.

The withdrawals at Brevan, which is headed by billionaire Alan Howard, mean the firm's assets have now more than halved in the past two years, from around $42 billion to $19.4 billion. It has shut commodities and emerging-markets funds in recent years, and spun out its credit-funds unit.

Brevan was long regarded as the most influential hedge fund in Europe with, until two years ago, the enviable record of making money every calendar year, even during the credit crisis and the Greek debt crisis.

However, the decision in 2009 to move top traders from London, including Mr. Howard, to Geneva was seen by some in the industry as a misstep, while the firm has also suffered the loss of several key traders, most notably co-founder Chris Rokos.

A spokesman for Brevan declined to comment.

Like most so-called global macro hedge funds, which trade bonds, currencies and stocks, Brevan has struggled in recent years to cope with markets dominated by central bank money-printing. This has flattened yield curves and suppressed volatility in bond markets, making it harder for such funds to profit.

Macro funds overall are up 3.7% this year on average, but lost money in four of the five past calendar years, according to Hedge Fund Research.

Staff numbers have dropped from around 460 in 2014 to around 350 currently, said a person familiar with the firm.

Like Brevan, Mr. Jones, 61 years old, has largely been defined as a macro oriented investor who makes bets in various global markets.

Mr. Jones, who helped launch high-profile charity The Robin Hood Foundation, began his career as a cotton trader. He first rose to fame on Wall Street after making an estimated $100 million on Black Monday, the day in October 1987 the Dow Jones Industrial Average dove 22%. He also made money when the market tumbled after the terrorist attacks in 2001, due to bearish positions.

A wildlife enthusiast with properties in Colorado, Africa and elsewhere, Mr. Jones in recent years generally has let his traders make their own investment decisions while spent most days at his firm's Greenwich, Conn., trading floor.

Five years ago, Tudor cut some fees charged by its largest hedge fund after years of grumbling by some clients, but raised other key fees and maintained overall charges that topped most hedge funds and other investment vehicles, a sign of the heated interest at the time for brand-named fund. At the time, Tudor's key BVI fund had averaged annual gains of 21% during its 25-year history.

But the fund has delivered uneven returns in recent years, including a gain of about 1% last year, investors say, and clients have withdrawn cash, leading to the decision to shift its trading.

La Defense Office District Rallies in Paris

By Art Patnaude and Inti Landauro 
898 words
17 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B6
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Cheap rents have companies flocking to La Defense, the Paris business district that became a symbol of France's weak economy in the years following the financial crisis.

In the first half of 2016, more office space was rented in the area than any other six-month period since 2000, according to property broker CBRE.

The uptick is a reverse of fortunes for La Defense, the largest business district in continental Europe, sitting about six kilometers west of the Eiffel Tower.

In the years following Europe's debt crisis, the district struggled as economic growth sputtered. Companies looking to cut costs moved out to the suburbs. Development projects were abandoned. Entire towers sat vacant.

With offices empty, rents fell. Average prime rents in La Defense fell to 466 euros a square meter a year in 2015 from 529 euros a square meter a year in 2010, CBRE data show. Meanwhile, prime office rents in central Paris remained far higher, averaging 709 euros a square meter a year last year.

Lower rents have become enticing to companies looking for space. Construction-materials firm Saint-Gobain SA, accountancy company Deloitte and health-care data company IMS Health Holdings Inc. are among the firms that signed up for office space in the area this year.

Saint-Gobain chose to gather all the headquarters of its different units in La Defense this year partly because of the rents, according to its Chief Executive Pierre-Andre de Chalendar. "We wouldn't have chosen it otherwise," he said in an emailed statement.

Another draw: La Defense offers the types of large office spaces where companies can install thousands of workers. In downtown Paris, where skyscrapers are forbidden, buildings are far smaller, requiring some big firms to rent multiple spaces. That makes rents in La Defense appear even cheaper.

In La Defense, development projects during the financial crisis stalled. But as firms move back, real-estate investors are building new towers, betting on continued demand.

Unibail Rodamco SE, the biggest listed property firm in Europe, is aiming to complete the 33-story Trinity Tower in the second half of 2018 and the much larger Sisters towers due after 2020.

The Abu Dhabi Investment Authority bought the 39-story Alto tower development that is due to be finished in late 2019.

"There is very little space being built in the next few years," said Karim Habra, head of France at LaSalle Investment Management, which advised ADIA on the Alto sale and is spearheading the development. "There is a window of opportunity."

A series of developments in other areas in the Paris periphery are likely to compete with La Defense. In the traditional areas close to either La Defense or downtown Paris -- like Boulogne-Billancourt, Neuilly-sur-Seine, Issy-les-Moulineaux or Levallois-Perret -- demand is rising at the same pace as in La Defense, said Philippe Depoux, the CEO of Gecina, another real-estate firm present in La Defense.

"These are the areas where we seek to focus on, beside intramuros Paris. Transactions are rising there, too," he said.

More daring developers have set their eyes on noncommercial areas such as Aubervilliers, Fontenay-sous-Bois or Ivry-sur-Seine, where land is cheap and the distance to Paris short.

French banks BNP Paribas and Societe Generale have recently installed some offices in Fontenay-sous-Bois and water utility Veolia Environnement is planning to move its headquarters to Aubervilliers.

More developments are coming. Chicago-based LaSalle in July bought an office development in Clichy, a northern suburb of Paris, and plans to complete another deal as soon as September, said Simon Marrison, CEO for Europe at the firm.

After Britain's vote to leave the European Union, office markets in Paris and other European capitals like Frankfurt and Dublin could benefit should companies, especially those in the financial-services sector, decide to relocate some operations away from London.

That isn't the reason LaSalle is pursuing development projects, but it would be the "icing on the cake," Mr. Marrison said.

Mr. Depoux at Gecina doesn't see developments outside La Defense as a threat. Public transportation to La Defense is far better, and many peripheral projects may not come to fruition because builders typically wait for tenants before starting any work, he said.

European real-estate investment dropped 30% to 107.5 billion euros in the first half of the year compared with the same period in 2015, according to real-estate consultant firm Real Capital Analytics. Political risks, including Brexit, and years of rising prices were among reasons global investors held back.

But Paris held up better than other major markets like London, Berlin and Madrid. Transaction volumes in the French capital were down 17% to 7.8 billion euros, the second-most active market behind London, RCA data show.

A number of headwinds are expected to weigh down the French economy in the coming months, such as the weakness in the U.K. after the vote to leave the EU and the spate of terror attacks in Europe.

For La Defense, swings should be expected, analysts said.

"Vacancy and occupancy rates there have always swung much more than other parts of Paris," said Francois Blin, head of international investment in France at broker JLL.

SingTel Revives Talks With Temasek

By P.R. Venkat 
383 words
17 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B7
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

SINGAPORE -- Singapore Telecommunications Ltd. has restarted discussions to buy a portion of Temasek Holdings Pte. Ltd.'s $2.4 billion stake in one of Thailand's largest telecommunications companies, according to people familiar with the situation.

SingTel has revived talks to purchase a stake in Intouch Holdings PCL, founded by former Thai Prime Minister Thaksin Shinawatra, from state-investment firm Temasek, after exploring a similar deal in 2013.

The stake purchase would further boost SingTel's presence in Thailand as Intouch owns a 40.6% stake in the country's largest mobile operator Advanced Info Services PCL. SingTel already holds a 23.3% stake in Advanced Info.

SingTel, Southeast Asia's largest telecom firm and the biggest company in Singapore by market capitalization, has chased growth around the globe to expand beyond its saturated local market. It snapped up U.S. managed-security services specialist firm Trustwave last year. In 2012, it bought Amobee, a digital advertising firm. SingTel owns substantial stakes in several Asian telecom firms, including India's Bharti Airtel Ltd. and Telkomsel in Indonesia.

Thailand is an attractive growth market for telecom companies. The country is experiencing increased mobile usage as incomes rise and the economy grows at a steady clip. Mobile phone and data subscribers are expected to increase to more than 88 million this year, which is up 4.4% from a year earlier.

SingTel is in discussions with Temasek over the exact size of the Intouch stake that it could buy, according to people familiar with the talks. Intouch, which trades on the Thai stock exchange, has a market cap of $5.9 billion and Temasek owns a 41% stake. The people cautioned that the discussions haven't been completed and could still fall apart or change.

Thai regulators cap foreign ownership of local telecom firms at 49%.

Intouch has been a lightning rod in local politics after being founded by Mr. Shinawatra. Temasek led a consortium that bought control of the firm, then known as Shin Corp., from Mr. Thaksin and his family in 2006.

A stake sale of Intouch would give Temasek more cash to invest in technology and newer industries, such as artificial intelligence and life sciences.

Investment: Rise of the DIY algo traders

Robin Wigglesworth 
2,064 words
16 August 2016
19:24
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

By day Dan Houghton helps run "http://chilango.co.uk/", that he co-founded. By night, he is an algorithmic stock trader, coding complex, automated investment strategies once his children are in bed.

In 2013 Mr Houghton sold an online texting company that he founded while studying mathematics at Cambridge university. The proceeds were ploughed into his restaurant and a new family home, but rather than deposit the remainder in a staid mutual fund he decided to use his maths skills to try to beat the market.

After reading books on finance and scouring the internet for articles on  quantitative  investing, he stumbled over a company tailor-made for people like him: Quantopian, an online trading platform and  crowdsourced  hedge fund. The company is designed for a new breed of do-it-yourself algorithmic traders who develop automated investment strategies. Each month, the best  algos  win trading money from "https://www.quantopian.com/" and a select few are included in its embryonic hedge fund.

 My wife thought I was crazy,  Mr Houghton says. He had to learn Python   a programming language popular among quantitative analysts, or  quants    from scratch, and after experimenting with the platform for a few months is trying to get one of his algorithms included in Quantopian s hedge fund.

So far he has made $7,971 after trading commissions, but making money is not the main driver.  It s primarily intellectual curiosity but I m fairly competitive and the chance of getting included [in Quantopian s fund] makes me stay up for the extra hour working on a strategy,  he says.

 Burrito-Dan , as he is known at Quantopian, is one of a swelling number of mathematicians, programmers, data engineers and physicists who are taking advantage of cheap, powerful computers and the availability of financial data to code their own trading strategies. These DIY algo traders are an updated version of the army of day-traders that emerged during the 1990s stock market boom.

Some analysts believe this new wave of amateur algo traders can be harnessed using crowdsourcing techniques to disrupt one of Wall Street s elite professions   the hedge fund manager.

 If our model is successful there will be no need for hedge funds any more,  says Martin Froehler, an Austrian mathematician who created "https://www.quantiacs.com/", one of several online platforms for DIY algo traders.  A smart guy with a laptop will be able to start his own hedge fund. It will be very challenging to the big incumbents. A very simple idea can prove very powerful. 

Most day traders are ultimately unsuccessful   many were wiped out when the dotcom bubble burst   and are often derided by professional money managers as  dumb money . The new DIY algo traders may be smarter and more computer-savvy than the average day trader but many experts warn they will find it just as hard to consistently beat the market. They scoff at the idea that their industry will face any threat from nerds coding in their basement.

But Quantiacs and its rivals are emerging as the classic "http://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Hedge_funds?ft_site=falcon&desktop=true" model is showing signs of strain. Investors have been pulling billions of dollars from hedge funds after a long period of underwhelming performance. And the fact that some technologists scent an opening underscores the sense of a looming industry shift, as more scientific, computer-driven approaches to investing gain favour.

Monetising pure data

Khaled Sharif, a young Jordanian computer engineer, arrived in the US in June to work at Nasa s Ames Research Center, the space agency s Silicon Valley outpost. Mr Sharif is a research assistant who specialises in deploying artificial intelligence techniques such as  machine learning  to crunch data on satellite images, weather patterns and earthquakes. But on the side he has an avid interest in another data-intensive area: the stock market.

He started using Quantopian a few years ago, attracted by a platform that allowed him to deepen his interest in using computer-based forecasting methods on the stock market. More recently, he found something that suited his expertise even more: Numerai, a small San Francisco-based hedge fund founded by Richard Craib, a South African mathematician.

Numerai provides users with encrypted, raw financial data to develop models using approaches like machine learning   Mr Sharif s field   to help them make stock market predictions. They submit their predictions to "https://numer.ai/", which awards prize money for the best models and incorporates them into automated trades for its hedge fund.

The prize money idea is similar to Quantopian s but Numerai differs in that it offers a  pure  data challenge, leaving how to solve it up to users.

In this respect it is similar to Kaggle, a data science website popular among computer scientists, where people post challenges and offer rewards for solutions.

The concept is esoteric and ambitious but in April Numerai raised $1.5m from investors led by Howard Morgan, one of the founders of Renaissance Technologies, a computer-powered hedge fund group. Numerai represents an intriguing return to his past and a pointer to a possible hedge fund model of the future, Mr Morgan says.

 Numerai could be disruptive because you can get a lot of people doing this part-time at home,  he told the Financial Times.  We re very excited about the democratisation of problem-solving. 

Institutional pressures

The hedge fund industry could be ripe for disruption, as investor dissatisfaction is rife. While the money trickling out is modest compared with the industry s $3tn size, even insiders admit that the old band of iconoclasts, including people such as George Soros, Michael Steinhardt and Julian Robertson, has been replaced by a far larger, institutional industry where the sheer number and size of hedge funds hit returns.

Tony James, president of Blackstone, the US asset management group, has predicted  a day of reckoning  that will contract the hedge fund industry by as much as a quarter.  There will be a shrinkage in the industry and it will be painful,  he told a "http://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2016-05-25/hedge-funds-may-lose-quarter-of-assets-blackstone-s-james-says".

However, quantitative hedge funds such as Two Sigma, Renaissance, DE Shaw and Winton Capital, which eschew the gut feelings of human traders in favour of the logic of computer programs and mathematical models, have generally performed much better. As a whole, the quant hedge fund industry is on its seventh straight year of client inflows, with total assets going from $407.7bn in 2009 to $878.7bn this year, according to data from Hedge Fund Research, accounting for almost a third of the overall industry.

Talent has emerged as the leading challenge, with an intense battle among funds to recruit from rivals and Silicon Valley. But many people with the requisite skills have little interest in working for a big, established hedge fund in New York or London. Some merely want to dabble on the side of a full-time job.

This is where the new platforms   like Numerai, Quantopian, Quantiacs and "https://www.quantconnect.com/"   come in. They want to enable a new generation of iconoclasts who will help upturn the traditional hedge fund model.

Quantiacs is a good example of this vision. Like Quantopian and Numerai, it attracts computer scientists to its platform by awarding trading funds to strategies that perform best in its tests. The Silicon Valley company invests some of its own money in the competition winners, but Quantiacs is primarily focused on becoming a  marketplace  for automated investment strategies, and plans to open up the platform to individual and institutional investors to invest in algos directly.

While hedge funds traditionally charge  2 and 20    a 2 per cent management fee and 20 per cent of profits   Quantiacs does not charge management fees and the 20 per cent of profits are split equally between the company and the quant. It hopes to funnel millions of dollars directly from investors to quants in their basements, dorms or research departments.

 There are a lot of people that don t want to work at a big hedge fund,  Mr Froehler argues. He set up Quantiacs after working for a hedge fund in Switzerland and becoming disillusioned with the industry.  It is elitist, closed to outsiders and wastes a lot of talent. I would have loved to do this myself when I was studying mathematics. 

These attempts at harnessing the talents of these DIY traders are nascent, and the new platforms may never attain the scale of the quant industry, let alone the broader hedge fund world. Most money is invested by pension funds, endowments and insurers that want a more traditional, institutional set-up.

Many hedge fund managers are also sceptical, arguing that DIY algos often underestimate the differences between financial theory and practice. An algorithm that might do well in a simulated test environment can swiftly unravel when exposed to the chaos of real markets. Even strategies that work with a modest amount of money can turn into duds when they are scaled up.

 We ve seen very little evidence that lone geniuses sitting in their living room will be successful. There are some examples but very few,  argues one quant hedge fund manager. Even superficially successful ones are vulnerable to sudden reversals.  These guys might not understand the risks that they re taking. They might be doing well but they might be exposed to a huge tail risk they re just not seeing. 

Powerful tools

However, some of the industry s leading names think the idea has potential. This year Steven Cohen, the head of Point72   a family office set up after SAC Capital, his former hedge fund, pleaded guilty to insider trading, and the Securities and Exchange Commission banned Mr Cohen from managing client money until 2018   invested in Quantopian and promised up to $250m for its embryonic crowdsourced hedge fund.

 The tools can now be put into anyone s hands around the world to do very sophisticated quantitative investing. That is very different, and very powerful,  says Matthew Granade, chief market intelligence officer at Point72 and a board member of Quantopian.

Numerai s Mr Craib admits that the concept is  hard to get your head around but I think this is the future . He is convinced that the crowdsourced alternative hedge fund models will supplant the dinosaurs that have lumbered around markets.

 Will the hedge fund of the future really be lots of people sitting in a big office? I think that in the post-artificial intelligence, post-internet world it will be far more democratised and diverse. 

Recruitment: asset managers search for tech talent

As banks, asset managers and hedge funds become dependent on technology, the demand for data scientists and programmers has become intense. Many hedge funds have been scouring Silicon Valley to attract some of the best minds in tech.

This year, Citadel, a Chicago hedge fund run by Ken Griffin, poached Microsoft s chief operating officer Kevin Turner to be the chief executive of its securities arm, while Bridgewater Associates, the world s biggest hedge fund manager, hired Apple s Jon Rubinstein to be co-chief executive.

Bridgewater had already hired IBM s David Ferrucci   the man behind the Jeapardy-beating Watson supercomputer   to lead its new artificial intelligence unit, and Two Sigma, a quantitative hedge fund, last year hired Alfred Spector, formerly Google s vice-president of research and special initiatives, as its chief scientist.

But the flurry of high-profile hires is just the tip of the iceberg, with a war for technology talent building across and at every level of the finance industry.

 What is an absolute certainty is that the asset management sector is set to become even more of a technology industry than it already is,  Rick Lacaille, chief investment officer at State Street Global Advisors, "http://www.institutionalinvestor.com/Article.aspx?ArticleID=3568697#/.V5lgkDkrI0Q".  Those firms that have made the necessary investments in data and technology will have the edge. 

This is a prime driver behind the establishment of online platforms for amateur or part-time quantitative traders. While a top quant hedge fund might employ several hundred mathematicians, physicists and programmers, these start-ups hope they can gain an edge by harnessing the skills of potentially tens of thousands of tech-savvy people from the US, Europe or Asia.

Business
Ford Developing Fully Driverless Car; Auto maker acquired Israeli firm SAIPS and invested $75 million in Velodyne

By Christina Rogers 
916 words
16 August 2016
19:09
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Ford Motor Co. plans to release a fully driverless car without a steering wheel or pedals in the next five years, the latest salvo in a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/detroit-battles-for-the-soul-of-self-driving-machines-1465782698" engulfing the global auto industry.

The Dearborn, Mich., auto maker on Tuesday said it would initially target ride-sharing fleets and package-delivery services with the unnamed model, underscoring the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ceo-mark-fields-maps-fords-future-1460502908" many car companies are taking before offering vehicles to consumers that don't require humans behind the wheel.

Ford expects the first of its driverless cars to be used by commercial-fleet operators looking to cut the costs of employing human drivers, company executives said. The vehicles largely will be confined to cities with pre-mapped zones designed for autonomous vehicles.

Separately, Ford said it had acquired an Israeli machine learning firm, SAIPS, which has 12 employees, and invested $75 million in Morgan Hill, Calif.-based laser sensor maker Velodyne Inc. Both are aimed at boosting Ford's autonomous-vehicle know-how, with the latter working on sensors to help autonomous cars successfully recognize objects and navigate traffic. Chinese web-service provider Baidu Inc. invested in Velodyne alongside Ford.

The nation's No.2 car maker also plans to double the staff at its Silicon Valley office to 260 by the end of 2017, hiring researchers and business-development staff in an effort to expand into new transportation services.

Ford's forays mark the latest attempt to keep up as traditional car companies and Silicon Valley upstarts race to deliver automated-driving technologies. But those endeavors have come under increased scrutiny after the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/tesla-draws-scrutiny-from-regulators-after-autopilot-feature-is-linked-to-a-death-1467319355". car driving itself. Questions also remain over regulations and legal liabilities arising from the advancements.

General Motors Co. punched the accelerator earlier this year, taking a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/gm-invests-500-million-in-lyft-plans-system-for-self-driving-cars-1451914204" in ride-hailing startup Lyft Inc., with which it plans to soon start "http://www.wsj.com/articles/gm-lyft-to-test-self-driving-electric-taxis-1462460094" of driverless Chevrolet Bolt taxis. The Detroit car maker also has said it expects next year to roll out its SuperCruise feature that allows for hands-free driving on the highway. It this year acquired the Silicon Valley autonomous-driving startup Cruise Automation Inc. to aid development efforts.

Other global giants including Toyota Motor Corp., Nissan Motor Co. and Volkswagen AG have committed to putting self-driving cars on the road. Nissan pledged it would roll out 10 new models within the next five years with a range of self-driving features aimed at individual buyers, including a fully autonomous car. Tesla, which released its driver-assist Autopilot system last year, says it will be the first to put a fully driverless car on the road, although it hasn't set a specific date.

Google parent Alphabet Inc. recently said it was pairing with Fiat Chrysler Automobiles NV to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/alphabet-fiat-chrysler-in-self-driving-cars-deal-1462306625" self-driving technology in minivans.

Ford's driverless car won't be made available for sales to individual customers until later in the decade, Chief Executive Officer Mark Fields said in an interview.

"We've done a lot of work reducing the cost on the technical components, but at the outset, it is still going to be a relatively expensive vehicle," he said.

Ford declined to say whether it plans to operate its own robo-taxi fleet or sell its forthcoming driverless car to independent ride-hailing services such as Uber Technologies Inc.

Like other major auto makers, Ford is allocating significant resources to developing self-driving cars, viewing the technology as a way to reach consumers who live in large, congested cities and don't own a vehicle. Earlier this year, the company established a separate division, Ford Smart Mobility LLC, to explore new business models that will cater to that growing market.

But Mr. Fields said Ford isn't interested in being the first auto maker to put a fully autonomous car on the market, noting "we're not in a race to make announcements."

Many car companies, including Ford, are installing semiautonomous features such as automatic emergency brakes and adaptive cruise control in vehicles. But Ford remains keenly focused on fully driverless cars, with product chief Raj Nair expressing concerns over whether drivers can take over quickly enough when driverless systems are disabled.

Other hurdles also remain. Regulators are still trying to sort out what rules and guidelines need to be in place before driverless cars start hitting roadways in large numbers.

"The regulators are being very forward leaning on this," Mr. Fields said. "Our desire is to get a 50-state national framework" on operational standards for driverless cars, he added. The National Highway Traffic Safety Administration plans this summer to release guidelines for local rules on driverless cars.

Ford and Baidu's investment in Velodyne also is aimed at helping the tech firm lower the costs of its sensors to between $300 and $500 a unit, inexpensive enough for mass adoption. The first Velodyne laser device cost $75,000 and was large and obtrusive, sitting on the roof of the car.

Data Manager Rubrik Collects $61M as Valuation Climbs

By Deborah Gage 
355 words
16 August 2016
20:59
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Rubrik Inc., which helps corporate customers manage and protect data whether it is in the cloud or on their premises, has raised $61 million, nearly doubling its total funding.

The funding was an up round. Rubrik's share price nearly tripled, to $6.85, after the company did a 4-to-1 stock split that valued its Series B shares at $2.45.

Chief Executive Bupal Sinha declined to comment on the funding. He said Rubrik is growing, with about 160 employees and an average deal size of about $250,000. He also said Rubrik could have raised more money than it did and turned away as much as $40 million.

"We want to be the data management platform for modern businesses &and feel there's an opportunity to build a large business and be an independent public company," Mr. Sinha said.

Rubrik consolidates data backup and recovery software with de-duplicated storage, functions that have historically been separated into different products.

The company was cited by analysis firm Gartner in April as a "cool vendor in storage technologies." Gartner noted that other startups offer similar backup architectures and that more established vendors were also adding features to their products.

Valuations for some private companies have fallen this year, and other companies that sell technology to businesses have "http://www.wsj.com/articles/martins-take-an-investor-market-leads-to-more-down-rounds-1471001400". Big-data software company MapR Technologies Inc. last week raised $50 million in a round that cut its share price by more than 26%. In March, database company Couchbase Inc. saw its share price cut by 41%, while security startup Bromium Inc. saw its price fall by 58%.

Rubrik's funding was led by Khosla Ventures, with current investors Lightspeed Venture Partners and Greylock Partners participating. The company has now raised more than $112 million and said it would invest the money in sales, marketing, support and engineering.

Correction: Rubrik raised an up round. A previous version of this article incorrectly stated that the company had raised a down round.

Strong Yen and Weak Oil Hit Asia Stocks; Shares mixed, with record gains in the U.S. offset by a dip in oil prices and a much stronger yen

By Ese Erheriene 
726 words
16 August 2016
08:31
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Shares in Asia were mixed on Tuesday, with record levels seen in the U.S. market overnight offset by a dip in oil prices and a much stronger yen.

The yen moves drove the Nikkei Stock Average down 1.6% for the day. Taiwan's Taiex ended down 0.4%, while Australia's S&P/ASX 200 slipped 0.1% as a downswing in oil hit commodity producers. The Hang Seng Index was last up 0.1% and Korea's Kospi closed 0.1% lower.

A paper released by Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco President John Williams said U.S. central bankers should either raise the U.S. Fed's inflation target from the current 2%, or move to a new target based on price levels or economic growth. The suggestion could give the Fed more room to keep rates at current levels, and the greenback softened against a range of currencies Tuesday, including the yen.

The firm Japanese currency, last up 1% and pushing up against the 100 level versus the dollar, hit the share prices of local auto makers. Toyota Motor Corp. closed down 1.3%, Nissan Motor Co. Ltd. was off 1.7%, Honda Motor Co. Ltd. was 0.7% lower and Mazda Motor Corp. lost 3.4% of its value.

The market had earlier attempted to shrug off Japan's disappointing gross-domestic-product data for the April-June quarter released on Monday, which weighed in at 0.2%, below an expected 0.7% expansion.

"On the one hand, the economy is not doing well, which doesn't look good for domestic profits of Japanese firms," said Marcel Thieliant, senior Japan economist at Capital Economics. However, the weak figures increase the chance of more easing by the Bank of Japan, he said.

Late on Monday, the S&P 500, Dow Jones Average and the Nasdaq Composite all reached record highs on the back of hopes for production cuts in the oversupplied oil market.

Brent crude oil eased back from a one-month high in Asian trade, though, and was last down to $47.94 a barrel.

"I don't think that [the U.S. highs are] too significant," said William Ma, chief investment office at Noah Holdings Ltd. The muted impact on Asian stocks was due to the small gains involved, he said.

The Shanghai market erased earlier gains as investors took profits in financial blue-chips. The benchmark Shanghai Composite Index closed down 0.5% to 3110.6, after trading up as high as 0.5% after the opening. Banking, car manufacturing and insurance stocks retreated the most as investors took profits in blue-chips.

Hong Kong stocks clawed back some of their early gains after losses in the afternoon session, with persistent talk that the Shenzhen-Hong Kong Connect trading link would be launched before the end of the year having a bullish impact. But banking stocks remained in the red, with HSBC PLC down 0.6% and Standard Chartered PLC 0.7% lower.

"The market was reaching...overbought conditions because of yesterday's moves, especially the Hong Kong market," said Hao Hong, chief strategist and co-head of research at Bank of Communications Co. Ltd. "Some people want to lock in gains."

Elsewhere, China Evergrande Group's Hong Kong shares rose 1% after it increased its stake in China Vanke Co. Ltd to 6.8% from 5%. According to Lucror Analytics, Evergrande's accumulation of Vanke shares was puzzling, given that the developer is heavily indebted, having aggressively used debt capital to fund recent expansions and acquisitions. Vanke was last trading up 2.7%.

The FTSE Bursa Malaysia Index climbed Tuesday lifted by expectations of more monetary easing around the globe. The Malaysia 30-stocks benchmark index increased 0.5% to 1,699.1 points in early trade, and was up 0.5% year-to-date.

In other commodities, London Metal Exchange copper futures were up 0.3% and nickel fell 0.9%, while gold rose 0.8% to $1,348 a troy ounce.

Dominique Fong, Yantoultra Ngui and Saumya Vaishampayan contributed to this article.

Business Briefs: Business Watch

406 words
16 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

XYLEM

Sensus to Be Bought

In $1.7 Billion Deal

Water-technology company Xylem Inc. said on Monday that it would buy smart-meter company Sensus for about $1.7 billion.

Sensus makes smart meters, network technologies and provides data analytics services for the water, electric and gas industries. It generated $837 million in adjusted revenue for its fiscal year ended March 31, Xylem said, and is owned by investment funds affiliated with Jordan Co. and GS Capital Partners 2000.

Sensus has about 3,300 employees in the U.S., U.K., Germany, Slovakia, and China and generates nearly 70% of revenue in the U.S. Last year, Xylem had revenue of $3.7 billion and about 12,700 employees.

The all-cash deal will be financed by about $400 million of Xylem's non-U.S. cash, new and existing credit facilities and a combination of short- and long-term debt. There is no change to Xylem's 2016 adjusted earnings outlook, but the deal is expected to add to 2017 earnings.

-- Austen Hufford

---

SYSCO

Food Distributor

Posts Higher Results

Sysco Corp. said profit, revenue and volumes all rose in its latest quarter.

Results easily beat expectations.

In all for the quarter, Sysco, one of the largest food and supplies distributors, earned $215.7 million, or 38 cents a share, up from $73 million, or 12 cents a share, a year prior.

Excluding restructuring costs and other items, adjusted earnings rose to 64 cents a share from 52 cents. The number of shares outstanding fell 5.2% in the quarter, boosting per-share earnings. Revenue rose 10% to $13.7 billion.

Analysts had projected 61 cents in adjusted earnings per share on $13.68 billion in revenue, according to Thomson Reuters.

-- Austen Hufford

---

HENNES & MAURITZ

Retailer's Sales

Increase by 10%

Sweden's Hennes & Mauritz AB said Monday July sales including value-added-tax increased 10% in local currencies compared with the same period a year earlier.

This follows the 8% sales rise the clothing retailer posted in June, and the 16% rise it posted in July last year.

The company had 4,105 stores at the end of July, compared with 3,649 on July 31 last year. It has previously said it plans to add around 425 stores this year, mostly in China and the U.S.

-- Dominic Chopping

COMPANIES
StanChart in biometric technology rollout to 5m; Banks

By Emma Dunkley 
507 words
15 August 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
16
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Voice recognition and fingerprints are replacing pin numbers and passwords at Standard Chartered as the UK-listed bank becomes the latest lender to introduce biometric technology.

StanChart, which operates mostly across Asia, the Middle East and Africa, will roll out the new service to some 5m mobile and telephone banking customers this year, allowing them to check bank balances and access investments without needing to remember multiple passwords.

The move comes as many of the world's biggest banks expand digital investments in order to meet the rising demand for mobile banking, as the number of customers visiting branches declines.

Earlier this month, Barclays announced that all personal phone banking customers in the UK would be able to use their voice as a form of secure ID instead of answering security questions or remembering passwords. HSBC is planning to launch voice recognition and fingerprint ID in the summer while Royal Bank of Scotland is launching artificial intelligence to interact with staff, to help them assist their small business customers.

In Singapore, local banks have gone one step further and are collaborating on a service that will allow customers to make payments using their social media ID and mobile phone numbers instead of account numbers and sort codes.

StanChart's moves are part of a plan unveiled by chief executive Bill Winters last November to invest $1.5bn in technology over three years and bolster the bank's retail banking division.

Last month, StanChart rolled out video banking for customers who wanted to speak to consultants about finding a mortgage, for example, without visiting a branch. The bank also plans to pilot "robo-advisers" before the end of the year to provide basic investment services.

Karen Fawcett , Standard Chartered's chief executive of retail banking, said the shift to fingerprint and voice biometric technology aimed to provide more efficient and secure services for customers, who are increasingly using smartphones to access their accounts.

With fingerprint recognition technology, customers will be able to use "touch login" on smartphones or iPads.

The bank will also introduce voice recognition for customers using phone banking in India and the UAE this year before rolling the service out across more markets in 2017.

StanChart has set a target of generating 30 per cent of sales and 40 per cent of payments online by 2018 "to improve the customer experience and make the bank more efficient." Private banking and wealth management is one target area for growth.

StanChart aims to increase its $60bn of assets under management to more than $100bn by 2020.

The bank has closed about 200 branches over the past 18 months, but Ms Fawcett denied that it planned to axe physical services in favour of online and said: "We don't think branches will disappear, they will change."

StanChart is developing new cost-efficient branch formats. In Korea, it has struck a deal with retail group Shinsegae to offer banking services in department stores, supermarkets and other outlets.

XYLEM

Sensus to Be Bought In $1.7 Billion Deal

Water-technology company Xylem Inc. said on Monday that it would buy smart-meter company Sensus for about $1.7 billion.

Sensus makes smart meters, network technologies and provides data analytics services for the water, electric and gas industries. It generated $837 million in adjusted revenue for its fiscal year ended March 31, Xylem said, and is owned by investment funds affiliated with Jordan Co. and GS Capital Partners 2000.

Sensus has about 3,300 employees in the U.S., U.K., Germany, Slovakia, and China and generates nearly 70% of revenue in the U.S. Last year, Xylem had revenue of $3.7 billion and about 12,700 employees.

The all-cash deal will be financed by about $400 million of Xylem's non-U.S. cash, new and existing credit facilities and a combination of short- and long-term debt. There is no change to Xylem's 2016 adjusted earnings outlook, but the deal is expected to add to 2017 earnings.

The transaction is expected to close in the fourth quarter and is subject to customary closing conditions and regulatory review, including by the Federal Communications Commission related to certain spectrum licenses.

Austen Hufford

SYSCO

Food Distributor Posts Higher Results

Sysco Corp. said profit, revenue and volumes all rose in its latest quarter.

Results easily beat expectations, and shares rose 4.4% to $54.50 in premarket trading.

In its fourth quarter, Sysco's domestic case volume grew 10.2% as sales volume rose 10.3% among smaller, local restaurants. It has faced competition recently from smaller, specialty distributors and wholesale stores.

Sysco is one of the largest food and supplies distributors and serves restaurants, hospitals and schools in the U.S.

In all for the quarter, Sysco, one of the largest food and supplies distributors, earned $215.7 million, or 38 cents a share, up from $73 million, or 12 cents a share, a year prior.

Excluding restructuring costs and other items, adjusted earnings rose to 64 cents a share from 52 cents. The number of shares outstanding fell 5.2% in the quarter, boosting per-share earnings. Revenue rose 10% to $13.7 billion.

Analysts had projected 61 cents in adjusted earnings per share on $13.68 billion in revenue, according to Thomson Reuters.

Gross margin improved to 18.34% from 17.90%.

Food deflation was 1.2% with deflation in meat and dairy being partially offset by inflation in other categories.

Austen Hufford

NEWCREST MINING

Dividend Returns After Three Years

SYDNEY--A big reduction in Newcrest Mining Ltd.'s debt pile has prompted the Australian gold miner to bring back a dividend for the first time in three years.

Miners including Newcrest have cut or scrapped payouts entirely in recent years as global commodities markets slumped, sending them racing to protect their balance sheets. Gold producers were some of the first to act, after an abrupt end of a 12-year bull run in gold prices earlier this decade on expectations of rising interest rates.

Newcrest, one of the world's top gold producers, said on Monday it will pay a final dividend of 7.5 U.S. cents a share for its fiscal year through June. The miner suspended dividends in 2013 as it posted its largest-ever annual loss.

Newcrest, whose mining operations span Papua New Guinea to Ivory Coast, has faced a slew of challenges in recent years, including weak commodity markets, safety incidents and large writedowns. As the downturn in gold prices deepened, Newcrest, like many miners, prioritized paying down its debts, aided by deep cuts to production costs.

The company said reduced net debt by 27% last fiscal year to US$2.1 billion.

"This strengthening financial position and outlook has provided the basis for the directors determining to pay a final dividend, whilst also preserving the capacity to fund future growth," Chairman Peter Hay said in a letter to shareholders.

On a call with reporters, Chief Executive Sandeep Biswas said he is keen for Newcrest to now hand investors consistent returns. Shares in the company have more than doubled over the past year.

"A sustainable dividend is very important for us, the quantum determined by the circumstances at the time," he said.

He pointed out Newcrest has repaid almost all its bilateral bank debt, reducing those borrowings to just US$25 million from US$975 million a year ago. "Once that disappears we will probably end up accumulating cash" and can then look at options for further capital management, he said.

Newcrest isn't the only gold miner looking to fatten payouts to investors.

Newmont Mining Corp. Chief Executive Gary Goldberg said in an interview last week the U.S. gold producer will likely mull a higher dividend after cutting its own debt levels quicker than expected.

Newcrest on Monday reported a net profit of US$332 million for the year through June, down from US$376 million in the same period a year ago. The miner said profits were weighed by lower gold and copper prices and the temporary suspension of operations at its Gosowong site in Indonesia earlier this year.

Newcrest produced slightly more gold last fiscal year, at 2.4 million troy ounces, as record output at the Lihir mine in Papua New Guinea offset the multi-month shutdown of Gosowong. Production was suspended there after a "geotechnical event" in February trapped a worker roughly 300 meters underground for eight days.

Newcrest said its gold output could rise in the year ahead, forecasting production in the range of 2.40-2.65 million ounces.

Mr. Biswas said the company is seeking to expand in the coming years, both by exploring land it already controls and by teaming up with small explorers in the industry.

Gold prices have been stronger than expected this year, fueled by expectations that the Federal Reserve will hold off from tightening monetary policy while other central banks continue to ease. Newcrest said it is expecting a gold price of roughly US$1,200-US$1,250 an ounce in the coming years. On Monday, spot gold traded at roughly US$1,338 an ounce.

On the call, Mr. Biswas said he was optimistic about the long run prospects for the price of copper, which Newcrest also produces, although said that, in the short term, "it is a bit of a lottery." Copper on the London Metal Exchange trades at US$4,775 a metric ton, up marginally on the start of the year.

Rhiannon Hoyle

ROSNEFT

Weak Oil Prices Weigh on Revenue

MOSCOW--Russian state-controlled oil giant PAO Rosneft said Monday that its net profit for the first half of the year nearly halved compared with the same period of 2015 as weak oil prices dragged down revenue despite a slight recovery in prices the second quarter.

Rosneft said net profit for the first half of the year was down 45.8% at 103 billion rubles ($1.6 billion), as revenue fell 14.2% to 2.28 trillion rubles. Net profit increased more than sixfold in the second quarter to 89 billion rubles compared with the first three months of the year on revenue that was up 17.6% to 1.23 trillion rubles.

"With remaining volatility, the commodity markets showed some improvements in 2Q 2016, and average ruble crude oil price went up by 20% against 1Q 2016," Rosneft Chief Executive Igor Sechin said in a statement. He said the priorities are control over operating and selling, general and administrative costs, as well as raising the quality of the investment program.

Rosneft's earnings before interest, taxation, depreciation and amortization, or Ebitda, fell 7.3% in the first half of the year to 621 billion rubles. Ebitda in the second quarter was 27.5% higher than the previous quarter at 348 billion rubles.

James Marson

MARKETS - BACK SECTION
Voice brokers answer call for liquidity; Analysis: Trading room

By Philip Stafford 
669 words
12 August 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Deal negotiators in demand again after retreat of banks favours electronic platforms

So much for the long-predicted demise of voice brokers at the hands of ever- more sophisticated technology.

In recent months BGC Partners and Tullett Prebon, the interdealer brokers, have been scooping up hundreds of brokers who negotiate deals between buyers and sellers over the phone for illiquid assets in fixed income, energy and currency markets. Even Citadel, the US electronic market maker, has recruited voice brokers as it pushes into the European swaps market.

The voice business, as it is known, is one of the City of London's strengths. Though global in nature, both Tullett and BGC have some of the largest interdealer floors in the UK capital and expect to base operations there even after Britain leaves the EU.

A revival of voice trading seems odd when some hedge funds are experimenting with artificial intelligence . But the resurgence of human activity is part of a land-grab for business across financial markets that are being transformed by post-crisis regulations.

Transacting in fixed income, currencies, commodities and derivatives has reached an inflection point. Incumbent trading platforms, voice brokers and automated traders are looking to take advantage of opportunities as tougher capital standards weaken the role of global banks.

In the new ecosystem brokers will increasingly compete against Tradeweb (backed by Thomson Reuters), Bloom-berg and MarketAxess as these make inroads into electronic trading.

Shaun Lynn , president of BGC, says it is not a case of one or the other. "The market is going electronic but it's going with the brokers. The exchange itself is electronic, but the broker executes for you on a range of other related products, from asset swaps to basis trades, that come off it," he says.

BGC has spent more than $2bn on technology since 1998. Tullett plans to upgrade its systems after a deal to take hybrid technology from CME, the US futures exchange.

Their confidence arises from market idiosyncrasies. Despite years of technological innovation, big operational issues endure. Institutional investors have long been wary of flagging their intentions for fear of having the price move against them. This is why many big investors have long chosen to trade directly with a bank, which completes the transaction with other banks in the interdealer market via a voice broker.

Some investors are altering course as the banks retreat, and want to deal with only their 10 largest customers, leaving dozens of smaller ones looking for ways to buy and sell bonds, derivatives, commodities and currencies.

Jim McCaughan , chief executive of Principal Global Investors, an asset manager with more than $400bn of assets, says the days of simply handing over his market orders to a bank are long gone. Investors have a greatly improved understanding of how information from their orders can be exploited, he says.

Dozens of potential traders can be sourced via a broker's contacts book or an electronic venue that takes trades from asset managers, hedge funds and high-frequency traders.

John Phizackerley , chief executive of Tullett, says there are "big opportunities" with these "all-to-all" markets.

But the voice market faces a challenge. While Bloomberg, Tradeweb and MarketAxess employ teams of people to bring parties together, their incentives are not the same as those of interdealer brokers. Their bosses see them more akin to "trade facilitators" even though the job appears similar.

"Voice is fundamentally different," says an executive at a European trading platform. "In order to make money, brokers have to derive it from the spread."

Interdealer brokers are confident they can thrive. Anthony Perrotta, a former broker and head of research at Tabb, says banks are turning to riskless principal, or agency, trading, for profit. And liquidity requirements, both to meet Basel capital rules and for customers' trades, create a need for human intervention.

"Even listed futures markets, like energy, need a voice element," says Mr Lynn.

Mr. Krane's influence in the new role could lead to an increased focus on consumer-related technology investment, the person said.

GV's overall focus on five sectors -- life sciences, enterprise, data and artificial intelligence, robotics, and consumer -- will remain unchanged under the new chief.

Economy
Economists in Survey Say Election Will Factor in September Fed Decision; More than half say the election will weigh on Fed officials at their next meeting Sept. 20-21

By David Harrison 
625 words
11 August 2016
15:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

More than half the economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal in August said November's presidential election is one of several factors that could make the Federal Reserve less likely to raise short-term interest rates in September.

Fed officials have long maintained they don't consider political developments such as elections as they debate monetary policy. Policy makers repeatedly have said their decisions are "data-dependent," meaning they will strive to do what is best for the economy based on the most recent figures.

But 36 of the 60 survey respondents said the election would weigh on Fed officials at their next meeting Sept. 20-21. The fear of startling financial markets and setting off an economic shock that could affect the outcome of an election causes central bankers to hold off in the immediate run-up to the vote, some observers said.

Although economic data would be "far more important" in determining whether the Fed takes action next month, the election "cannot be ignored entirely," said Sean Snaith, an economist at the University of Central Florida.

The two major party candidates have proposed "http://graphics.wsj.com/elections/2016/donald-trump-hillary-clinton-on-the-economy/". While both propose more government spending, Democratic candidate Hillary Clinton has called for raising taxes on high earners and overhauling the corporate tax system to generate more revenue, while her Republican opponent, Donald Trump, wants to cut taxes across the board.

A Moody's Analytics report found that "http://blogs.wsj.com/economics/2016/07/29/hillary-clintons-policies-would-boost-growth-and-budget-deficits-new-analysis-says/" would boost employment by 3.2 million jobs while spurring economic growth and adding slightly to the national deficit. "http://blogs.wsj.com/economics/2016/06/20/u-s-economy-would-be-diminished-under-trumps-economic-plan-new-analysis-says/" would cause a prolonged recession and lead to big deficits, the firm said in a separate report.

"From a monetary policy perspective, this is the most problematic presidential election in modern history," said Bernard Baumohl, chief global economist at the Economic Outlook Group. "When faced with such a glaring unknown, the Fed should lie low until December."

Earlier this month, New York Fed President William Dudley took the unusual step of recognizing that the uncertainty around the election could have an effect on the economy.

"Although I expect business fixed investment to begin to grow again in the second half, it will likely remain soft as profits have stagnated and election year uncertainty could act as an additional depressant," he said.

Other Fed officials, though, have stuck by their contention that the election plays no role in their assessment of the economy.

"We are very focused on assessing the economic outlook and making changes that are appropriate without taking politics into account," Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen said in a June 15 press conference.

Fed Vice Chairman Stanley Fischer echoed that sentiment two weeks later.

"We will do what we have to do in accordance with the law," he said. "We are not going to get into,  Oh, it's the elections, we can't do anything.'"

History shows the central bank will adjust policy even in presidential election years. The Fed raised rates "http://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-john-williams-says-election-wont-stop-central-bank-from-acting-on-rates-1463933622" of 1984, 1988, 2000 and 2004 and cut interest rates in 1992 and 2008. In 2012, the central bank launched the third round of its controversial bond-buying program known as quantitative easing.

In the past 30 years, 1996 is the only election year during which the Fed made no major policy moves.
