Having struck gold in Chinese ecommerce, foreign investors such as Japan s SoftBank and New York-based Tiger Global Management turned their sights on India and pumped in billions of dollars, betting on its huge population, rapid growth in internet penetration and strong economic growth.

By last year India had amassed eight  unicorns , or unlisted companies valued at more than $1bn   behind only the US and China. The funding wave was a conspicuous vote of confidence in "http://www.ft.com/global-economy/india" now the fastest-growing of any major nation, and in its ability to use technology to skip over 20th-century concepts such as hypermarkets, while driving higher living standards.

 India never really had modern retail, so the leapfrog that will happen is quite compelling,  says Sanjeev Aggarwal, co-founder of Helion Venture Partners, an Indian venture capital group.  This is day one of India s consumption going digital. 

But both investors and companies now talk of a conspicuous cooling of sentiment, amid fears that the funding rush has led to widespread overvaluation. There has been concern about the scale of losses at some of the companies, stiff competition from foreign rivals and the knock-on effect on other markets of tech investment writedowns. Some businesses are shedding staff and withdrawing job offers as they adjust to a world in which exuberant foreign investors can no longer be relied on to underwrite losses indefinitely.

 If you d asked me   when we got the round   do you deserve the $1bn, I d have said absolutely not,  says Mr Goyal.  But for anything, the value is about how much you want it. And then the market changed and suddenly everyone is sceptical about everything. 

Chinese parallels

When the influential venture capitalist Mary Meeker presented her "http://www.kpcb.com/internet-trends" on web trends in June, she provided two estimates for internet usage growth in 2015: one stripped out India, which was growing so fast that it distorted the sluggish worldwide figure.

The number of web users reached about 317m last October, 49 per cent higher than a year earlier, according to Ms Meeker, citing data from the Internet and Mobile Association of India. This growth was driven by smartphones, of which sales in India increased at a rate of 12 per cent in the first quarter of this year, while they stagnated or even fell globally, according to research groups.

The smartphone boom has attracted the attention of tech leaders such as Tim Cook, the Apple chief executive, who made his first visit to India in May, and it is inspiring excitement among analysts about the outlook for ecommerce growth in the country. Most bullish is Morgan Stanley, which predicted in February that total sales in the sector will grow to $119bn by 2020, up from about $16bn last year.

Such optimism has been fuelled partly by the dramatic rise of Chinese ecommerce groups such as Tencent, JD.com and especially Alibaba, which undertook the biggest ever initial public offering last year.

But investors and entrepreneurs in India warn that any parallel should be treated with extreme caution.  I was very surprised that all these funds were so naive in treating India just like China,  says Kashyap Deorah, a serial technology entrepreneur and author of a book on the  hyperfunding  of Indian internet groups.

India s lower level of development   its per capita gross domestic product was $1,617 last year, against China s $7,990, according to the International Monetary Fund   is one obvious difference. Just as important, Mr Deorah says, is the fact that US internet groups have struggled to make themselves felt in China.

Google, Facebook and Twitter are all unavailable to most Chinese internet users but have won dominant positions in India, limiting the space for local players. Even where Indian start-ups have stolen a march on US rivals, some have seen that lead rapidly eroded.

American rivals

In September 2014, Amazon founder Jeff Bezos clambered on top of a gaudily painted truck in Bangalore wearing a white Indian wedding suit, and handed a giant cheque for $2bn   symbolising planned investment in the country   to Amit Agarwal, whom he had sent to run its Indian operation.

The hilarity of the pantomime-style performance may have been lost on Flipkart co-founders Sachin and Binny Bansal, former employees of Mr Bezos who now found themselves in his competitive crosshairs.

The Bansals   unrelated despite having a surname in common   met while working for Amazon at its Bangalore campus, which then provided back-office support. In 2007, with Amazon showing no sign of launching sales in India, the two men decided to put the company s playbook into practice.

Just like Amazon, Flipkart honed its service by selling books before branching into other products, and after a slow start began attracting investor attention. In 2009 it raised funding that valued it at $4m; in 2010 at $50m; in 2012 at $1bn, and in July last year, at $15bn, making it by far India s highest-valued internet company.  In terms of market share, funding and the brand we have been able to build up in a very short amount of time,  Sachin Bansal told the Financial Times this year.

But losses have also grown rapidly. Flipkart s most recent regulatory filings in Singapore, where it is domiciled, show revenue of Rs102bn ($1.5bn) in the year ending in March 2015, with a pre-tax loss of Rs29.8bn   up from the previous year s figures of Rs29.4bn and Rs10.3bn respectively.

And although the heady investor enthusiasm for Flipkart continued long after Amazon s entry to the market, it is now showing clear signs of fading amid rapid growth at Amazon India, which received an additional $3bn funding pledge from Mr Bezos in June.

A fund run by Morgan Stanley has twice this year marked down its stake in Flipkart, giving it a sharply lower valuation of $9.4bn, and other shareholders have made similar moves.

Funding trends suggest a broad turn in sentiment. In the nine months to the end of June, Indian technology companies raised $3.1bn, down from $6.7bn in the preceding nine months, according to Tracxn, a research company.

Chief executives and investors say the slowdown was triggered in part by sharp falls in US and Chinese technology stocks last summer, and most argue that well-run Indian tech companies will still be able to raise capital, with funding far from drying up. ShopClues, an ecommerce platform, in January became India s ninth unicorn through a financing round led by GIC, Singapore s sovereign wealth fund.

But even Sandeep Aggarwal, a co-founder of ShopClues, perceives a chillier funding environment.  The fear of missing out was so great that [investors] went for spray and pray,  he says.  And when the market started slowing, reality hit them in the face. 

That blow was especially violent for Nikesh Arora, the former Google executive who became one of the most prominent investors in the Indian tech sector after being recruited by SoftBank in 2014. Within weeks of his appointment as president, Mr Arora led investments of more than $800m in Flipkart rival Snapdeal and in Ola, a competitor to Uber in ride-hailing, and made further large capital injections into other Indian tech companies.

But shareholder unease grew as Snapdeal fell far behind Flipkart and Amazon in sales. Ola slashed its prices to fend off a fierce assault from Uber, which is set to redouble its focus on India having sold its Chinese business to rival Didi Chuxing last week.

Mr Arora resigned from SoftBank in June shortly after being cleared by an internal investigation prompted by anonymous investor allegations of improper conduct, largely in relation to his Indian investments. Mr Arora and SoftBank deny that his departure was linked to these complaints, but his brief career at the Japanese group is viewed by some investors in India as a cautionary tale that sums up the rollercoaster ride of the past two years.

The quieter funding market of recent months has come as a relief to some entrepreneurs.

 We re very happy that phase has ended. In the long run that s the right thing for the company and for the country,  says Rohit Bansal, co-founder of Snapdeal.  No company can exist just by raising money from investors. 

Global battleground

Despite their newfound global status, China s leading ecommerce companies have been slow to make incursions abroad. In India, their expansion has been limited to portfolio investments: Didi Chuxing took a stake in its counterpart Ola while Alibaba invested in Snapdeal and in PayTM, a payments and ecommerce business.

However, Alibaba announced in March that it planned to start operating in India as soon as this year and the country looks set to become the first major market to host a full-on fight for supremacy between US, Chinese and local ecommerce companies, says Nandan Nilekani, co-founder of Infosys, the IT services group. Now a venture capital investor, Mr Nilekani argues that companies that have niches least exposed to well-resourced foreign competitors are best placed to succeed.

Among these are groups tailoring their offerings to the many millions of Indians taking their first steps online, typically through newly acquired, low-cost smartphones. Such companies include DailyHunt, a news aggregator that provides content in local languages rather than English.

Asked to demonstrate the product, Virendra Gupta, DailyHunt s chief executive, reaches for his unfashionable mid-range Chinese smartphone, waiting patiently while its basic 2G data connection grapples with downloads. For Mr Gupta such inconvenience is part of an effort to understand his target audience. With his focus on the  next 400m  Indian web users, he has little patience for questions such as how many Indian companies deserve a $1bn valuation.

 People get into a mad rush when there is [a successful model] somewhere else and they just put a multiplier around it,  he says.  It s important for people who invest in the companies to realise: the growth will happen, but India just takes a bit more time. Throwing money doesn t solve problems. 

Value for money? The unicorn generation

Flipkart India s biggest online marketplace by volume. Started by selling books before expanding into general merchandise. Founded in 2007. Valuation at last fundraising: $15bn (May 2015)

InMobi A platform for buying mobile advertising that competes with Google and Facebook. Founded in 2007. Valuation at last fundraising: $2.5bn (December 2014)

Mu Sigma A data analytics company with operations in the US and India. Founded in 2004. Reported valuation at last fundraising: $1.5bn (February 2013)

Zomato Online restaurant directory that offers table bookings and food delivery services. Founded in 2008. Valuation at last fundraising: $1bn (April 2015)

Ola A ride-hailing app that competes with Uber. Founded in 2010. Valuation at last fundraising: $5bn (November 2015)

PayTM Payments and online shopping platform backed by China s Alibaba. Founded in 2010. Valuation at last fundraising: $3.4bn (September 2015)

Quikr Classified advertisement site that supports payments and deliveries. Founded in 2008. Valuation at last fundraising: $1bn (April 2015)

ShopClues Marketplace with a capital-light model. Does not own any warehouses. Founded in 2011. Valuation at last fundraising: $1.1bn (January 2016)

Snapdeal Started by offering discount vouchers but became a general marketplace a year later. Founded in 2010. Valuation at last fundraising: $6.5bn (February 2016)

Tech
Intel, Apple Add to Artificial-Intelligence  Deal Wave; Chip giant to buy Nervana Systems on heels of iPhone maker's Turi acquisition

By Don Clark 
843 words
9 August 2016
21:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Technology companies are hurriedly snapping up startups in the field of artificial intelligence, and Intel Corp. is the latest to join a buying spree fueled by one of the hottest trends in the tech sector.

The chip maker on Tuesday announced plans to pay an undisclosed amount for Nervana Systems, a 48-employee company working on semiconductors, software and services to exploit a popular AI technique called deep learning.

Intel's move follows "http://www.wsj.com/articles/apple-buys-machine-learning-startup-turi-1470448718" by Apple Inc. to purchase Turi Inc., a Seattle-based specialist in the field. The two acquisitions add to a string of 31 purchases since 2011 of AI startups by large companies, according to venture-capital research firm CB Insights. The firm lists buyers that include Alphabet Inc.'s Google, Twitter Inc., Yahoo Inc., International Business Machines Corp. and Salesforce.com Inc.

Factoring in smaller acquirers, PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP counts 29 related acquisitions so far this year, suggesting the total deal count for 2016 will top the 37 deals announced last year.

"We are on a very swift and increasing pace," said Todson Page, the consulting and accounting firm's U.S. technology industry deals leader.

Industry executives link the deal activity to the rapidly widening applications of AI, a broad term referring to ways to make computers emulate attributes of the human brain. An initial cycle of excitement about the technology arose in the 1980s and later fizzled, but interest has boomed in recent years due to breakthroughs in the field known as machine learning, especially in the specific area of deep learning.

Instead of programming machines explicitly to, say, recognize a human face, deep learning allows computers to learn to perform the task by analyzing vast troves of images of faces. Apple, for instance, uses deep learning to analyze speech commands issued to Siri, the software used for a variety of tasks on iPhones. Other applications include detecting credit-card fraud, interpreting medical images, studying crop growth, and enabling drones and self-driving cars to navigate.

"AI is about to transform industry after industry," said Andrew Ng, a deep-learning pioneer who is chief scientist at Baidu Inc. and an associate professor at Stanford University. Dollar figures for the AI acquisition binge are hard to come by as are figures for individual AI transactions because the big-tech companies are swooping in on startups before they have built market valuations of a size that buyers would be compelled to disclose. The buyers need talent more than technology, Mr. Ng said, since today's AI products quickly will need to be updated.Google, which led its peers with nine purchases, according to CB Insights, mined the AI research community in 2013 by buying DNNresearch, a company helmed by the influential University of Toronto AI researcher Geoffrey Hinton.Intel has special motivations. While its Xeon chips handle most computing tasks in data centers, rival Nvidia Corp. has popularized chips known as graphics processing units, or GPUs, to more quickly handle specialized chores associated with deep learning.

The following year, Google"http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702304691904579345641537039368", a London-based startup founded by Cambridge University graduates that some publications pegged at a price tag of more than $400 million. The DeepMind team developed AlphaGo, the AI software that made headlines earlier this year by defeating a South Korean master of the game Go.

Apple helped to ignite the recent consumer AI boom following "http://blogs.wsj.com/venturecapital/2010/04/28/apple-moves-deeper-into-voice-activated-search-with-siri-buy/". In buying Turi, the company stands to benefit from the expertise of Chief Executive Carlos Guestrin, who holds a University of Washingtonmachine learning professorship endowed by Amazon.com Inc.

Industry executives say an array of other companies feel a need to counter AI results at younger companies such as Google and Facebook Inc.

"They are arming up," said Matt Ocko, a managing partner at the venture-capital firm Data Collective, which invested in Nervana.

The pressures are particularly acute on companies that manage vast amounts of images or sounds, said Greg Papadopoulos, a venture partner at New Enterprise Associates, an investor in Turi. Companies that don't step up activity through acquisitions, he said, soon will face serious competitive threats.

Nervana, which divides its staff between the California cities of San Diego and Palo Alto, has AI software that exploits GPUs but also expects to deliver a chip next year it says can complete deep-learning tasks even more quickly.

Jason Waxman, a corporate vice president in Intel's data-center group, declined to say how it might use Nervana's chips. But Naveen Rao, Nervana's chief executive and co-founder, said Intel had unique manufacturing and technical expertise to help propagate the startup's technology.

"No matter how much money we raised, we would not have access to the same kind of technology as we will have at Intel," Mr. Rao said.

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Pharmaceutical Product Development LLC, a contract research organization backed by Carlyle Group LP and Hellman & Friedman, agreed to buy Evidera Inc., a CRO peer backed by Symphony Technology Group.

Evidera, of Bethesda, Md., provides health economics, outcomes research, market access, data analytics and epidemiology services to life sciences organizations to help them gauge the effectiveness and value of biopharmaceutical products.

Wilmington, N.C.-based PPD said in a news release that the Evidera acquisition will strengthen PPD's capabilities in the later stages of clinical trials and in the post-drug approval phase.

PPD, with offices in 46 countries, provides drug discovery, development, life-cycle management and laboratory services, serving clients in the pharmaceutical, biotechnology, medical device, academic and government sectors.

Terms of the Evidera transaction weren't disclosed. PPD said in the release that the deal is expected to close in the third quarter.

It added that Evidera will continue to operate its office locations in North America and Europe and expand its network of experts in major markets around world-wide.

Evidera was created as an independent company in 2013 when Symphony Technology Group acquired the health economics, outcomes research, market access, epidemiology and data analytics practices of United BioSource Corp., a wholly owned subsidiary of pharmacy benefit management business Express Scripts Holding Co., according to the company's website. The "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0020140108ea18iyl29" it bought health-care modeling business Archimedes Inc. from health-care provider Kaiser Permanente.

Carlyle, of Washington, D.C., and San Francisco firm Hellman & Friedman took PPD private in 2011 in "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJLBO20111004234936" at $3.9 billion.

pict032.png

Pro Private Markets
Dentsu To Buy Majority Stake in Data Marketing Firm Merkle

By Suzanne Vranica 
592 words
8 August 2016
13:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Dentsu Inc .'s Dentsu Aegis Network has agreed to acquire a majority stake in data marketing firm Merkle, as the Japanese advertising conglomerate continues its aggressive buying spree.

Baltimore-based Merkle specializes in so-called "customer relationship marketing," which includes crafting loyalty programs for marketers and managing their vast customer databases that hold reams of consumer information. It also offers a host of other digital marketing and technology services including search advertising and data-driven ad buying and analytics.

Terms of the deal were not disclosed but people familiar with the matter said the cash deal has an enterprise value of roughly $1.5 billion when including Merkle's debt.

As part of the agreement, Dentsu said it will buy a Merkle stake held by private-equity firm Technology Crossover Ventures , as well as other Merkle shares held by other shareholders. Merkle's management and employees expect to retain a "significant" minority stake in the firm.

Closely held Merkle had $436 million in revenue in 2015 and works on behalf of companies such as MetLife Inc ., Geico Corp ., and Dell Inc . In recent years, it has invested in ad and marketing technologies to help power its clients' campaigns. Its systems are closely integrated with Facebook , for example, so Merkle could help target ads for a retailer using the data the retailer collects from its customers such as email addresses.

Dentsu was attracted to Merkle, in part, for its data capabilities, which allow Merkle to develop highly targeted marketing efforts for its clients based on data it has on consumers.

"Their ability to develop people-based-marketing highlights where this business is going," said Jerry Buhlmann , chief executive officer of Dentsu Aegis Network. "Convergence is driving our business towards a much greater level of addressability."

Merkle is the latest deal in a long string of acquisitions by Dentsu , the fifth-largest ad company in the world, by revenue. It is seeking to reduce its dependence on its home market of Japan, which isn't growing as fast as other countries.

Dentsu derived 54.3% of its business from markets outside of Japan in fiscal 2015, up from 15% in fiscal 2012. Its shopping spree went into high gear in 2013 with its $5 billion acquisition of U.K.-based Aegis Group PLC , a big player in the ad buying space. Dentsu has signed over 80 deals in the past three years.

Last month, it bought Gyro, one of the largest independent ad firms that specializes in business-to-business advertising. Its other acquisitions this year have included digital analytics firm Cardinal Path, digital agency Flock, and sports marketing firm Alesport Group.

The company said it has no plans to abandon that strategy. "We are still continuing to use acquisitions to accelerate our growth" and bolster Dentsu 's product suite, added Mr. Buhlmann, pointing to areas of continued interest such as social marketing, mobile and data marketing.

Merkle decided to sell to Dentsu because the ad company will be able to help Merkle expand in overseas markets with the help of its big footprint around the globe. Merkle has been trying to expand in Europe and Asia.

"To be able to bring capital to support our own acquisition strategy is a big part of this deal," said David Williams, Merkle's chief executive officer.

Dentsu Aegis was advised on the transaction by Lazard while Merkle was advised by Evercore.

Write to Suzanne Vranica at "mailto:suzanne.vranica@wsj.com"

Last month, it bought Gyro, one of the largest independent ad firms that specializes in business-to-business advertising. Its other acquisitions this year have included digital analytics firm Cardinal Path, digital agency Flock, and sports marketing firm Alesport Group.

The company said it has no plans to abandon that strategy. "We are still continuing to use acquisitions to accelerate our growth" and bolster Dentsu 's product suite, added Mr. Buhlmann, pointing to areas of continued interest such as social marketing, mobile and data marketing.

Merkle decided to sell to Dentsu because the ad company will be able to help Merkle expand in overseas markets with the help of its big footprint around the globe. Merkle has been trying to expand in Europe and Asia.

"To be able to bring capital to support our own acquisition strategy is a big part of this deal," said David Williams, Merkle's chief executive officer.

Dentsu Aegis was advised on the transaction by Lazard while Merkle was advised by Evercore.

Axa tracks news to beat the market

By Aliya Ram

Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Axa Investment Managers has begun analysing news articles to help the French asset manager predict market movements and better gauge the performance of the companies in which it invests.

The  679bn fund house said it is trialling a system to monitor news announcements and is in discussions with a San Francisco-based data analytics company to help it beat the market and give it an advantage over competitors.

Jeremy Baskin, chief executive of Axa's  19bn quantitative investing arm, Axa Rosenberg, said he had considered the move before, but the tools to monitor news have now become more cost effective.

"What has changed is that natural-language analytics and technology are just cheaper and more readily available," he said.

"We have already been looking at credit card data and transactional data and we have begun that assessment for news items."

Mainstream asset managers have been slower than banks and hedge funds to experiment with artificially intelligent data tools that can read tweets, news articles and other non-traditional information on the internet to inform investment decisions.

But a growing number of fund houses have begun to look at newsflow analytics and other online data sources in an attempt to cut costs and improve performance.

BlackRock, the world's largest fund house, said in October that it had entered into talks with Google, the technology company, to explore how big data can improve its investment decisions. BlackRock declined to comment.

Many quantitative managers are still sceptical. "Big-data tools just track what happens and do not ask for the reason why things happen," said Matthew Stemp, head of client relations at Berenberg Bank.

The 1980s movie "Back to the Future Part II" transports viewers to a 2015 where people pay for cab rides with the swipe of a finger.

In the real 2016, fingerprints have entered the mainstream as a way to log on to smart devices, but it's still difficult to pay for rides or at the cash register with just a finger.

To be successful, a payment system would have to match the print against a database that may contain thousands or millions of other prints, and -- to keep impatient customers happy -- do it within seconds.

That's the challenge Yasuhiro Kuda says his Tokyo-based startup, Liquid Inc., has addressed. Backed by the Japanese government and some of the nation's biggest names in finance and technology -- including the venture-capital arms of banking giant Mizuho Financial Group Inc. and cellphone provider NTT DoCoMo Inc. -- Liquid is rolling out a fingerprint-payment system at retailers in Japan and elsewhere in Asia.

Biometrics -- identification through fingerprints, veins, facial features or any other pattern unique to each individual -- has long been considered an ideal form of authentication.

Most biometric systems used by consumers today, including the fingerprint ID used to unlock an Apple Inc. iPhone or iPad, deliver "1:1" identification. The iPhone knows only one fingerprint -- the owner's -- and checks for a match with the one finger on the touch button.

Liquid's system is an example of more-difficult "1:N" identification, which requires finding a match between the fingerprint offered and a large database of prints.

Thanks to artificial-intelligence techniques including "deep learning" -- in which programs figure out on their own how to improve -- Liquid's system can deliver fast and accurate 1:N authentication, Mr. Kuda said.

One business testing Liquid's system is Huis Ten Bosch Co., the operator of a theme park in southern Japan. Season-pass holders who let the park take their fingerprints can pay for food and rides inside the park with just a touch. Though only a few have tried the system, implementation has been "very smooth," a spokeswoman said.

Technology
Fingerprint Payment Shows Gains

By Takashi Mochizuki 
838 words
8 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B3
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

TOKYO -- The 1980s movie "Back to the Future Part II" transports viewers to a 2015 where people pay for cab rides with the swipe of a finger.

In the real 2016, fingerprints have entered the mainstream as a way to log on to smart devices, but it's still difficult to pay for rides or at the cash register with just a finger.

To be successful, a payment system would have to match the print against a database that may contain thousands or millions of other prints, and -- to keep impatient customers happy -- do it within seconds.

That's the challenge Yasuhiro Kuda says his Tokyo-based startup, Liquid Inc., has addressed. Backed by the Japanese government and some of the nation's biggest names in finance and technology -- including the venture-capital arms of banking giant Mizuho Financial Group Inc. and cellphone provider NTT DoCoMo Inc. -- Liquid is rolling out a fingerprint-payment system at retailers in Japan and elsewhere in Asia, including Sri Lanka and the Philippines.

"Biometrics is convenient but it's not widely used as much as it should be," said Mr. Kuda, a 31-year-old Okinawa native who used to work at a Japanese brokerage.

Biometrics -- identification through fingerprints, veins, facial features or any other pattern unique to each individual -- has long been considered an ideal form of authentication.

Research firm Tractica predicts the global biometrics market will expand to $14.9 billion by 2024 from $2 billion in 2015.

The difficulty lies in the fuzziness of the information. A computer can quickly confirm whether the digital data on a credit-card magnetic strip or chip matches an entry in a database of card holders. It isn't so easy to match the patterns on a fingertip.

Most biometric systems used by consumers today, including the fingerprint ID used to unlock an Apple Inc. iPhone or iPad, deliver "1:1" identification.

The iPhone knows only one fingerprint -- the owner's -- and checks for a match with the one finger on the touch button.

In another kind of 1:1 identification, a person first presents a physical card or ID number, then offers a fingerprint as confirmation.

This method may not be the most convenient for consumers, but it offers security benefits because a criminal would need both to copy the victim's fingerprint and steal the person's ID, said Marios Savvides, director of Cylab Biometrics Center at Carnegie Mellon University.

Liquid's system is an example of more-difficult "1:N" identification, which requires finding a match between the fingerprint offered and a large database of prints. This is nothing new for police departments, which use 1:N systems to check fingerprints taken at crime scenes. The difference is that Liquid can't wait a day for the crime lab, and it must rule out all the prints in the database except the match.

Thanks to artificial-intelligence techniques including "deep learning" -- in which programs figure out on their own how to improve -- Liquid's system can deliver fast and accurate 1:N authentication, Mr. Kuda said. He said the fingerprint readers can distinguish between prints from a living human and those imprinted in, say, gelatin or clay.

One business testing Liquid's system is Huis Ten Bosch Co., the operator of a theme park in southern Japan. Season-pass holders who let the park take their fingerprints can pay for food and rides inside the park with just a touch.

Though only a few have tried the system, implementation has been "very smooth," a spokeswoman said.

In June, Liquid teamed up with Japanese telecommunications operator KDDI Corp. on a service for travelers visiting Tokyo's Ikebukuro shopping district. They can register their fingerprints and credit cards when they check into their hotel, then shop or even rent a car using just a finger.

The government is backing the service, hoping it can be introduced more widely by the time the city hosts the 2020 Summer Olympics.

"It looks interesting and we have never done it before, so wanted to give it a try," said Chien Yu Lin, a 24-year-old visiting with her parents from Taiwan.

Some experts aren't sure Mr. Kuda's ideas are realistic.

"It is not useful for commercial verification without sacrifices in speed and accuracy," said William Saito, an entrepreneur who has worked on biometric technology and advises the Japanese government on cybersecurity.

Such doubts could lead merchants using Liquid's system to require a second form of identification, such as a card -- undercutting the simplicity of Mr. Kuda's technology.

Gartner analyst Ant Allan said he isn't aware of competitors offering similar services, but added that market interest has yet to develop. For that to happen, he said, Liquid must build trust incrementally.

Other tech companies say they're working on 1:N, but with institutional customers in mind.

---

Rena Nakachi Simkowitz contributed to this article.

Former Barclays chief Antony Jenkins has joined a digital provider of foreign exchange and payment services in his first company directorship since leaving the bank last year.

Mr Jenkins, who was ousted from Barclays last July after reports of a disagreement about the future of the investment banking division, has become chairman of Currencies Direct.

His appointment comes a year after the fintech group was acquired by private equity firms Corsair Capital and Palamon Capital Partners in a deal that valued Currencies Direct at over 200m.

In his role, Mr Jenkins aims to help steer the company through what he believes is a digital revolution in financial services.

"We are seeing a time where technology has evolved to a point where it is creating a truly transformational customer experience," he says.

However, he warns that traditional high street banks need to change their culture to deal with the "tsunami" of new technology that threatens to disrupt their business.

He estimates the number of branches and staff employed in the financial services sector could drop by as much as 50 per cent over the next decade.

Digital developments such as artificial intelligence will drive automation through banks, he says, replacing the need for as many staff. Customer demand for mobile is also causing a rapid decrease in branch footfall.

His comments come after the Bank of England cut the base rate to a record low of 0.25 per cent, putting further pressure on banks' profit margins. To compensate, lenders are set to make more aggressive cost cuts. Last week, Lloyds revealed it would close a further 200 branches and cut 3,000 jobs.

Royal Bank of Scotland chief Ross McEwan said yesterday, after the bank posted a 2bn net loss in the first half, that in the low-interest rate environment "clearly income will be lower therefore we will have to look at costs."

Mr Jenkins says: "It is incredibly hard for incumbents to change their business model . . . People inside the banks have a clear view of how things have already been done. It's less risky to continue with the status quo."

Tech
Cambridge's Tech Hub Generates Successes and Growing Pains; The historic college town has transformed into a modern tech hub

By Stu Woo 
1,066 words

The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

CAMBRIDGE, England After a long push inspired by Silicon Valley, this centuries-old college town has taken on a new role as a modern, tech-industry hub.

The reward has been a flood of jobs and money. The downsides have been the same sort of housing and traffic strains beleaguering the San Francisco Bay Area. Another, more recent complaint by some locals: Foreigners keep snapping up the best companies that have sprouted here.

Cambridge's tech bona fides were validated afresh last month when Japanese telecommunications giant SoftBank Group Corp. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-bets-on-internet-of-things-with-arm-deal-1468946389" for ARM Holdings PLC. The "http://www.wsj.com/articles/arms-race-to-the-top-started-with-integration-1468837394" in more than 95% of the world's smartphones.

That deal follows the acquisitions of other, well-known Cambridge-affiliated firms by some of America's tech titans. In 2011, Hewlett-Packard"http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424053111903596904576517690864419816" for Autonomy Corp., an analytics-software developer that, at the time, was Britain's reigning tech darling. That deal ended in acrimony after Hewlett-Packard"http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424127887324352004578130712448913412", an allegation Autonomy's founder is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/autonomy-former-ceo-sues-h-p-for-more-than-150-million-1443724340".

More recently, Alphabet Inc.'s Google"http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052702304691904579345641537039368"DeepMind Technologies Ltd., a giant in the world of artificial intelligence. Microsoft Corp. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/microsofts-swiftkey-acquisition-could-make-mobile-apps-more-productive-1454538400" SwiftKey, which designs smartphone keyboards that try to predict what a person is typing.

"I'm happy at some level to have great U.K. tech successes," said Andy Hopper, the head of the University of Cambridge Computer Laboratory. He was research director at Acorn Computers Ltd., which helped build ARM. On the other hand, he said he fears ARM will be vulnerable "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-shares-tumble-as-investors-question-32-billion-arm-deal-1468902976". SoftBank has pledged to double the size of ARM's 1,600-employee British workforce in five years.

There is a new potential challenge to Cambridge as a global tech hub: Brexit. Some industry players, including Mr. Hopper, worry the U.K.'s vote in June to leave the European Union, could damp Cambridge's tech clout if it makes it harder for EU citizens to work in the U.K. At the same time, British venture capitalists say the plummeting pound, a collateral effect of Brexit, may also make U.K. tech firms more attractive to foreign takeovers.

The city's recent tech offspring has helped Cambridge punch above its weight. It remains a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/oxford-catches-up-to-cambridge-in-tech-1445904044", for instance, a key benchmark for cities aspiring to be the next Silicon Valley.

Venture-backed companies in the area raised $317 million in equity financing in the first half of 2016, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. By comparison, venture-backed companies in London raised $1.2 billion. Those in the Bay Area hauled in $9 billion over the same period.

Still, Cambridge has become the pride of the U.K. tech industry, which dubs the area "Silicon Fen," a nod to both its budding computer-engineering sector and age-old marshes. About 57,000 people work at more than 1,500 local tech firms with a combined annual revenue of more than 13 billion, according to estimates from the University of Cambridge, which has helped foster the tech boom.

Founded in 1209, the University of Cambridge is the English-speaking world's second-oldest university behind the University of Oxford, which is about 100 years older. Over the centuries, the university here played host to a variety of innovators including physicists Isaac Newton and Stephen Hawking and poets John Milton and Lord Byron.

Just as Stanford University helped spur Silicon Valley, the University of Cambridge did the same starting in 1970. It used its lands and funds to establish the Cambridge Science Park to house and support high-tech businesses.

"They did look to Silicon Valley and Kendall Square" the area near the Massachusetts Institute of Technology in that other Cambridge, outside Boston "as something to aspire to," said Tony Raven, chief executive of Cambridge Enterprise Ltd., a university initiative that helps faculty and students commercialize tech-startup ideas.

The organization shares a building with IdeaSpace, an incubator and co-working space for Cambridge entrepreneurs, at the west end of campus. It is housed in a gleaming glass building, past the Cambridge Lawn Tennis Club and across from the university farm's wheat field.

The university's tech initiatives paralleled the rise of local tech firms such as Sinclair Computers, founded in 1973, and ARM, founded in 1990, that took advantage of the brain power graduating from Cambridge every June. Microsoft said it started a Cambridge lab in 1997 because of the city's "rich history as a center of higher learning and growing global impact as a leading high-tech hub."

London-based pharmaceutical giant AstraZeneca PLC is in the midst of moving its global research-and-development center from near Manchester, England, to Cambridge, where it plans to have 2,000 employees.

Now Cambridge is experiencing classic boomtown stresses. The median price of a Cambridge house in the second quarter of 2015 was 360,000 (about $476,633 at current exchange rates), up 47% from 245,000 from the same period in 2010, according to government data. That outpaced rising prices in London. The median rental rate of a private-market, one-bedroom flat in Cambridge for the year ended September 2015 was 900 a month, the highest rate in England outside London, according to government data.

Eben Upton, founder of the nonprofit Raspberry Pi Foundation, known for its $5 computers, said the 10-mile, rush-hour drive to his central Cambridge office takes 45 minutes, or just five minutes less than cycling. "There's a need for some sort of plausible transportation strategy," Mr. Upton said.

Mr. Raven, the Cambridge Enterprise chief, said the U.K. lacks the infrastructure deep-pocketed venture-capital firms or corporate investors with tech-sector expertise needed to keep promising companies under local control. He said this has extended a British problem that predates the modern tech industry.

"We came up with penicillin. We came up with the jet engine," Mr. Raven said. "If you look across the world, the great technologies were designed by the British, but they were not exploited by the British."

Markets
Tracy's Take: Post-Postcrisis Bank of England, Again, Cuts Capital Requirements; The move is seen likely to further inflame the debate over postcrisis regulation

By Ryan Tracy 
596 words
5 August 2016
12:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.K. regulators have once again fiddled with bank capital rules as they try to keep the country's economy on track after the vote to exit the European Union, a move likely to further inflame the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/decision-to-loosen-u-k-banks-capital-constraints-divides-experts-1468874551".

The little-noticed shift finalized Thursday "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bank-of-england-cuts-key-interest-rate-to-new-low-1470309155" underscores the dilemma facing central banks struggling to balance their sometimes-competing goals of shoring up financial stability and shoring up growth.

Specifically, the Bank of England's Prudential Regulation Authority said it would allow banks to exclude reserves held at central banks from their calculation of the "leverage ratio,' a key measurement of bank capital adopted globally after the financial crisis.

"The U.K. has recognized that applying their leverage ratio &endangers monetary policy when interest rates are low and accommodative policy has reached its limits," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc., in an e-mail. "This is not a unique U.K. concern."

Ms. Petrou's firm has "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/FedFin arguing the leverage ratio could create new risks.

While technical, the ratio lies at the heart of one of the most intensive debates between bankers and regulators.

The ratio measures equity capital as a percentage of a bank's total assets. Proponents say it is the best measure of bank capital because of its simplicity: Unlike other capital rules, it doesn't rely on complex calculations about which assets might pose a future risk.

Banks prefer the more complex rules, which can allow them to carry less loss-absorbing capital. They have for years argued that the leverage ratio doesn't make sense because it treats super safe assets the same as risky assets.

As evidence, they often point to reserves at central banks. The leverage ratio requires banks maintain the same amount of capital against those assets as they would a subprime mortgage. Banks argue that effectively discourages them from holding central bank reserves. If a riskier - but more profitable - asset counts the same under capital rules as a safe asset, why not hold the riskier asset instead?

It appears U.K. policy makers are sympathetic to the industry's argument that the leverage ratio could discourage banks from boosting their reserves. The BOE is trying to stimulate the U.K. economy in part by boosting the amount of cash banks have on hand to lend. For that policy to work, banks may need to boost the amount of cash they keep on reserve at the central bank.

The aim of the policy change "is to ensure that the leverage ratio does not act as a barrier to the effective implementation of policy measures that might lead to an increase in central bank reserves," the central bank said in a "http://www.bankofengland.co.uk/publications/Pages/news/2016/062.aspx" last week, before the new policy was made final by the PRA Thursday.

The U.K. move is likely to embolden skeptics of the leverage ratio at a crucial time. The Basel Committee of global regulators is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/basel-committee-weighs-both-a-tougher-standard-and-a-measure-to-water-it-down-1459933202". U.S. banks have so far been unsuccessful in their efforts to scale back the U.S. leverage ratio, which is far tougher on the very largest American banks than similar rules imposed in other countries.

Pro Private Markets
Or's Take: Private Equity Warms Up to Managed Care; The need to control costs lures firms to the sector

By Amy Or 
382 words
5 August 2016
13:48
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Private equity is warming to managed care, betting that the doctors they back are so good they deserve to earn more.

Clayton Dubilier & Rice LLC, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Pamplona Capital Management over the past two years have backed physician groups that contract with commercial insurers and government agencies to manage part or all of a population's care at predetermined charge for each member, each month. The goal of such businesses is to keep expenses below total cash inflow and thus generate profits.

Private equity for years steered clear of managed-care providers because of the risks associated with their relatively unpredictable earnings, which often vary depending on how sick their patients get or how well they manage those patients, said Scott Bingham, a co-practice leader of advisory firm FTI Consulting's transaction advisory services group.

More recently, however, health-care reform has put greater pressure on hospitals and other providers to operate more efficiently by linking reimbursements to the quality of care. That shift has made managed care "much more interesting," said Jeremy Gelber, a partner at Pamplona Capital.

Reimbursements to managed-care providers can be four to six times those that typical fee-for-service providers get, said Ravi Sachdev, partner at CD&R, which recently backed Agilon Health, a managed care provider focused on Medicare and Medicaid patients.

Michael Loiacano, a partner who leads the health-care services practice at executive search firm Heidrick & Struggles International Inc., said that private-equity firms aren't only backing managed-care providers but also the technology companies that support them. Such businesses track patients' diagnoses and treatment, and monitor them to ensure a doctor's thousands of patients get the care they need.

"That's where reporting, logistics, analytics and big data can really support [a physician]," said Ronald Williams, the former chairman and chief executive of insurer Aetna Inc. who is now chairman of Agilon.

He added that "more care isn't always better care. Today, we have the metrics to demonstrate what care is better care and what care isn't."

Pro Private Markets
Tech-Enabled Care-Management Company PopHealthCare Hires Bank to Seek Investor; Founder not looking to sell control of the company, said one person close to discussions

By Amy Or 
593 words
5 August 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A number of private-equity firms are circling PopHealthCare LLC, a service provider that specializes in technology-enabled care management.

People familiar with the situation said that PopHealthCare, of Tempe, Ariz., hired investment bank Cain Brothers & Co. to help it find an investor to aid in its expansion plans.

One of the people said the founder of the privately owned company has no plans to sell PopHealthCare outright.

Two other people said PopHealthCare, which provides health analytics and risk adjustment services to payers and health-care providers, as well as provision-of-care services to high-risk populations, so far has garnered bids that value the company at up to $150 million.

PopHealthCare posted annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of $7 million, the people said. A $150 million enterprise value translates to more than 20 times the company's Ebitda, reflecting keen interest among investors in new technologies that can improve the quality of care while driving down costs.

The company specializes in what it calls population management, which refers to an approach that aims to improve the health of a segment of the population through identifying patients who may run the risk of having serious health issues and providing them with preventive and clinical support, often in a lower cost setting.

The highest-risk population often incurs the most health-care spending. A 2014 Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality study estimated that 5% of the nation's sickest population accounted for 50% of overall health-care expenditure.

Such proactive care management is also deemed to be the preferred mode of care going forward, as ongoing health-care reforms are altering the payment model to one based on the quality of care rather than on the volume of care provided.

The government has been a major driving force behind that shift. The Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services recently proposed a bundled payment system for treating heart attacks as well as hip and knee replacements, reimbursing physicians and providers a lump sum for the care of Medicare patients rather than billing for each individual service rendered during the course of care.

PopHealthCare, which also has an office in Nashville, Tenn., said on its website it partners with payers and health organizations on risk assessment and care delivery systems for high-risk populations, serving 40 health plans across 49 states in the U.S. and in Puerto Rico.

The company predominantly serves government payer clients under programs such as Medicare Advantage and managed Medicaid, as well as clients who are eligible for both Medicaid and Medicare. PopHealthCare also works with commercial health-exchange clients and in long-term care markets, including in facility-, home- and community-based care settings.

PopHealthCare in 2014 bought Educerus Health LLC, a provider of in-home screening programs focused on the evaluation and diagnosis of key chronic conditions for Medicare Advantage plans, an integrated care program for individuals in which private companies contract with Medicare.

PopHealthCare's management has experience in the managed-care field. Chief Executive Mike Tudeen previously served in the same position for care and care-management service provider Inspiris Ltd., which was backed by venture-capital firm River Cities Capital Funds. Founded in 1998, Inspiris served medically complex, chronically ill and elderly patients in Medicare, Medicaid and commercial insurance populations. Inspiris was "http://rccf.com/capital-fund-focus/healthcare-services/case-study-inspiris/"UnitedHealth Group Inc. in 2011.

Tracy's Take: Post-Postcrisis Bank of England, Again, Cuts Capital Requirements

Justice Department Appeals Ruling Throwing Out Crisis-Era Bank of America Case

Post-Postcrisis Bank of England, Again, Cuts Capital Requirements

U.K. regulators have once again fiddled with bank capital rules as they try to keep the country's economy on track after the vote to exit the European Union, a move likely to further inflame the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/decision-to-loosen-u-k-banks-capital-constraints-divides-experts-1468874551".

The little-noticed shift finalized Thursday "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bank-of-england-cuts-key-interest-rate-to-new-low-1470309155" underscores the dilemma facing central banks struggling to balance their sometimes-competing goals of shoring up financial stability and shoring up growth.

Specifically, the Bank of England's Prudential Regulation Authority said it would allow banks to exclude reserves held at central banks from their calculation of the "leverage ratio,' a key measurement of bank capital adopted globally after the financial crisis.

"The U.K. has recognized that applying their leverage ratio &endangers monetary policy when interest rates are low and accommodative policy has reached its limits," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner of the policy analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc., in an e-mail. "This is not a unique U.K. concern."

Ms. Petrou's firm has "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/FedFin arguing the leverage ratio could create new risks.

While technical, the ratio lies at the heart of one of the most intensive debates between bankers and regulators.

The ratio measures equity capital as a percentage of a bank's total assets. Proponents say it is the best measure of bank capital because of its simplicity: Unlike other capital rules, it doesn't rely on complex calculations about which assets might pose a future risk.

Banks prefer the more complex rules, which can allow them to carry less loss-absorbing capital. They have for years argued that the leverage ratio doesn't make sense because it treats super safe assets the same as risky assets.

As evidence, they often point to reserves at central banks. The leverage ratio requires banks maintain the same amount of capital against those assets as they would a subprime mortgage. Banks argue that effectively discourages them from holding central bank reserves. If a riskier - but more profitable - asset counts the same under capital rules as a safe asset, why not hold the riskier asset instead?

It appears U.K. policy makers are sympathetic to the industry's argument that the leverage ratio could discourage banks from boosting their reserves. The BOE is trying to stimulate the U.K. economy in part by boosting the amount of cash banks have on hand to lend. For that policy to work, banks may need to boost the amount of cash they keep on reserve at the central bank.

The aim of the policy change "is to ensure that the leverage ratio does not act as a barrier to the effective implementation of policy measures that might lead to an increase in central bank reserves," the central bank said in a "http://www.bankofengland.co.uk/publications/Pages/news/2016/062.aspx" last week, before the new policy was made final by the PRA Thursday.

The U.K. move is likely to embolden skeptics of the leverage ratio at a crucial time. The Basel Committee of global regulators is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/basel-committee-weighs-both-a-tougher-standard-and-a-measure-to-water-it-down-1459933202". U.S. banks have so far been unsuccessful in their efforts to scale back the U.S. leverage ratio, which is far tougher on the very largest American banks than similar rules imposed in other countries.

Politics, Home Bias and Transmitting Risk

The European financial crisis was aggravated by the so-called banks-sovereign nexus and that can be blamed on politics, "http://www.federalreserve.gov/econresdata/feds/2016/files/2016060pap.pdf". The two researchers for the Federal Reserve set out to determine why European banks suffer from a significant bias toward domestic government debt in their sovereign bond portfolios, nodding to the argument that such holdings were the critical channel that transmitted credit risk from sovereigns under stress to the real economy. The pair analyzed 2011 stress test data on European banks and concluded that certain banks held a disproportionate amount of their own country's sovereign debt because they were coerced by domestic politicians. Politically connected banks   those owned by the government or have board members who are former politicians   held more domestic sovereign bonds, the researchers found. That "home bias" increased during the crisis, especially after government bailouts, though the injections themselves weren't politically motivated, they say.

Is Modigliani-Miller Right About Capital Requirements

The Modigliani-Miller theory of bank capital posits that higher capital requirements result in negligible costs for banks, making them an effective way for regulators to increase the safety of banks without becoming a drag on their performance. But those costs actually create a tradeoff that regulators should consider more carefully, "https://www.magnetmail.net/actions/email_web_version.cfm?recipient_id=1350835540&message_id=13337985&user_id=IIF_&group_id=1813924&jobid=34782165", an industry group. The financial industry's analysis of Modigliani-Miller shows that it "cannot on its own support arguments for new capital requirements," the group says. They say that the cost of equity capital has risen significantly in regions that have increased their capital ratios above the Basel III minimum requirements, which seems to contradict Modigliani-Miller's predictions. They also note the decline of bank profitability in these regions. Increasing capital requirements "will do more to reduce the volume and increase the cost of lending than to increase stability in the system" the note concludes.

SEC: Judge and Jury

The SEC's increased use of administrative-law judges is an "abuse," "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-sec-plays-judge-and-jury-1470353410". Citing the SEC case involving Lynn Tilton, CEO of Patriarch Partners LLC, Ms. Strassel says the specifics "are long, complex and best weighed by a court of law. But they aren't being settled by any outside court and therein rests the abuse. Ever since the Dodd-Frank financial "reform," the SEC has been pushing more cases toward in-house administrative-law judges. These judges are hired by the SEC and sit on the commission's payroll. They have ultimate discretion over the cases they handle, and the rules severely limit discovery and depositions." The case against Ms. Tilton, Ms. Strassel writes, "shows another Obama agency that like the EPA or the IRS seems to think it gets to operate outside the usual rules of democracy."

Preparing for a World Without Cash

The Federal Reserve should start considering how the world might work in the future as more central banks test and adopt digital currencies, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/preparing-for-a-world-without-cash-1470353068". "As the world's central monetary power, America should play a leading role in studying the benefits and pitfalls of a digital-currency future," they write. "While plenty of risks would come with such a conversion, the potential perks are so great that it merits serious consideration."

Quant funds on the lookout for top-notch talent; Financial services: Investing

By Mary Childs and Robin Wigglesworth in New York 
797 words
4 August 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
13
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Algo traders jostle to recruit mathematicians and scientists after 6 years of client inflows

Last year, billionaire investor Steven Cohen emailed a terse question to one of his new managers, Matthew Granade, formerly of Bridgewater. The subject line said: "Have you heard of Quantopian?"

As it happened, Mr Granade sat on the board of the Boston-based start-up, which offers freelance computer scientists, mathematicians and data engineers a platform to design, test and run automated trading algorithms. Last year Quantopian began allocating money to a handful of the best ones, for an embryonic "crowdsourced" hedge fund.

Once Mr Granade had given his boss a rundown on the company, Mr Cohen said: "Sounds cool. Let's get them in."

The meeting paid off. Last week, Mr Cohen said he would not just invest $2m in the company from his venture capital unit, but also hand over up to $250m to Quantopian's "do-it-yourself" algo traders to play with.

The move underscores one of the biggest trends in the hedge fund industry - the mounting interest in "quantitative" investing, where trading is driven by sophisticated computer algorithms rather than humans.

Mr Cohen's Point72 Asset Managementalready has a computer-driven unit and a "big data" group. But the capital Quantopian will manage gives the family office access to ideas and people "in all four corners of the world", instead of "fishing in the same talent pool" as competitors, says Mr Granade, Point72's chief market intelligence officer.

"By looking in different geographies and different backgrounds, you're going to have different strategies and different ways of thinking that could very well produce something that's a lot less correlated both to markets and to the hedge fund strategies."

Quantitative investing has been around for decades and some of the hedge fund industry's most profitable members are powered by computers scanning markets for patterns, rather than human traders.

The quant industry suffered a minor heart attack in the summer of 2007, when many popular strategies went awry and inflicted serious losses on investors, but most recovered their footing quickly. Many of the more technology-savvy hedge funds proved to be the most adept in the years after the financial crisis, while the deteriorating ability of the average traditional hedge fund to beat the market has burnished investors' attraction to quants.

Such funds are on their seventh straight year of client inflows, growing from $408bn in 2009 to $879bn this year, according to data from Hedge Fund Research. Even as many hedge funds have suffered client outflows in recent quarters, computerised ones have generally continued to attract money.

As a result, more funds outside the traditional quant universe are snapping up computer scientists, overhauling their technology, and tapping big data and analytics. Take Kensho, which automates previously human-intensive research, or research firm 1010data, and credit card data provider Yodlee.

"You don't have to be at the bleeding edge any more to start analysing data, massive amounts of data," says one hedge fund quant. The differentiation comes in how the data are processed into something useful - a signal.

Banks are also responding to the mounting demand for information. JPMorgan's capital introduction team, which funnels money from investors to fund managers, has hired two people to focus on analytics.

While quant strategies are seen as "hot" right now, "it's hard to dabble and be successful", says Andrew Fishman, president at Schonfeld Strategic Advisors, whose Quantbot unit has $3bn of market exposure.

Fundamental equity investing and long-short hedge funds using quantitative analysis as part of the process is "a more likely push than really entering the quant space to trade against people like Quantbot and RenTech. People are too far removed to be able to do it," he adds.

But Quantopian hopes eventually to be able to go head-to-head with the quantitative hedge fund industry's biggest names, betting that the cumulative nous of its 80,000 plus members from 180 countries can give it an edge.

This year the company hired former Millennium Management and Bluecrest trader Jonathan Larkin as its chief investment officer to help add an institutional veneer to its motley group of computer programmers.

Quantopian's central thesis is that the biggest advantage for a quant fund is no longer its hardware but its people - and top-notch talent is in short supply. "The need for talent is so intense and we cannot produce enough talent fast enough," John Fawcett, Quantopian's founder, told the FT. "There are lots of people brilliant enough to develop these strategies, but who don't want to devote their life to investing."

RSA produces record underwriting performance

Oliver Ralph, insurance correspondent

Insurer RSA overcame fires in Canada and floods in Europe to produce a record first-half underwriting performance.

The UK-based insurer, which last year received an offer from Swiss rival Zurich, said its first-half operating profits rose a fifth to 312m. The company also raised its dividend by 43 per cent to 5p a share.

Stephen Hester, RSA s chief executive, described the results as excellent.  It is very unusual, in the current environment, to have these kinds of numbers,  he said.

He added:  We plan to pay out 40-50 per cent of our profits as dividends. There will be scope for special dividends in the future, but not in the near term. 

RSA s shares rose 2 per cent on Thursday morning, and are up 5 per cent since the UK voted in June to leave the EU. The company, which has large operations in Scandinavia and Canada, generates three-quarters of its profits in currencies other than sterling.

Barrie Cornes, analyst at Panmure Gordon, said the numbers exceeded consensus expectations.  With a for sale sign flapping above the company, these figures should highlight the attractiveness of RSA to overseas buyers helped further by weaker sterling,  he said.

RSA s underwriting profits jumped almost three-quarters to 174m as the combined ratio (claims and costs as a proportion of premiums) improved from 96 per cent to 94 per cent. That was despite large losses from events such as floods and fires coming in at 277m, which was 49m worse than last year.

Mr Hester put the improvement down to changes the company had made to its business portfolio, its underwriting models and its use of data analytics.

That underwriting performance was offset by weaker investment income, which fell a tenth to 187m because of lower interest rates.  This is the first half-year in RSA s history where underwriting profits are higher than investment income,  said Mr Hester.

Mr Hester has been restructuring RSA since he joined the company in 2014. He says that, while most of the large strategic changes are out of the way, there is still work to do. The company says it is on track to cut 250m of costs by the end of this year and to beat its 350m cost reduction target by the end of 2018.

RSA said that its Solvency II capital ratio   a measure of capital available as a proportion of the minimum required   was 158 per cent at June 30, at the upper end of its preferred 130-160 per cent target range.

Although the group s operating profits rose sharply, reported net profit more than halved to 91m because of the accounting impact of disposals.

Tracy's Take: Fed, Financial Industry Are Playing Chess; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Thursday, August 4, 2016.

Tracy's Take: Fed, Financial Industry Are Playing Chess

Barclays Cut in Half as Europe Slims Down on Wall Street

Goldman to Settle Federal Reserve Charges Over Leak of Central-Bank Secrets

One More Reason for Investors to Worry About  Earnings Before Bad Stuff'

China Inches Closer to Launching Credit-Default-Swap Market

Fed, Financial Industry Are Playing Chess

In 2014, the Federal Reserve imposed new rules on U.S. units of foreign-owned banks, despite objections from both the banks affected and their home-country regulators. On Wednesday, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/barclays-cut-in-half-as-europe-slims-down-on-wall-street-1470250141" about how some European banks appear to be responding: shrinking their U.S. brokerage units.

At first glance, it looks like the Fed rules have achieved their objective by forcing foreign-owned banks to boost the capital ratios in their U.S. operations, either by raising new equity or cutting down on risky assets. That may indeed be the ultimate outcome. But there is also evidence that the financial industry is evolving in a way that could undermine the Fed's goals.

Take Socit Gnrale SA . "http://www.wsj.com/articles/the-potential-loophole-in-the-feds-foreign-bank-rules-1470250917" the French bank appears to be taking advantage of a potential loophole in the new rules. Foreign-owned firms can move assets from their U.S.-based holding companies to their U.S.-based bank branches, the latter of which are subject to less stringent rules under the Fed regime. Socit Gnrale appears to have moved roughly $18 billion of securities financing assets from its U.S. brokerage to its U.S. branch between 2014 and 2015.

Securities financing, a typically benign but sometimes volatile form of short-term lending, is one of the activities the Fed intends to curb with the new capital rules. If a bank merely moves the activity rather than curtailing it, are the rules really working? Both the Fed and Socit Gnrale declined to comment on the firm's moves.

To be sure, other firms made significant changes in response to the rules, rather than simply reworking their legal structures to circumvent them. Barclays PLC and Deutsche Bank AG have reduced securities financing activity at their U.S. brokerages. Unlike Socit Gnrale , their American bank branches haven't grown significantly.

The twist is that new evidence shows "http://www.wsj.com/articles/direct-repo-loans-gaining-traction-as-banks-step-back-1469037601", where the Fed has limited regulatory authority. While these new players are still relatively small compared to big banks, their growing role illustrates how the financial system can reinvent itself in response to new rules.

Fed officials say they are monitoring the shift. They are contemplating "http://www.wsj.com/articles/fed-eyes-margin-rules-to-bolster-oversight-1452471174" that would apply to all players, not just banks. If the Fed moves ahead with those rules, it will once again be the industry's turn to assess the chessboard and make a move.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Barclays Cut in Half as Europe Slims Down on Wall Street

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/barclays-cut-in-half-as-europe-slims-down-on-wall-street-1470250141" no secret of their efforts to slim down, but little-noticed regulatory filings show just how deep their cuts on Wall Street have been.

Barclays PLC reduced assets at its U.S. brokerage, Barclays Capital , by 43% between the end of 2013 and the beginning of this year, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission . Assets at Deutsche Bank 's U.S. broker-dealer, Deutsche Bank Securities , were down 30% during that period, while Credit Suisse Group AG 's U.S. brokerage shrank by 26%.

The Fed's Effect on Solvency Shocks and Liquidity Shocks

The establishment of the Federal Reserve in 1914 has resulted in an interbank system that is more resilient to solvency shocks but less resilient to liquidity shocks, "http://www.federalreserve.gov/econresdata/feds/2016/files/2016059pap.pdf". The two dug into how the founding of the Fed affected the size of the interbank system and its vulnerability to shocks. They examined how creation of the Fed affected solvency risk related to contagion from central reserve city banks in New York City and Chicago to reserve city and country national banks, and how it affected the resilience of the banking system to liquidity stresses.

Diversifying the Fed

The Democratic Party platform's attack on the lack of diversity at the Federal Reserve should not be dismissed as just "election season Fed bashing" but instead taken seriously, "https://www.brookings.edu/opinions/the-feds-striking-lack-of-diversity-and-why-it-matters/". Of the 134 different presidents of the 12 Fed regional banks throughout their history, none have been African-American and none have been Latino. There have only been two nonwhite regional bank presidents, both at the Minneapolis Fed : current President Neel Kashkari and his immediate predecessor Narayana Kocherlakota . There have been only six women to serve as regional bank presidents, including current presidents Loretta Mester in Cleveland and Esther George at the Kansas City Fed . Mr. Klein cites a recent Government Accountability Office report that urged more diversity among Fed leaders to ensure a "broader spectrum of information useful for the formation and execution of monetary policy and the oversight of Reserve Bank operations." Diversity, writes Mr. Klein, was the main goal behind having a system of regional banks in the first place.

Bitcoin Falls After Exchange Is Hacked

By Paul Vigna and Gregor Stuart Hunter 
672 words
4 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The price of the virtual currency bitcoin fell sharply after Hong Kong-based digital-currency exchange Bitfinex said it was hacked, resulting in the possible theft of about $65 million worth of bitcoin.

News of the Bitfinex hack hit the price of bitcoin hard in heavy trading on Tuesday. It fell to $540 by late in the day, down about 12% from its level near $613 early Tuesday, according to CoinDesk. At one point, it traded as low as $480, down about 22%, though it recovered to about $548 by late morning in New York on Wednesday.

The hack marks one of the largest thefts in bitcoin's short history and follows a separate alleged theft of an estimated $60 million worth of ethereum, a rival virtual currency, in June. In 2014, investor confidence in bitcoin also was dented by another larger cybersecurity breach, at the Japanese exchange Mt. Gox.

Hacking and thefts of investor property stand as two of the biggest issues that may prevent the fast-growing digital currency from gaining more widespread use. Bitcoin trades on an open ledger known as the blockchain that has excited technologists for its ability to cut out expensive layers of bureaucracy in various areas of commerce.

But securing the bitcoin trading platform has proved elusive. Tuesday, Bitfinex acknowledged the latest theft in a statement on its website and said it was halting all trading on Bitfinex as well as the deposits and withdrawals of digital tokens.

"The theft is being reported to -- and we are co-operating with -- law enforcement," the statement said. "We are deeply concerned about this issue and we are committing every resource to try to resolve it."

Zane Tackett, Bitfinex's director of community and product development, confirmed that 119,756 bitcoins were stolen and said the company knows "exactly how relevant systems were compromised." At Tuesday's value, the amount of bitcoin stolen was worth about $65 million. Mr. Tackett said the company is working with law enforcement and analytics companies to try to track down the stolen coins and is working to get its platform back up so customers can check their accounts.

It wasn't clear what percentage of Bitfinex's overall assets were stolen or whether or not the company had adequate insurance to cover the theft.

"We are investigating the breach to determine what happened, but we know that some of our users have had their bitcoins stolen," the statement added. "We are undertaking a review to determine which users have been affected by the breach. While we conduct this initial investigation and secure our environment, bitfinex.com will be taken down and the maintenance page will be left up."

In 2014, the Tokyo-based exchange Mt. Gox collapsed after a yearslong series of attacks resulted in the theft of about 850,000 bitcoins, at the time worth about $450 million. About 200,000 were later recovered. In June, Mt. Gox Chief Executive Mark Karpales was released from a Japanese prison on bail, after serving 10 months. The company's liquidation is ongoing.

Bitcoin rallied earlier this year but had been selling off lately after an anticipated event known as a "halving" in early July lowered the subsidy paid to bitcoin miners supporting the network.

In 2015, Bitfinex switched to a system protected by what is known as "multiple signature" security, a feature that requires multiple "keys" to access bitcoin in a virtual wallet, and keeps the customers' money in separate accounts, rather than pooling them into one larger account.

The exchange was fined $75,000 by the U.S. Commodity Futures Trading Commission in June for offering illegal off-exchange commodity transactions financed in bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies and for failing to register as a futures commission merchant. The CFTC said at the time that Bitfinex cooperated with its investigation and voluntarily made changes to its business practices to comply with regulations.
