Pro Private Markets
Direct Investment Matchmaker Axial Raises $14M Series C

By Patience Haggin 
567 words
3 August 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

"http://www.axial.net/", a platform for accredited investors such as family offices and wealthy individuals to connect with companies seeking direct investment, has found a second matchmaking market: helping company chief executives find the bankers and service providers who can help them close a deal.

The company, incorporated as Axial Networks Inc., has raised a $14 million Series C led by Edison Partners , and it has expanded its subscriber base to include 3,300 intermediaries, including Goldman Sachs 's direct lending group and Oaktree Capital Management .

At its 2010 launch, Axial focused on connecting investors with brokers and middlemen. In early 2014 the New York-based company began making direct outreach to company chief executives themselves, who use the platform to select investment bankers and M&A advisers.

Investment bankers such as New York-based Young America Capital use the network to find clients preparing for a sale. Young America founder and Managing Partner Peter Formanek said he also uses Axial's competitor DealNexus, owned by Intralinks Holdings , to originate deals.

Chief Executive Peter Lehrman is considering opening Axial up to additional service providers. Wealth managers, for instance, are very interested in connecting with founders who may be one or two years away from selling their company. But Mr. Lehrman said the company is weighing how to do so in a way that won't alienate the CEO users.

"We want to make sure that interaction is really well-received by the CEO, not just by the wealth manager hunting for new business," Mr. Lehrman said.

Subscribers use the platform's profiles and recommendation algorithm to find matches, like on a dating site. Then they move to face-to-face meetings, and Axial plays no role in negotiations. Axial claims to have matched parties that led to 672 closed deals in 2015, according to survey results reported by users.

The site makes money from annual subscription fees, which range from $20,000 to $250,000, depending on the size of business investors are looking to invest in. Chief executives can join free. Axial has 11,000 capital providers and private companies as members, according to the startup.

Axial focuses on "the middle market, as opposed to the venture market," Mr. Lehrman said, aiming for profitable companies seeking growth equity with annual revenue between $10 million and $250 million.

Yet Kelly Ford , a partner at Edison Partners who joined Axial's board, wouldn't rule out an eventual expansion to include companies that also raise venture funding.

"The market will probably come to them, if larger-size deals start clamoring to them," Ms. Ford said. "Like any network effect business, you need to stay focused, and then the market kind of decides for you eventually."

Axial will use the new funding to build out its sales and marketing operations and to invest in its machine learning techniques to match buyers and sellers. The company has about 100 employees now, and it plans to hire about 20 more, Mr. Lehrman said. Global expansion is a priority for the company, which currently gets about 90% of its business from North America.

Returning investors Comcast Ventures , First Round Capital and Redpoint Ventures participated in the round. The new investment puts the company's total funding over $34 million. Its last financing event was an $11 million round in 2014.

JPMorgan and Virtu Financial in US Treasuries tie-up

By Nicole Bullock in New York and Joe Rennison in Washington 
526 words
3 August 2016
21:24
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

JPMorgan Chase and high-frequency trading firm Virtu Financial are teaming up in the US Treasuries market in the latest sign that banks are trying to beef up their capabilities in electronic trading.

Under a strategic partnership announced on Wednesday, Virtu will provide technology to JPMorgan to trade in the dealer-to-dealer markets for Treasuries. The agreement will last for at least three years.

The deal comes as non-bank trading firms such as Virtu that rely on advanced algorithms to trade at lightning speed have elbowed their way into the $13tn Treasury market.

Banks still dominate trading with clients such as asset managers and insurance companies but high-frequency firms have taken on a more significant role in the so-called interdealer market, which mostly trades electronically on platforms such as ICAP s BrokerTec and Nasdaq s eSpeed.

 We are committed to staying a leader in fixed income,  JPMorgan said.  Working with outside firms is complementary to our internal investments and ensures that we are evaluating innovative technologies as the markets evolve. 

Some banks have struggled to compete with the technology capabilities of high-frequency trading firms. The JPMorgan deal follows a tie-up between Credit Suisse and high-frequency trading firm Tower Research, which joined forces in mid-2014. Credit Suisse has been quiet about the specifics of the partnership, but people familiar with it say that Credit Suisse is using Tower s technology to price trades for its clients.

Other large high-frequency firms also say they have explored links with banks.

 From JPMorgan s perspective, dealer-to-dealer US Treasuries has become a very competitive electronic marketplace,  Doug Cifu, Virtu chief executive, told the FT.  It is a sub asset class where they could benefit from our efficient access to electronic markets, which is the foundation of our business. 

The boom in revenue for high-frequency firms has been tailing off with more seeking to find new sources of income as the race for speed has become less lucrative.

The JPMorgan deal helps Virtu gain a stronger foothold in the US Treasury market, which trades in larger size and less frequently than equities and futures, where high-frequency firms are well established. The bank will pay Virtu a fixed fee plus a portion of profits, said a person briefed on the terms.

 Candidly it has been a struggle for us to get into the marketplace,  Mr Cifu said.  To have the opportunity to work with a big institution that knows the market well is a blessing. 

It comes alongside an effort to provide order routing and post-trade analysis to institutional investors. Virtu is working with T Rowe Price, which is using Virtu s algorithms to improve trade execution.

Neither the T Rowe Price deal nor the JPMorgan one is exclusive and Virtu is seeking to expand these businesses as it looks to diversify revenues. Its core business is closely linked to market volumes and volatility.

Bank of New York Mellon licenses Virtu s technology infrastructure   connectivity, risk management and analytics   for foreign exchange.

Markets
One More Reason for Investors to Worry About  Earnings Before Bad Stuff'; Companies that report tailored results are more likely to encounter some kinds of accounting problems

By Michael Rapoport 
885 words
3 August 2016
22:54
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Regulators and investors are increasingly wary when companies overemphasize their own customized earnings metrics. New research shows they may have a point.

Companies that report significantly stronger earnings by using tailored figures like "adjusted net income" or "adjusted operating income" are more likely to encounter some kinds of accounting problems than those that stick to standard measures, according to research by consulting firm Audit Analytics.

The rules allow companies to report such tailored figures, and the research, conducted for The Wall Street Journal, doesn't necessarily mean such companies are less scrupulous in their bookkeeping. But it does suggest that heavy use of metrics outside of generally accepted accounting principles sometimes referred to derisively as "earnings before bad stuff" could be a warning sign.

"I would say an overprominent user of non-GAAP metrics would justify more attention and is a red flag," said Olga Usvyatsky, Audit Analytics's vice president of research. Heavy use of non-GAAP metrics may indicate a company's accounting is "more aggressive," she said.

The study focused on companies in the S&P 1500 index. It found that just 3.8% of those exclusively using standard GAAP metrics had formal earnings restatements from 2011 to 2015. Among heavy users of non-GAAP measures those whose non-GAAP earnings were at least twice as high as their GAAP net income the rate was 6.5%.

Similarly, 7.5% of the GAAP-only group had material weaknesses in internal controls flaws in their procedures to prevent financial errors and fraud versus 11% of the non-GAAP group.

Some of the numbers are small, and the use of non-GAAP metrics didn't specifically cause or relate directly to the companies' accounting flaws. Audit Analytics cautioned more research is needed.

Still, the results suggest companies using non-GAAP metrics heavily "may be somewhat less rigorous in other accounting areas" than companies using only GAAP, said Robert Pozen, a senior lecturer at the MIT Sloan School of Management.

Two examples are Valeant Pharmaceuticals International Inc. and LendingClub Corp. Both companies have been heavy users of pro forma metrics, and both have run into accounting and other problems that have hammered their shares.

Valeant restated earnings earlier this year over revenue-booking issues and said it had material weaknesses relating to its "tone at the top," or the commitment by a company's leadership to doing business ethically. Valeant has used non-GAAP metrics for years, often benefiting significantly. In 2015, the company had a GAAP loss of $291.7 million but an "adjusted" non-GAAP profit of $2.84 billion after stripping out amortization of intangible assets, acquisition costs and other expenses.

Valeant spokeswoman Laurie Little said the company believes its non-GAAP measures "are useful to investors in their assessment of our operating performance and the valuation of our company."

Online lender LendingClub forced its chief executive to resign in May after it found disclosure problems on some loans, and it too cited a "tone at the top" material weakness. The company had a 2015 GAAP loss of $5 million but non-GAAP net income of $56.8 million. LendingClub declined to comment.

Neither company was in the pool Audit Analytics examined.

Companies are allowed to use nonstandard metrics as long as they also provide GAAP numbers and show the differences between the two. The tailored measures strip out unusual or noncash items to present what companies say is a clearer picture of performance.

Even critics acknowledge the tailored metrics can sometimes be helpful showing a company's results in constant currency is a legitimate adjustment, for instance, Mr. Pozen said.

But there is also a concern they are being abused, that companies are stripping out normal, ongoing costs to make themselves look healthier.

A study in June by financial data-research firm Calcbench and corporate-compliance consultant Radical Compliance showed non-GAAP metrics inflated 2015 earnings by $164.1 billion over GAAP at a group of 816 public companies.

In May, the Securities and Exchange Commission issued new guidelines warning companies against placing too much emphasis on non-GAAP metrics. The commission also has been taking issue with companies' non-GAAP disclosures more frequently in comment letters critiquing the companies' SEC filings.

For each accounting problem examined in the Audit Analytics survey restatements, control weaknesses and so on the trend was the same. The problems occurred at relatively low levels in the GAAP-only group, at higher levels among all non-GAAP users and at still-higher levels among heavy non-GAAP users.

Any connection between non-GAAP metrics and accounting problems doesn't automatically signal aggressive practices.

For instance, companies using non-GAAP income measures to exclude employee stock compensation may be the kind of younger outfits that encounter troubles with accounting systems because they are expanding rapidly, said Patricia Dechow, a University of California at Berkeley accounting professor.

But non-GAAP use could also signify a company is concerned with managing market expectations and "may be more likely to push the line on GAAP earnings," Ms. Dechow said.

Distil Networks Raises $21 M, Plans For Acquisition

By Cat Zakrzewski 
382 words
2 August 2016
12:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Distil Networks Inc. is setting cash aside from its latest funding round to make an acquisition in the increasingly consolidating cybersecurity sector.

The company announced $21 million in Series C funding led by Silicon Valley Bank . After "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120160113ec1db0yvo" earlier this year, Chief Executive Rami Essaid said the company is earmarking $5 million to $10 million to acquire another early stage security company.

This move comes as venture capitalists are expecting more mergers and acquisitions in cybersecurity after the sector has seen some large deals, like Symantec Corp .'s $4.65 billion acquisition of Blue Coat Systems Inc .

Distil Networks uses machine learning and big data to detect bots and protect online data from scraping, a technique hackers use to collect data from websites. Mr. Essaid said the company sees an evolving threat from bots, and increasingly bots are targeting mobile apps and mobile API's.

Mr. Essaid said he sensed a positive shift in investor sentiment in recent months when raising funding from venture capitalists. Venture investment in cybersecurity startups totaled $830 million in the second quarter vs. $561 million in the first quarter, according to data from Dow Jones VentureSource.

"It was like when all your friends are standing around a pool and no one wants to jump in because they're afraid the water's cold," Mr. Essaid said of the funding environment in the first quarter of 2016.

Mr. Essaid said he was able to close a round by focusing on how the Series C would bring the company to profitability. He said the company's valuation doubled with the new round. Though he declined to comment on the exact valuation, data tracker Pitchbook estimated the company's post-money valuation to be $81 million as of its Series B round.

He said the environment is still tougher on valuations than it was a year ago.

"I thought tripling in revenue would mean we would more than double in value," Mr. Essaid said. "We came in on low end of my spectrum."

Existing investors Bessemer Venture Partners , Foundry Group and Techstars also participated in the round. The company has raised $65 million to date.

Pro Private Markets
Sensay Uses Artificial Intelligence  to Connect People Seeking Advice and Ideas

By Cat Zakrzewski 
681 words
2 August 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A chatbot-messaging platform is betting there are some questions people just can't ask their Facebook friends or iPhone contacts.

Sensay Inc. uses natural language processing to connect a one person to another anonymously through messaging apps such as Facebook Inc .'s Messenger and Kik Interactive Inc . The startup raised $4.5 million in seed funding led by Norwest Venture Partners .

Sensay conversations can be about anything from relationship advice to restaurant recommendations. When you start a Sensay chat, the chatbot asks if you need something or know something. People can type in such phrases as "Need recommendations for a book to read on vacation." Within minutes, a chat begins with another person who has told Sensay they "know" about books.

Chatbots are software agents that use artificial intelligence to understand what you type or say and then respond with an answer. They can live in chat platforms like Facebook Messenger, Slack and Line. Some can be used via SMS text messages, and others are stand-alone apps.

Chief Executive Ariel Jalali said the Los Angeles-based company has more than one million users. He added 50% of those people use Sensay to both ask and answer questions.

"People are connecting in serendipitous ways we wouldn't have imagined," Mr. Jalali said.

The team believes Sensay will be able to help people talk about topics that may be uncomfortable to post about publicly, like how to reach a custody agreement following a divorce. After each interaction with a human, Sensay prompts the other user to rate the person they just spoke to and report any abuse, limiting negativity on the platform.

As chatbots or virtual assistants come to consumers, Sensay is one of several early entrants that has combined artificial intelligence and humans to make an experience that feels like a human to human conversation. While advances in natural language processing have led to improved chatbots, many of the emerging chatbots that venture capitalists have backed still rely on human communication. They automate parts of the conversation to help those humans talk to more people more quickly. As more conversations occur, they train machine learning algorithms and make the automated conversation better and better.

Chatbots have captured the attention of venture capitalists as mobile matures and people download fewer apps. Chat platforms like Facebook Inc . see chatbots as a possible alternative to the Apple Inc . App Store.

Despite the recent flurry of investment activity in bots, it remains to be seen if the medium will take off among consumers. By combining both artificial intelligence and humans, companies like Sensay are avoiding some of the pitfalls that other early chatbots have fallen into. Microsoft Corp .'s early foray with a bot named Tay was deemed a failure after pranksters prompted her too spew anti-Semitic comments.

Many of the chatbots that have raised funding recently, like Mezi, are automating tasks that previously would be completed by customer service and sales representatives. Sensay is unique because it connects people to their peers and others with similar interests. This brings to the messaging world the types of conversations that typically take place on anonymous forums like Reddit .

Sensay declined to comment on whether it generates revenue, but the company is still considering different business models. It may one day rely on transactions in the chats, such as virtual coins. It has also developed a method to use paywalls within the chat service.

Mr. Jalali said that down the line, more of the Sensay experience may become automated. He declined to elaborate but said the future of automated conversations are less like the dystopian future presented in the "Terminator" and more like the friendly assistant R2D2 popularized by the "Star Wars" films.

Sensay has raised $6 million to date. Greycroft Partners , Sweet Capital, NHN Investment Corp., Draper Associates and other existing investors also participated in the round. The company plans to use the funding for international expansion.

MARKETS & INVESTING
Banks' profitability strained by low rates; Analysis: Capital markets

By Thomas Hale and Richard Blackden 
805 words
2 August 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Stress test results highlight competing interests between holders of bonds and shares

Amid the drama of the European Banking Authority's stress test results on Friday, one character was notably absent. Across 46 pages, there was no mention of negative interest rates.

Unprecedentedly low rates have been one of several factors fuelling a collapse in bank shares so far this year. The Euro Stoxx banking index has already lost 31 per cent of its value.

"What they don't test for is the real issue around banks - negative interest rates and the questions around profitability," says Paul Markham, who manages 4bn of global equities at Newton Investment Management.

The thinking is that low rates depress the net interest margin - the difference between the rate at which a bank borrows and lends - and portend weak growth expectations over coming years, which is bad news for bank equity investors.

The EBA tests are designed to focus on the banking system's resilience to "adverse economic shocks".

What they demonstrated is that, despite concerns over certain banks, capital - a measure of balance sheet strength - has improved markedly over recent years. The EBA's measure for transitional core equity tier 1 ratios stood at 13.2 per cent on average at the end of 2015, up from 11.1 at the end of 2013.

What emerges is a bipartite view of the European banking system. While shareholders gnash their teeth at the prospect of weaker growth and low rates bondholders are buoyed by the prospect of more capital, which insulates them from the threat of losses in a downturn.

"Weak profitability per se for us as bondholders isn't bad necessarily, as long as the banks have decent capital buffers and are managing their risks on the balance sheet," says Steve Hussey, head of Financial Institutions Credit Research at AllianceBernstein.

"It's whether, number one, you believe the numbers, and whether certain banks have outsized potential risks or losses that might come through."

"Equity investors are focused on dividends," he adds. "CEOs run banks in the short term."

The Euro Stoxx banking index fell more than 2 per cent yesterday after markets responded to the stress tests.

The worst stress test performer, Monte dei Paschi, was the sole Italian riser. It announced on Friday a plan to boost capital which does not involve hitting bondholders.

Its debt has fallen in price over recent months over fears of losses but yesterday one of the bank's subordinated bonds rose by over 4 per cent to 83 cents on the euro.

Unlike bondholders, who closely watched the capital buffer protecting them from losses, shareholders often see higher capital requirements as an impediment to their returns.

Mr Markham describes higher capital requirements as one of the "structural headwinds" facing banks alongside compressed interest rates.

James Sym, an equities fund manager at Schroders, also sees low interest rates as a structural problem for banks.

But he also thinks the current market presents buying opportunities for certain high quality names. "I would say the banking sector in Europe is the last explicitly cheap area of the market," he says.

Mr Sym points to the political pressures on extremely low bond yields and interest rates. "I think they're ridiculously low. I think we'll all look back in 20 years' time and think what were we doing? Does the current set of conditions really justify that?"

If negative rates are bad for bank shareholders, it stands to reason that higher rates might help them. But the EBA's adverse scenario entailed a rise in interest rates, albeit among other issues.

"Higher interest rates in the adverse scenario still manage to hit banks because they lead to higher, nonpaying NPLs, and the cost of funding increases," says Alexandre Birry, head of analytics and research at S&P global ratings.

He adds that low rates effectively support asset quality, as part of a kind of "monetary sedation".

Many market participants are psychologically conditioned by the brutal impact of the 2008 financial crisis.

The conundrum that banks now face is a different proposition. It concerns the extent to which weak bank profitability, rooted in a stagnant growth environment, will remain a headache for equity investors alone, or whether it will ultimately weaken capital as well.

The latter case would entail a lingering transition from stagnation to systemic disaster, rather than a sudden, unpredictable shock. For signs of this, bond prices, rather than shares, are the crucial factor to watch.

"The impact of low growth, low interest rate environment, I think that's something that will be looked at more by regulators," says Mr Birry. "It's not a severe hit. It's a gradual erosion in the profitability of banks."

Bitcoin s panopticon problem

Izabella Kaminska 
788 words
2 August 2016
14:43
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

One of the more curious things about bitcoin enthusiasts is their attitude to privacy.

In one sense they re the most privacy-minded people in the world (allegedly). In the other sense they seem happier than most to provide government authenticated ID to relative newcomer businesses   who have little to no track record with respect to abiding by data privacy laws.

What s more, it s odd that these people should offer up all this information for the sole benefit of using or speculating on a virtual currency that, via that ID link alone, allows the same third parties to maintain some of the most intrusive records of day-to-day spending activities within their closed systems, but also beyond.

Take Coinbase as an example. Being something of a centralising liquidity hub, it s fair to say the company, which operates both as an exchange and a wallet provider, probably knows as much if not more than banking institutions about how their customers deploy their balances.

Once you further account for the fact that most of these third-parties operate on a broker-dealer basis, wherein accounts are made up of third party liabilities to customers not actual bitcoin balances (do see the Mt Gox drama for how that can end up), one really has to wonder what exactly the innovation is?

Overall, the average customer needs to give up as much if not more personal data, often by more dubious means (online upload mechanisms, email or the post) to much less experienced organisations. Once in the system, meanwhile, customer transactions can be linked on a much broader and more publicly intrusive level than anything in the standing banking system. Moreover, there are no associated par value or liquidity guarantees for the customer if and when things go wrong.

And that s before accounting for services like "https://www.elliptic.co/" which take pride in describing themselves as..

 &the global standard in blockchain monitoring technology, and is recognised as an established authority on blockchain compliance. In addition to providing data and analytics services to financial institutions and law enforcement agencies around the world, Elliptic advises governments on blockchain regulatory matters.

So, thus far, it seems to fuse the worst of the old system with the worst of a hyper transparent future "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panopticon"system.

Which begs the question once again, where exactly is the innovation?

Bar the whole point of the bitcoin blockchain being an attempt to manufacture a false sense of security among those who would ordinarily never trust the financial sector, it also seems like a play to gobble up more personal data and related transaction history than any banking system would ever manage to unless it was inclined to bank a much broader and more risky clientele.

Mostly, and arguably, it s a giant privacy bait and switch. There are simply no money transmitter institutions of Coinbase s size that can afford to operate in defiance of the law of the land, unless they care to be based in the sort of jurisdictions most other banking institutions won t care to do business with.

Meanwhile, if the cost in the banking system is indeed mostly related to the cost credit checking, due diligence and policing non-compliance, it s worth considering how exactly the likes of Coinbase improve on these processes vis-a-vis traditional institutions? After all, just knowing who your customer is fairly meaningless. What really matters is having the guts to deny them access if they fail to make the grade, blocking or requesting further information about transactions if and when they re suspected to be fraudulent and generally actively monitoring the clientele base for risk   and that usually takes expensive layers of personnel. If bitcoin third parties are doing that properly, it s likely their cost advantage over conventional banks is linked to more to their non-bank  money transmitter  status, than it is to the fact they re operating in bitcoin.

Which begs one last question about Coinbase. Is it an exchange, a money transmitter or actually a plain old deposit-taking institution such as a bank? Judging by "https://twitter.com/brian_armstrong/status/759995925410385920" from Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong on August 1, Coinbase certainly views itself as a deposit taker. If that s truly the case, it might not be that long before authorities insist Coinbase gets itself a proper banking license   in the process losing most of its cost advantage.

And that s generally how you go full loop in the world of financial innovation.

Bitcoin companies come of age; start moaning about unfair playing fields - FT Alphaville

Is it a bank, a money transmitter, or a Silicon Valley shadow financier? No, it s just Paypal! - FT Alphaville

Pro Private Markets
LBO Focus: Private Equity Bets on Managed Care's Comeback

By Amy Or 
1,283 words
2 August 2016
15:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Changes in reimbursements by U.S. government insurance programs have stirred interest among a small group of private-equity investors in the managed-care sector, which refers to health care based on controlling costs and monitoring outcomes.

The government penalizes hospitals that have too many readmissions within 30 days of an inpatient stay and rewards providers that help propel meaningful savings in the system. The Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services has adopted a rating system that evaluates providers and individual physicians on areas such as patient satisfaction and the amount of preventive screening for common ailments such as diabetes and breast cancer. The higher the score, the better physicians and providers are reimbursed.

This shift away from payments based on the amount of services rendered means medical providers take on the risk of patient health being worse than expected, but the upside can be much greater for companies that keep patients healthy.

Ravi Sachdev, a partner with Clayton Dubilier & Rice LLC, said "the profit opportunity [for managed care] can be four to six times as big as it is for fee for service."

These potential gains have tempted some private-equity firms to back physician groups with payment measures linked to performance, be they primary-care providers or specialist doctors. These physician groups contract with payers, whether commercial insurers or the government agencies of Medicare and Medicaid, to manage part or all of a population's care for a predetermined sum per member, per month. The goal is to manage care effectively, so expenses stay below the sum received or even produce a surplus.

Government and industry participants think the incentive model is better for aligning health-care providers' interests with those of patients, as true cost savings occur when patients are healthy and their ailments are caught early so their conditions won't deteriorate to a state where expensive hospitalization is needed.

Boston's Summit Partners in December led a $250 million investment in DuPage Medical Group, a  physician management company of  primary and multispecialty  doctors in the Chicago area that contracts with both commercial and government insurers like Medicare.

Summit Managing Director Darren Black said most of the company's contracts focus on "taking risks on care unrelated to inpatient hospital care, specifically keeping people out of expensive hospitalizations." Other investments in this area include Pamplona Capital Management LLP and Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s bet on physician practice management company Privia Health LLC, made two years ago, and a new CD&R-backed  platform called agilon health, which provides primary-care physician  management services and technology-based clinical support to enable doctors to take risks.

Managed-Care Reboot

The notion of managed care isn't new. Managed care organizations started in the 1980s as a way to help employers combat rising health-care costs. But such programs got a bad reputation in the late 1980s and early 1990s when fierce competition drove health plans to compete on price and cost savings, sometimes by limiting plan members on the services they could get and the physicians they could visit.

Grievances among the insured led to the rise of preferred provider organizations, which gave patients the flexibility to see wide networks of physicians at the cost of higher premiums.

As the economic pendulum swung back to tougher times after the financial crisis of 2008, keeping tabs on costs through care management came back into vogue. But some investors say managed care is here to stay this time around.

"We are in fact nested inside of a macro trend  that is  promote d,  pushed and  advocated for  by the government, the largest payer in the system," said Ronald Williams, the former chairman and chief executive of insurer Aetna Inc., who is chairman of CD&R's Agilon Health.

Jeremy Gelber, a partner with Pamplona Capital, said not only are government and commercial payers showing greater dedication to incorporating  risks in the equation of care reimbursement, physicians and provider groups also are better equipped to take risks.

"There also are better care-coordination tools, better data capabilities and better ability to engage with patients in cost appropriate settings," he said. "The actual risk models are smarter as it's not just about utilization management, but about taking patients who are really sick and reducing the costs by giving them care at lower cost settings, keeping them out of the emergency room and adhering to medicine."

Managed-Care Risks Remain

Still, managing health-care costs isn't a walk in the park. It involves complicated math based on a set of assumptions of a population's demographic characteristics and their medical needs. Insurance companies, armed with teams of actuaries, are best at making such assessments, but not physicians who have for decades seen patients as they come.

Michael Loiacano, who heads the health-care services practice at executive search firm Heidrick & Struggles International Inc., said physician groups need professionals with technology and data analytics knowledge to help calculate risks, as well as those with customer service experience to help recruit and take care of patients.

"The investors need to hire  the  right talent  for their portfolio companies, but there is just  not  enough talent  out there to do that ," he said.

Scott Bingham, a co-practice leader of U.S. transaction advisory services at advisory firm FTI Consulting Inc., said it is not uncommon for risk-taking physician groups to suffer losses for the first couple of years "as they don't know the ultimate costs of care or they haven't fully built out a closed network" with the clinical scale and capability to manage their members' needs.

And losses can be unforeseeable. Mr. Bingham said he had seen "profits for managed Medicaid contracts get wiped out with a very bad cold and flu season."

To patients and payers, the biggest risk, however, is compliance.

A private-equity executive who declined to be named said that over the years, some operators defrauded government programs on the amount of services they have rendered or falsely represented how sick their patients were in a bid to get more funding.

"It's a known risk," he said. "The Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services can't chase after every single doctor, but it's been well publicized that the agency has been taking a series of actions to crack down on such frauds."

The Medicare Fraud Strike Force, a unit spanning multiple departments including the Office of Inspector General, the Department of Justice and the Federal Bureau of Investigation, among other government agencies, said last month that nearly 2,900 defendants have been charged for having defrauded the Medicare program for more than $10 billion since the unit began its work in 2007.

It is still the early days in the revival of interest in care management, but already there are companies that have called it quits. Privately owned Health Alliance Medical Plans, for one, said last month it would discontinue its Medicaid managed-care contracts next year, as "financial losses from the Medicaid managed-care business are not sustainable." The company declined to comment further.

Private-equity executives said while the macroeconomic environment favors the operation and expansion of risk-taking provider groups, whether investors will make money from such investments boils down to how well their portfolio companies are run.

"Risks go both ways ," said Pamplona's Mr. Gelber. "If you can reduce the costs of care, you have an upside to it; but if you can't manage the costs, you are going to take a hit.

Write to Amy Or at "mailto:amy.or@wsj.com"

Pro Private Markets
VC Profile: FirstMark Capital Grows Along With New York's Venture-Capital Market

By Tomio Geron 
853 words
2 August 2016
15:16
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

When Rick Heitzmann helped launch FirstMark Capital in 2008 to focus on New York startups, many people told him it wouldn't work.

The New York venture-capital ecosystem was much smaller then, with a few other investors such as Union Square Ventures. Now there are many venture firms and incubators in New York and a startup industry that is the second largest after the San Francisco Bay Area.

"When we started in 2008, people said they aren't sure New York is big enough a market to start a VC firm," Mr. Heitzmann said. "That's changed over the past eight years, and it's gotten to the point where it's sustainable and building on itself."

FirstMark Capital

The Firm

The New York firm, which invests in technology startups, has more than $1.5 billion in assets under management.

The Fund

A $275 million early-stage fund to invest in early-stage technology and a $205 million growth-stage fund.

The Strategy

The fund makes 50% of its deals in New York, while also looking for companies throughout the U.S.

FirstMark was formed in 2008 by the technology team of Pequot Ventures, the former venture arm of hedge fund Pequot Capital Management Inc. that has since closed. FirstMark continued the investing it had done prior to the spinout, but expanded its focus on tech startups in the New York area. This followed the spinout of Pequot Ventures' health-care practice as Longitude Capital in 2006.

FirstMark, which has invested in companies such as Pinterest Inc. and Shopify Inc., in June raised $480 million for two new venture-capital funds: $275 million for a fourth early-stage fund and $205 million for a second opportunity fund to invest in growth-stage deals.

Venture capitalists invested $8.5 billion in New York City startups in 2015 and $5.9 billion in 2014, up from $2.4 billion in 2008 and $1.8 billion in 2009, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co. Investors pulled back in the first half of 2016 in New York as they did industry-wide, investing $3.8 billion.

The New York startup industry has also been buoyed by exits such as online handmade-goods marketplace Etsy Inc., which went public in 2015, and social site Tumblr Inc., which was acquired by Yahoo Inc. for $1.1 billion in 2013, as well as more recent large private companies such as WeWork Management LLC, Kickstarter, BuzzFeed Inc. and ZocDoc Inc. Large West Coast companies such as Google Inc., Facebook Inc., Microsoft Corp. and others also have opened significant offices in New York.

FirstMark's growth fund will invest at the Series B, C or D stages in startups backed from its own early-stage funds as well as in companies outside its funds that have broken out and shown that they are winners, Mr. Heitzmann said.

Some companies from the firm's first opportunity fund include Pinterest, Airbnb Inc., InVision Inc. and Tapad Inc., which agreed to be acquired by Telenor for $360 million in February.

FirstMark, one of the larger venture-capital firms in New York, is bullish on local startups, having made about 50% of its investments there, while also sealing 25% of its deals in the San Francisco Bay Area.

"The New York angle actually worked for us. I liked their angle and they've had some winners," said a limited partner in FirstMark who declined to be named because he wasn't allowed to speak publicly for his firm. "Ultimately, I was able to sell it [to my partners], and it has worked out quite well."

FirstMark is probably best known for its early-stage investment in Pinterest, which was valued at $11 billion last year. FirstMark first invested in Pinterest in a 2010 angel round after investing in the founders' previous app Tote. The firm has also invested in Riot Games Inc., which was acquired by Tencent Holdings Ltd.; x.ai; Lumos Labs Inc., which does business as Lumosity; Knewton Inc.; and HyperScience.

The firm prefers to invest in serial entrepreneurs as well as people it has worked with before or known for a number of years. Areas of focus include big data, artificial intelligence and marketplaces, as well as social, enterprise, software as a service, health care and education.

FirstMark also has played a notable role in building today's sizable New York tech community with its monthly events on big data, design and other topics, as well as services for its companies, including recruiting and customer service.

The firm's managing directors are Mr. Heitzmann, Amish Jani and Matt Turck. Two partners in the firm's last fund, FirstMark co-founders Lawrence Lenihan and Gerald Poch, aren't in the new fund. The firm doesn't have immediate plans to add new partners, but eventually would do so if the right candidate is available, Mr. Heitzmann said.

Write to Tomio Geron at "mailto:tomio.geron@wsj.com"

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup; A collection of private-equity fundraising stories from WSJ Pro Private Equity and WSJ Pro Venture Capital

5,920 words
2 August 2016
15:15
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. & CANADA

BUYOUTS

Amulet Capital Partners LP, Greenwich, Conn.

Amulet Capital Partners LP raised at least $145 million for its debut fund, which includes the firm's general partner commitment, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm is seeking $250 million for Amulet Capital Fund I LP, according to the filing. The midmarket firm, which invests $25 million to $150 million of equity per deal, focuses exclusively on health-care investments in both private and public companies. Amulet seeks equity investments of $25 million to $150 million in both public and private companies.

Arbor Private Investment Co., Chicago

Arbor Private Investment Co., a midmarket firm focused exclusively on food and beverage deals, closed its fourth private-equity fund, Arbor Investments IV LP, at $765 million. The fund was targeting $600 million, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing a person familiar with the offering. Investors in the fund include Danish pension system Unipension, insurer Ascension Health Alliance and asset managers DuPont Capital Management and Pantheon Ventures. The fund's predecessor closed on $400 million in 2011. The firm also closed a debut subordinated-debt fund, Arbor Debt Opportunities Fund I LP, with $125 million to invest alongside Arbor's buyout funds.

Brookfield Asset Management Inc., Toronto

Publicly traded Brookfield Asset Management Inc. closed Brookfield Infrastructure Fund III, a global infrastructure vehicle, with $14 billion of equity commitments. The fund, to which Brookfield committed $4 billion, was targeting $10 billion. Its predecessor raised $7 billion in 2013. The vehicle so far has committed to invest more than $3 billion in assets, including a portfolio of U.S. hydroelectric facilities; a portfolio of Brazilian electricity transmission projects; a Colombian power-generation company; a portfolio of Peruvian toll roads; and a U.S. water infrastructure project developer. In the past 18 months, Brookfield raised about $27 billion across its flagship private-fund strategies, including the close of its real-estate fund, Brookfield Strategic Real Estate Partners II, at $9 billion; and its private-equity fund, Brookfield Capital Partners IV, at $4 billion.

CenterGate Capital, Austin, Texas

Lower midmarket firm CenterGate Capital collected at least $156.5 million so far for CenterGate Capital Partners I LP, putting the firm's debut fund more than halfway to a $300 million goal, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund has a $350 million hard cap, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. One person familiar with the offering said the fund has additional investor interest that should take it to the $200 million mark within the next month or so. Investors in the fund include Los Angeles City Employees' Retirement System and Los Angeles Fire and Police Pensions. CenterGate itself pledged $15 million to the fund as its general-partner commitment, said the person familiar with the offering. The firm targets investments in companies with annual revenue of $20 million to $250 million and up to $20 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Invision Capital, Chicago

Invision Capital collected at least $29.9 million so far for Invision Capital II LP, which is seeking $50 million, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Invision invests in manufacturing, distribution and services businesses. The firm invests $2 million to $10 million per deal in companies with annual revenue of $5 million to $100 million. The firm launched its first fund in 2009 with a $50 million target, according to an SEC filing, although it isn't clear how much Invision ultimately gathered.

Leerink Capital Partners LLC, Boston

Leerink Capital Partners LLC, the asset management arm of health care-focused investment bank Leerink Partners LLC, raised at least $71.3 million for Leerink Transformation Fund I LP, which is seeking $250 million. Leerink Capital Partners established Leerink Transformation Partners LLC, which makes growth-equity investments in companies within the health-care technology and services sectors, in 2015.

Live Oak Resources Partners LLC, Houston

Live Oak Resource Partners LLC closed its debut royalty and mineral fund, which will seek to acquire royalty and mineral rights with a focus on natural-gas plays. Live Oak Resource Partners I LP was backed entirely by family offices, said Andrew Keene, the firm's president and chief financial officer. Mr. Keene declined to comment on the size of the fund. Live Oak is managed by Mr. Keene, along with Brandon Meyers, vice president of land and business development at the firm.

Morgan Stanley, New York

Morgan Stanley gathered at least $1.08 billion so far for North Haven Capital Partners VI LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Morgan Stanley rebranded its private-equity, real-assets and credit funds under the North Haven name last year in response to the Dodd-Frank Act's Volcker rule, which prohibits banks from including their own names in the names for such funds.

NextGen Growth Partners, Chicago

NextGen Growth Partners held a first close on about $22.3 million for NextGen Growth Partners Fund I LP, which is targeting $75 million, said Brian O'Connor, the firm's founder and managing partner. The firm expects to hold a final close for the fund by the end of this year or early 2017, Mr. O'Connor said. The fund, whose investors include family offices, high-net-worth individuals and institutions, will make equity investments of $5 million to $10 million in service-oriented businesses, Mr. O'Connor said. The firm targets U.S. companies with $5 million to $50 million in annual revenue and $1 million to $5 million in annual cash flow.

Oak Hill Capital Partners, New York

Oak Hill Capital Partners collected at least $1.27 billion so far for its fourth fund, according to various filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm also lowered the vehicle's offering amount to $2 billion from a $3 billion goal indicated in a separate filing from August 2015. The firm raised $3.8 billion for its third buyout fund in 2009. Although the filings don't specify how much the firm itself has pledged to Oak Hill Capital Partners IV, financier Robert M. Bass and Oak Hill's team historically have been the biggest limited partners in each of the firm's funds. Oak Hill invests $100 million to $300 million of equity in North American companies across four core areas: retail, consumer and distribution; industrials; media and communications; and services.

OpenGate Capital, Los Angeles

OpenGate Capital, a specialist in lower midmarket corporate carve-out transactions, raised its first institutional fund after more than a decade of investing its own capital in dozens of private-equity deals. The firm closed OpenGate Capital Partners I LP at $305 million, above the vehicle's $300 million target. OpenGate targets companies with $50 million to $1 billion in annual revenue in North America, South America and Europe. The firm has so far closed four investments from the fund, and a fifth investment should close later this year, after which the fund will be 43% invested, said Andrew Nikou, who founded the firm in 2005. Although the firm's fund received investments from large, international institutional investors, OpenGate remains "one of the largest [limited partners] in the fund," Mr. Nikou said.

Pelican Energy Partners LP, Houston

Pelican Energy Partners LP gathered $210 million for its second fund, which will back energy-services and equipment companies. The fund, Pelican Energy Partners II LP, is a step up from the firm's first vehicle, which closed on $120 million in 2013. A Securities and Exchange Commission document listed a $225 million offering amount for the latest fund. Limited partners include public pension funds, endowments, consultants and individuals. The firm makes buyout and growth investments in North American businesses with $1 million to $10 million in annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Renovus Capital Partners, Wynnewood, Pa.

Renovus Capital Partners, a firm focused on the education and training industries, expects to close on $150 million in private-capital commitments for a sophomore fund that can also access up to $150 million in leverage from the U.S. Small Business Administration, said a person familiar with the fundraising process. Renovus collected at least $132.2 million in private commitments for Renovus Capital Partners II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund was targeting $100 million. Renovus's previous fund, which also included SBA leverage, wrapped up with $185 million in 2012. The firm can provide equity and mezzanine debt for control-oriented buyout transactions and minority investments. Renovus invests $10 million to $30 million in companies with $2 million to $10 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Spindletop Capital, Austin, Texas

Health-care investor Spindletop Capital is seeking $200 million for Spindletop Healthcare Growth Capital II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm in 2011 collected at least $50.5 million for the fund's predecessor, which was seeking $150 million, according to an SEC filing. It isn't clear how much the fund ultimately raised. Spindletop provides capital for commercial-stage health-care companies in the U.S. The firm invests within the health-care services, biopharmaceutical, health-care technology and medical-device sectors.

Staley Capital Management LLC, Wellesley, Mass.

Staley Capital Management LLC is aiming to raise $125 million for Staley Capital Fund II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Staley invests in software companies and technology-focused providers of business services, generally deploying $5 million to $19 million at a time for growth-equity and buyout investments. The firm gathered at least $20.9 million for the fund's predecessor in 2011, according to SEC filings. It isn't clear how much the vehicle raised and when it ultimately closed.

TorQuest Partners, Toronto

TorQuest Partners said it closed TorQuest Partners Fund IV at a hard cap of 925 million Canadian dollars ($708.2 million), surpassing a C$750 million target. The latest closing includes Torquest Partners IV LP and Torquest Partners Fund (U.S.) IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm's previous fund gathered C$535 million in 2013. TorQuest invests primarily in North American businesses in the consumer-products, financial-services, industrials and chemicals sectors.

Trivest Partners, Coral Gables, Fla.

Trivest Partners said it held a first and final close for Trivest Growth Investment Fund LP with $225 million in total commitments, more than double its $100 million target. The fund will target growth and noncontrol investments in founder-owned businesses. Investors include endowments, pensions, insurance companies, funds of funds, family offices and individuals. The firm's most recent buyout fund, which targets control investments, closed in 2012 with $415 million. Trivest targets U.S. and Canadian businesses with $20 million or more in revenue and $4 million or more in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

DISTRESSED/TURNAROUND

Crown Capital Partners Inc., Calgary, Alberta

Crown Capital Partners Inc. completed an additional 25 million Canadian dollar ($19.3 million) closing for Crown Capital Fund IV LP, bringing the total capital committed to the firm's fourth special-situations debt fund to C$125 million. Crown Capital, which closed Fund IV in September with initial capital commitments of C$100 million, is seeking C$300 million for the fund. It said it expects to have additional closings through 2018 as financing opportunities are identified. Crown Capital offers special-situations financing for recapitalizations, growth financings, acquisitions and management buyouts. The publicly traded firm provides capital to Canadian and select U.S. companies that are unwilling or unable to obtain financing from banks and private-equity firms.

H.I.G. Bayside Capital, Miami

H.I.G. Bayside Capital, a credit-oriented investment affiliate of H.I.G. Capital LLC, held a final close for H.I.G. Bayside Loan Opportunity Fund IV LP with $1.1 billion in capital commitments, exceeding a $1 billion target for the vehicle. The fund, which will mostly invest in noncontrol loan obligations of stressed and distressed U.S. companies, is backed by investors including the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement System, Maine Public Employees Retirement System and Texas Municipal Retirement System, according to pension documents. H.I.G. Bayside in 2013 raised more than $1 billion for H.I.G. Bayside Loan Opportunity Fund III (Europe), which invests primarily in debt obligations of small and midsize European companies.

Origami Capital Partners, Chicago

Origami Capital Partners closed its third fund at $371 million. Origami Opportunities Fund III LP was seeking $450 million, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Among the fund's investors were endowments, foundations, pensions, multimanager funds and family offices. The firm finds assets with value that is inaccessible because of legal, structural, ownership or valuation factors, which the firm seeks to address by restructuring, litigation or active management. Examples of typical investments include hedge-fund positions, private-equity fund restructurings, illiquid assets owned by funds and nonperforming loans. The firm invests $10 million to $50 million of equity primarily in North America and Europe.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

Industry Ventures LLC, San Francisco

Industry Ventures LLC raised $410 million for a pair of funds that will back small venture funds and the companies they support. Industry Ventures Partnership Holdings IV LP is a $209 million fund of funds that, like its predecessors, will invest directly in companies backed by funds in its portfolio. The firm also raised $201 million for Industry Ventures Direct LP to give it more firepower. The firm's partnership-holdings funds are restricted from investing more than 20% of committed capital into companies, according to Managing Director Roland Reynolds. With its new co-investment vehicle, the firm will look to make initial investments of up to about $7 million between the two funds. The direct fund won't make investments independent of the main fund, Mr. Reynolds said. The firm's previous partnership fund raised $170 million in 2014.

Audax Group raised $1.2 billion for its fourth mezzanine fund, surpassing the fund's $1 billion target. The final tally for Audax Mezzanine Fund IV came in ahead of the $1 billion Audax raised for its third mezzanine fund in 2011. Investors in the fund include Alaska Permanent Fund Corp. and the Minnesota State Board of Investment. Audax plans to invest the fund in junior-debt securities in addition to co-investments in equity securities, with a specific focus on direct lending to U.S. midmarket companies as part of private equity-sponsored buyouts, recapitalizations, financings and acquisitions. Audax, which generally invests up to $100 million at a time for mezzanine transactions, raised its first mezzanine fund back in 2002 with $440 million in commitments.

TriGuard Management LLC, Irvine, Calif.

TriGuard Management LLC said it collected more than $660 million for Montauk TriGuard Fund VII LP, a secondary fund that was targeting $600 million. The firm focuses on niche investment opportunities in the secondary market, including the purchase of stakes in other secondary funds or the purchase of older tail-end assets from secondary funds. The firm will also back what it calls "carve-out" opportunities, purchasing niche assets in larger secondary transactions led by other buyers or acquiring slices of secondary portfolios from other buyers that may face concentration limits in their own funds. Investors in the fund include Ohio Police and Fire Pension Fund and the Oklahoma State Regents for Higher Education. The firm raised $505 million for its previous secondary fund in 2014.

This new fund is $50 million smaller than Bain Capital Ventures' last early-stage fund because the firm still has plenty of capital from its $200 million co-investment fund, said Managing Director Mike Krupka. The firm matched the largest limited partner commitment in the new fund, Mr. Krupka said. Bain Capital Ventures uses the co-investment fund to invest alongside its main fund in larger deals.

Early-stage investor Bee Partners LLC closed its second fund, Bee II, with $30 million, beating a $25 million target. The fund is more than four times the size of its predecessor, Bee Partners I, according to the firm. Bee II has taken stakes in 17 companies, nearly half of which have ties to the University of California, Berkeley. Bee Partners invests in about eight companies annually, with investments starting at about $400,000.

Managing Partner Chris Sugden said the firm is a "growth-stage investor," though it frequently invests in companies that haven't previously raised institutional funding. Mr. Sugden said 75% of the firm's investments are in Series A rounds and that the rest are mostly in Series B rounds. The firm focuses on marketing and financial technologies, health-care information technology and enterprise software. Edison has completed eight investments from its latest fund, which counts the New Mexico Educational Retirement Board among its investors. The firm will focus on Series A and some seed investments, she said.

Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Menlo Park, Calif.

Venture-capital firm Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers closed on $1.4 billion across two funds: Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers XVII, a $400 million fund for early-stage investments; and KPCB Digital Growth Fund III, a $1 billion fund for later-stage deals, according to a person familiar with the matter. Growth-equity firm Updata Partners closed on $280 million for Updata Partners V LP. The firm, which beat a $225 million target for its latest fund, closed its last fund with $223 million in 2007. Pro Private Markets
VC Focus: Venture Investment Rises, Deal Pace Slows as Investors Remain Cautious

By Russ Garland 
1,641 words
2 August 2016
15:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

After a first quarter that recorded a striking slowdown in U.S. venture-capital investing, the second quarter was a mixed bag with investment volume increasing but deal pace declining.

Investment volume climbed to $15.78 billion in the second quarter, with Uber Technologies Inc. and Snapchat Inc. accounting for nearly $5 billion of the total, according to VentureSource, a data provider owned by Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co. Uber raised $3.5 billion and Snapchat hauled in $1.27 billion during the second quarter.

Not counting those two deals, venture financing during the quarter came in well below the first quarter's $13.18 billion. Even including them, total investment fell by 20% from the second quarter of 2015, when U.S. venture-backed companies raised $19.61 billion.

Investment in the first half of this year totaled $28.96 billion, compared with $36.74 billion in the first six months of 2015.

Deal activity during the second quarter reflects a more cautious atmosphere than that of previous investment periods, with 845 rounds of financing during the quarter, an 8% decline from the prior quarter and a 25% drop from the same period last year. Deal flow in the second quarter was the slowest since the third quarter of 2010, when there were 744 funding deals.

"Capital is patient and waiting for entrepreneurs' valuation expectations to come back down to where the market is now," said Ian Sigalow, a co-founder and partner at Greycroft Partners. "Also people are shopping for bargains and adding reserves for companies where VCs overpaid."

Valuations have cooled, according to VentureSource. The median valuation in the first half of this year stood at $20 million, a little below the $21.25 million median for 2013 but markedly lower than last year's $55 million mark.

Here is a look at what happened in various sectors during the past quarter.

Health Care

Sliding public-market valuations for biotechnology companies have reverberated through private markets, as investments in health-care startups sharply declined during the first half of this year.

Health-care venture investment dropped by 23% to $6.34 billion compared with the first six months of last year, which recorded $8.24 billion for the sector, industry data show.

Biotech brings in more venture funding than any other medical sector and is one of the leading industries for venture-capital investment. In recent years, venture investment totals for biotech have markedly heated up amid a booming initial public offering market, which helped pull crossover investors into private financings of drug startups.

Last year, biotech crossover investors helped drive up overall health-care venture investment to $8.24 billion in the first half. Since then, however, biotech IPOs have cooled and medical venture investment totals have declined.

Venture firms invested $2.96 billion in 182 health-care financing deals last quarter, a 30% drop from the $4.23 billion spread across 204 rounds in the year-ago period. Last quarter's total was the lowest health-care quarterly sum since the $2.45 billion wagered in the third quarter of 2014.

Investment dropped in every medical sector last quarter compared with the year-ago period, but biotech saw the steepest decline. The $1.6 billion put into 69 biotech rounds was down 34% from the $2.34 billion pumped into 80 deals during the same period last year.

Information Technology

Investments in information-technology companies fell during the second quarter to the lowest level in nearly two years, as expectations of venture capitalists and entrepreneurs continued to diverge.

Companies raised $2.39 billion, about 35% below the first quarter's $3.65 billion and nearly the same drop compared with the year-ago quarter, when funding was $3.64 billion.

The number of deals, at 238, was also down. They've dropped by 11% from 267 deals since the first quarter and by 20% year over year.

Information technology has become a world of haves and have-nots: Some companies are raising capital without much difficulty while others are trying to get acquired, raising down rounds or going out of business.

Cybersecurity company Cylance Inc., for instance, which uses machine learning to detect malware and block threats, raised $100 million in June at a valuation of $1.1 billion, more than triple the valuation it had a year ago.

But Keen Labs Inc., which lets customers develop analytics tools, and Qumulo Inc., which makes analytics for network-attached storage, both raised down rounds, as did employer review site Glassdoor Inc.

Financial Technology

Investment in financial technology this year is shaping up quite differently from last year, when online lenders dominated a banner year for fundraising deals.

Overall, 51 financial institutions and services companies raised $1.03 billion in the second quarter, improving on the first quarter, when fintech companies sealed 46 deals totaling $783 million, but still lagging behind 2015's performance, when 59 deals raised $1.19 billion in the second quarter.

Large deals announced in the second quarter include $160 million for health-insurance startup Clover Health, $100 million for online lender Affirm Inc., $75 million for financial adviser Personal Capital Advisors Corp., and $60 million for blockchain-payments company Circle Internet Financial Ltd.

Online lending startups, which hauled in unprecedented capital infusions at high valuations last year, have lost much of the favor in the market, causing widespread pain, as evidenced by layoffs, loan volume decreases and shutdowns.

For the first time since 2014, lending startups took a back seat to payments in terms of venture fundraising, even as the insurance sector gained ground.

"The market was too quick to award these ultrapremium valuations to alternative lenders, some of whom were building not terribly differentiated businesses," said Rob Stavis, a partner at Bessemer Venture Partners. Now, he said, the market is acting "as if the whole thing is over," with the sentiment pendulum swinging back in the opposite direction.

Consumer Services

Billion-dollar deals for Snapchat and Uber boosted overall funding for consumer-services startups in the second quarter, although the sector saw a decline in financing rounds.

Venture capitalists made 155 investments in the category, its lowest number of deals since the third quarter of 2010.

"We're definitely seeing a slight slowdown in the pace of quality deal flow, and the number of meetings that we're willing to take have come down," said Jim Scheinman, Maven Ventures' founder and managing partner. "There's more companies who were funded in the last year that are coming back for funding, which frankly look more attractive to us than some of the newer startups that obviously have a lot more risk."

Startups in consumer services raised $6.52 billion, more than three times what the same group received in the first quarter of this year, according to VentureSource. Excluding the megarounds in Uber and Snapchat, though, the category's funding was at its lowest level since 2013.

Retail

Venture funding rebounded in the second quarter, but the bounce back wasn't enough to revive a sluggish retail sector. Retail-focused startups had their slowest first half in terms of total funding since 2010.

Venture capitalists invested more than $250 million in retail startups in the second quarter. That represented a nearly threefold rise over the sector's first-quarter funding largely because of a $111 million round for members-only organic-products startup Thrive Market Inc. The number of retail-focused deals fell to 13 during the second quarter, compared with 21 during the year-ago period.

Investors are pulling back after a few high-profile failures in recent months became symbols of a risky, crowded sector. Members-only sale site Gilt Groupe Inc., once valued at $1.1 billion, sold for $250 million in January, not even fetching the amount it had raised from investors. In June, flash-sale website One Kings Lane Inc., valued at about $900 million in 2014, was acquired for an undisclosed price that buyer Bed Bath & Beyond Inc. termed "not material."

"The poster children have made it very difficult for investors to say,  I'm going to put another $100 million into a retail play,' " said Tim Wilson, a partner at Artiman Ventures.

Food

Venture capitalists' appetite for food startups dropped in the second quarter, particularly at the late stage, after a ravenous second half of 2015.

Investment for food entrepreneurs totaled $49.8 million across 10 deals, down from $69.9 million for 20 deals during the first quarter and $99.6 million for 11 deals during the year-ago period.

By contrast, the sector took in $275.2 million from 15 deals during the third quarter of 2015 and $192.3 million from 14 deals during the fourth quarter.

Still, investors in the category this year maintained a deal pace on par with last year, albeit with smaller sums of money per investment. All deals cemented during the second quarter of this year were seed, Series A or Series B rounds. Moreover, the average deal size in the first half of this year was $4 million, compared with nearly $8 million in the first half of 2015.

"Volume-wise there's still considerable deal flow," said Victor Friedberg, co-founder and managing director at S2G Ventures, which focuses on food and agriculture. "In terms of total capital, it could be down because it's just smaller checks."

"Investors are looking for revenue and gross-margin performance," said James Joaquin, co-founder of Obvious Ventures. "In the early stage, where we do most of our work, it's very vibrant."

With reporting from Yuliya Chernova, Deborah Gage, Tomio Geron, Brian Gormley, Patience Haggin and Cat Zakrzewski

Write to Russ Garland at "mailto:russell.garland@wsj.com"

Kroger's 84.51 and Market6 Merge

85 words
2 August 2016
01:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A subsidiary of Kroger Co., 84.51, and venture-backed Market6 Inc. have merged.

Markets
Hunt for Returns Reaches Pakistan; Since March, more than $67 billion has poured into a group of 30 emerging markets

By Mia Lamar And Carolyn Cui 
1,013 words
1 August 2016
07:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Ross Teverson, head of emerging-market strategy at $49 billion money manager Jupiter Asset Management, has a new star pick: Pakistan.

The country wasn't even considered an emerging market when he decided to invest. Until earlier this summer, it was a rung lower a "frontier" market, where stocks are notoriously hard to trade and the political climate is tumultuous. And yet, the benchmark KSE-100 stock index is up 20% in dollar terms this year, Exhibit A in how far global investors are willing to go for returns these days.

Sluggish growth in developed economies has prompted central banks to push interest rates down to zero and beyond, wiping out yields on government debt and sending investors into all sorts of fringe or previously unloved markets such as Pakistan's.

By estimates, since March more than $67 billion has poured into a group of 30 emerging markets tracked by the Institute of International Finance. That number doesn't include money heading to markets such as China and Russia, where information on foreign buying is limited.

"Investors have had to go further down the risk spectrum," said Supriya Menon, senior multiasset strategist at $153 billion money manager Pictet Asset Management.

Alex Muromcew, who manages the TIAA Emerging Markets Equity Fund, has been increasing his fund's exposure to Peru, a fringe investment even among emerging-market specialists. The country has attracted attention with a new president who is seen as market friendly. Stocks there are up 56% this year in dollar terms.

Bond prices in Ecuador have rallied 27% this year, despite an energy-price slump that has cut revenue for the oil exporter. Hungary's stock market is up 16%, beating most European bourses, as investors bet that falling inflation will allow the central bank to ease monetary policy.

Interest is even high in junk-rated Sri Lanka, which reached a deal with the International Monetary Fund in April to stave off a financial crisis. Foreigners have boosted their holdings of Sri Lanka's local-government debt for six straight weeks, according to HSBC Holdings PLC.

Over a single week in July, foreign investors poured more than $5.5 billion into Indonesia, India and five other major emerging stock and bond markets tracked by the Institute of International Finance, the biggest one-week influx of cash in 15 months. Emerging-market debt funds drew a record $14 billion in the four weeks ended July 27, according to fund tracker EPFR Global.

There are limits to how much money these markets can absorb, and analysts warn that investors have probably underestimated the risks. Many emerging economies still rely heavily on commodity exports, making them vulnerable to continued weakness in global growth. Some investors might find it hard to find the exits if markets take a tumultuous turn.

"They might find liquidity to be an issue in emerging markets when there is a need to sell," said Sean Ryan, senior analyst at MPI, a global provider of quantitative research and risk analytics.

Emerging-market stocks and local-currency bonds sustained losses for three consecutive years between 2013 and 2015, as investors pulled out billions of dollars. The MSCI EM index fell 25% over that period, compared with a 52.6% gain for the S&P 500 index. The J.P. Morgan Global Bond Index Emerging Markets Diversified lost 27%, while U.S. Treasury bonds rose 3.4%.

Peru's $109 billion stock market is among the most volatile in all emerging markets. During the past three years, the market lost nearly half of its value, according to MSCI Inc.

Bond investors also have to wrestle with deteriorating credit quality. There have been 19 defaults in emerging countries this year, up from 15 at the same time last year, according to S&P Global Ratings. Nearly one-third of all emerging-market issuers are at the risk of being downgraded.

But an easing of concerns about China's economic slowdown, along with a rebound for commodity prices from their lows early this year, has helped encourage the return to riskier markets. Analysts and investors widely say they expect inflows to continue.

"Emerging markets have been broadly out of favor with global investors for the better part of four years," said Mr. Teverson, whose investment in Pakistan helped his fund gain 17% this year through June. "When you have seen an asset class out of favor for so long and you see valuations so low, that inflow can be sustained for a long period."

Asia, long snubbed by global investors, has been a big winner. The region has accounted for more than half of the emerging-market influx since March, according to the Institute of International Finance.

Foreign ownership of local-government bonds in Indonesia where benchmark yields are hovering around 7% has reached almost 40%, a 15-month peak. That comes although S&P Global Ratings stuck by its below-investment-grade or junk rating on the country in June, citing a population that is still relatively poor and a heavily indebted corporate sector.

In late July, Chinese property developer Greenland Hong Kong Holdings issued $450 million in three-year bonds yielding 4.125%, a half-percentage point below where the junk-rated company initially marketed the bonds and just 3.3 percentage points more than three-year U.S. Treasury notes.

Pacific Investment Management Co., the world's biggest bond-fund manager, has done well this year investing in "out of consensus" countries such as Sri Lanka, according to Luke Spajic, who oversees the firm's emerging-market investments in Asia. He warned, however, that risks such as a sharp slide in oil prices could send investors fleeing again.

"Everything could change on a dime," Mr. Spajic said.

Chao Deng contributed to this article.
