How I Was Wrong About ObamaCare

By Bob Kocher 
913 words
1 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

I was wrong. Wrong about an important part of ObamaCare.

When I joined the Obama White House to advise the president on health-care policy as the only physician on the National Economic Council, I was deeply committed to developing the best health-care reform we could to expand coverage, improve quality and bring down costs. We worked for months to pass this landmark legislation, and I still count celebrating the passage of the Affordable Care Act with the president one balmy spring night in 2010 as one of my greatest Washington memories.

What I got wrong about ObamaCare was how the change in the delivery of health care would, and should, happen. I believed then that the consolidation of doctors into larger physician groups was inevitable and desirable under the ACA. I joined my White House health-care colleagues -- Ezekiel Emanuel and Nancy-Ann DeParle -- in writing a medical journal article arguing that "these reforms will unleash forces that favor integration across the continuum of care." We added that "only hospitals or health plans can afford to make the necessary investments" needed to provide the care we will need in a post-ACA world.

Well, the consolidation we predicted has happened: Last year saw 112 hospital mergers (up 18% from 2014). Now I think we were wrong to favor it.

I still believe that organizing medicine into networks that can share information, coordinate care for patients and manage risk is critical for delivering higher-quality care, generating cost savings and improving the experience for patients. What I know now, though, is that having every provider in health care "owned" by a single organization is more likely to be a barrier to better care.

Over the past five years, published research, some of it well summarized on a Harvard Medical School site, has indicated that savings and quality improvement are generated much more often by independent primary-care doctors than by large hospital-centric health systems.

Look at accountable-care organizations (ACOs), in which doctors and health-care providers come together to provide complete care for an individual and are compensated for keeping them healthy and generating savings. Based upon the latest data the Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services has released,from 2014, independent physician-led ACOs, like the Rio Grande ACO on the Texas border, are outperforming ACOs from many of the most famous health systems. Johns Hopkins Hospital in Baltimore has been ranked as one of the top three health systems in the nation, but its ACO failed to achieve shared savings in 2014.

Small, independent practices know their patients better than any large health system ever can. They are going up against the incumbent and thus are driven to innovate. These small businesses can learn faster without holding weeks of committee discussions and without permission from finance, legal and IT departments to make a change.

More often than not, one of the most important changes these practices make is embracing technology. The ability to store, analyze and make sense of data has now become so easy and inexpensive that all physicians can use "big data."

In my White House days, we believed it would take three to five years for physicians to use electronic health records effectively. We were wrong about that too. At every opportunity, organized medicine has asked to delay and lower thresholds for tracking and reporting basic quality measures; yet they have no reason to delay.

In the ACOs run by Aledade, which advises small medical practices (I sit on its board), we have found that independent primary-care doctors are able to change their care models in weeks and rapidly learn how to use data to drive savings and quality. For small practices, it does not take years to root out waste, rewire referrals to providers who charge less but deliver more, and redesign schedules so patients can see their doctors more often to avert emergency-room visits and readmissions.

Recognizing the strength in the small practices, the federal government needs to write rules that make it easier for them to thrive under ObamaCare and don't tip the scales toward consolidation. That means introducing payment models that limit losses for small providers to the Medicare dollars they receive rather than total spending, and which rely on multiyear benchmarks instead of single-year swings. It also means comparing small practices to other small ones -- instead of to large health systems with large balance sheets -- when determining if a practice deserves bonus payments for savings.

Large health systems deliver "personalized" care in the same way that GM can sell you a car with the desired options. Yet personal relationships of the kind often found in smaller practices are the key to the practice of medicine. They are the relationships that doctors want to forge with patients, and vice versa. It may sound old-fashioned, but what I have learned is that we do not need to sacrifice this unique feature of our health-care system as we move forward in adapting new value-based payment models and improving the health of patients.

---

Dr. Kocher was special assistant to President Obama for health care and economic policy from 2009 to 2010. He is now a partner at Venrock, the venture-capital firm.

(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Dr. Kocher's Damascus-Like ACA Conversion" -- WSJ Aug. 8, 2016)

Business News: Group Buys Israeli Game Business

447 words
1 August 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Corrections & Amplifications

The Chinese consortium buying some subsidiaries of Caesars Interactive Entertainment, an Israeli games business, consists of more than a dozen investors. A Business News article Monday about the deal incorrectly said the consortium consists of 11 investors.

(WSJE Aug. 2, 2016)

(END)

SHANGHAI -- A Chinese consortium led by Shanghai Giant Network Technology Co. and joined by a fleet of financial moguls including a private-equity arm set up by Alibaba founder Jack Ma has agreed to purchase an Israeli games business for $4.4 billion in cash.

The consortium of 11 investors -- including Giant Investment Ltd., Yunfeng Capital, a private-equity firm co-founded by Alibaba Group Holding's Mr. Ma, China Oceanwide Holdings Group Co., China Minsheng Trust Co., CDH China HF Holdings Company and Hony Capital Fund -- will purchase a 100% stake in Caesars Interactive Entertainment's subsidiaries, including mobile-games unit Playtika. A statement announcing the deal was released Sunday by Shenzhen-listed Chongqing New Century Cruise Co., a shell company bought by Giant Interactive Group last year.

Caesars Interactive Entertainment is a subsidiary under a joint venture between Caesars Acquisition Co. and Caesars Entertainment Corp. It has developed casino-style games including Caesars Casino, Bingo Blitz and World Series of Poker. The deal doesn't include World Series of Poker and CIE's real-money online gaming business.

The deal, underscoring the Chinese game developer's ambition to expand overseas, is another big Israel-focused move by a Chinese firm. China National Chemical Corp. paid $1.44 billion for a 40% stake in crop-protection producer Adama Agricultural Solutions in July and last year Shanghai Bright Food took over Tnuva, Israel's largest food producer, for more than $2 billion.

Possessing advanced research and development, big data and artificial-intelligence analysis capabilities, Playtika has maintained rapid growth over the past five years, the statement said. Its main business is focused on games such as poker, and it has no presence in China yet. The virtual currency used on the Playtika platforms will remain unexchangeable into real currency, the filing said.

The consortium boosted capital in Alpha Frontier Ltd. in its purchase of CIE's Playtika, said the statement. Playtika will continue to run independently with its headquarters remaining in Herzliya, while its existing management will oversee daily operations, said the companies.

In October 2015, the formerly U.S.-listed Giant Interactive, owned by billionaire Shi Yuzhu, returned to the Shenzhen Stock Exchange via a $2.1 billion reverse merger with Chongqing New Century Cruise Co., which is currently valued at $12.6 billion.

-- Yifan Xie

Huffington Post CEO Joins Board of Ed-Tech Startup Newsela

Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Huffington Post Chief Executive Jared Grusd is joining the board of directors at education-technology company Newsela Inc.

Backed by Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and Mark Zuckerberg's education fund, New York-based Newsela aims to make news articles and other nonfiction content accessible to students at different reading levels. Mr. Grusd has previously worked at several technology companies, including Spotify Technology SA.

Mr. Grusd is also a professor at Columbia Business School and said he is personally passionate about education as a cause.

"The mission of Newsela immediately grabbed me to unlock the written word for everyone," Mr. Grusd said.

Newsela CEO Matthew Gross said the company is bringing on Mr. Grusd because of his experience in the technology industry. The company uses a combination of machine learning and freelance journalists to rewrite articles and historical documents at five different grade levels.

Currently the company uses a "freemium" model, offering its service free to students and teachers but requiring school districts to pay for access to its analytics. Mr. Grusd has experience in using this model from his time at Spotify, which uses a similar model. Though Spotify has yet to turn a profit using this approach, Mr. Grusd said he's confident that Newsela will "crack the code" in the education space.

Newsela has several partnerships with media companies, including The Washington Post. However at this time, it has no plans to do so with Huffington Post. Mr. Grusd said his role at Huffington Post didn't lead him to join the board.

"I spent my career less in media and more at technology companies," he said.

Pro Private Markets
Talend Nudges Dormant Tech IPO Market; Software company's stock surges on only fifth tech debut of 2016

By Deborah Gage 
726 words
29 July 2016
23:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Software company Talend SA had a strong debut on the Nasdaq on Friday in what investors see as a possible boost for the lackluster tech IPO market so far this year.

Talend priced the offering at $18 a share, above the company's estimated range of $15 to $17, and raised $95 million on the deal. Shares opened Friday at $27.66, up nearly 54% from the IPO price, and closed at $25.50.

The IPO, only the fifth tech debut of 2016, provided an encouraging sign as companies have hesitated to risk a poor performance in the public markets even as private capital has become harder to raise.

"While relatively few in number, the strong aftermarket performance of these [recent] offerings is reinforcing the demand," said Brett Paschke, head of Equity Capital Markets at investment banking firm William Blair & Co. "We are seeing many companies making preparations to launch and expect a more active IPO market in the coming months."

Talend makes software that combines data from disparate corporate systems so that companies can use it to make business decisions. It claims more than 1,300 customers, including General Electric Corp.

It attributed the decision to go public to a combination of revenue growth, positive cash flow, and burgeoning demand.

"Part of the reason we were able to take the company public in an environment like this is a combination of high and accelerating growth with cash-flow break even," said Chief Executive Mike Tuchen. "We are sustainably cash-flow positive, and there is an enormous market [of big data and cloud customers] moving our way."

The offering shows that the market for tech IPOs may be reviving after a dormant period.

Line Corp., which makes a mobile messaging app, raised $1.14 billion in July in "http://www.wsj.com/articles/lines-post-ipo-run-defies-host-of-skeptics-1468587242"in New York and Tokyo and was trading Friday nearly 12% above its IPO price of $32.84.

Twilio Inc., which provides telephony services over the internet, closed Friday at $40.63, up from an IPO price of $15.

Talend is somewhat unusual, according to Menlo Ventures Managing Director Matt Murphy, who isn't a Talend investor. It was founded in France, has several European investors and less than $100 million in revenue, which venture investors have considered the bar for companies going public.

"If a company like that can get out and do well, it says a lot about the appetite out there for new issues, which is what everyone is worried about," Mr. Murphy said.

Still, the IPO market has been missing big names like Uber or Airbnb, according to Andreessen Horowitz Managing Partner Scott Kupor.

"We need one of those marquee names to go out," Mr. Kupor said. "Until then, you'll see a slower drumbeat" of smaller companies.

Talend has raised more than $100 million from investors including Balderton Capital, Bpifrance, Idinvest Partners, Iris Capital and Silver Lake Sumeru.

Although it isn't profitable, both revenue and net losses have been moving in the right direction. Its revenue last year was $76 million, up from $62.6 million in 2014. Net loss was $22 million, compared with a $22.5 million loss in 2014.

The market for software that, like Talend's, helps corporate customers analyze large amounts of data has been uneven, as many customers have struggled to understand what they want to do with data and how to do it.

Big data technology is also shifting as more companies move software from their own premises to the cloud.

A couple of Talend's competitors, Tibco Software Inc. and Informatica Corp., "http://www.wsj.com/articles/permira-cppib-poised-to-take-informatica-private-in-5-billion-deal-1428398039" to manage this transition, which requires changing from selling perpetual licenses to cloud software subscriptions, according to IDC Research Director Stewart Bond.

While Talend's cloud business is growing, "almost every company will be hybrid [cloud and on-premises] for at least another decade," Mr. Tuchen said. "We can solve the problem while they're doing it."

About half the market doesn't use specialized software to extract and analyze data, instead doing it by hand, he said. That leaves lots of opportunity for software companies to automate the work, he added.

Marriott and Hilton cut forecasts amid fears of growth peak

By Aimee Keane in New York 
614 words
28 July 2016
13:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Two of the biggest US hotel chains, Marriott International and Hilton Worldwide Holdings, cut their growth forecasts as tightening corporate travel budgets and global economic uncertainty put pressure on hotel bookings.

Some analysts suggest the US hotel industry might be reaching a cyclical peak. Occupancy rates plateaued in the second quarter at 69.4 per cent, up just 0.6 per cent on the same period in 2015, according to STR, a data and analytics group.

During the peak before the financial crisis, occupancy rates hit 63 per cent.

 We re probably in the later innings of this cycle,  said Dan Wasiolek, senior equity analyst at Morningstar.  Revpar [revenue per available room] has probably peaked, but that doesn t mean it won t be positive. 

Lukas Hartwich, a senior analyst at Green Street Advisors, said he expects revenue growth rates to remain low until profits in the energy sector recover and multinationals loosen travel budgets.  The macro backdrop is driving the bus right now,  he said.

"http://www.ft.com/topics/organisations/Hilton_Hotels_Corp" revised its guidance on full year revenue growth on Wednesday, citing a slowdown in corporate bookings, to a range of 2-4 per cent per available room, down from previous estimates of 3-5 per cent.

It also revised down its estimate of adjusted earnings before interest, tax, depreciation and amortisation for the year from $3.02bn-$3.1bn to $2.98bn-$3.04bn.

"http://www.ft.com/topics/organisations/Marriott_International_Inc" followed suit, limiting 2016 revenue growth to roughly 3 per cent per available room, on a constant dollar basis, from earlier estimates of 3-5 per cent.

The company, which excluded the potential impact from its takeover of Starwood, revised down its adjusted ebitda to $1.9bn-$1.97bn from $1.89bn-$1.9bn.

Laura Paugh, global chief investor relations officer at Marriott, said business bookings have had an effect on revpar since the fourth quarter of 2015.  With the economy growing slower than we d like, we re trying to make up for that dearth of business travellers with leisure [travellers] at lower rates,  she said.

Instability in the global economy, including fallout from the Brexit vote, terror events in Europe and the recession in Brazil, have also put pressure on performance.

Starwood Hotels reported second quarter declines in revenue per available room in Latin America and Africa and the Middle East of 5.1 per cent and 9.7 per cent respectively. The W Hotels chain owner reported global revenue growth of 1.4 per cent per available room.

The large hotel operators, which survived the financial crisis by selling off property and becoming primarily hotel managers rather than owners, have been racing to add scale and consolidate.

Marriott s cash-and-stock takeover of Starwood, worth $13.6bn when it was agreed in March, awaits regulatory clearance from China.

Ms Paugh said Marriott has been in a  two horse race  with Hilton to add new units.  If you re a developer and looking to select a brand, Marriott and Hilton are pretty much taking the lion share of new development. What will be interesting is how the Starwood brand plays into that,  she said.

With the closing of the Starwood deal, Marriott is expected to top the list of largest hotels by room size. In the second quarter the group added close to 11,000 net rooms, while Hilton added 10,400.

Marriott shares are up 5.2 per cent this year, slightly trailing behind the Baird/STR hotel index, which is up 5.6 per cent. Hilton, the largest hotel operator by market cap, has gained 8.3 per cent.

Markets
Debt Collectors Want New CFPB Rules to Address Email, Text Messages; Law governing industry was enacted in 1977, lacks provisions for digital communication

By Yuka Hayashi 
391 words
28 July 2016
23:21
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

If a collection agency trying to recoup an outstanding debt doesn't respond to an email or keeps calling without leaving a voice mail, there is a reason for it.

The law governing the debt collection industry was enacted in 1977 and doesn't have provisions for digital communication methods used widely today.

"The law explicitly addresses the use of postcards, collect calls and telegrams but is silent about the use of voice mail, email and text messages," Richard Cordray, director of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau said. "As new forms of technology have emerged, many questions have arisen as to how to apply the law."

That is one of the reasons why Mr. Cordray announced a plan Thursday "http://www.wsj.com/articles/cfpb-unveils-overhaul-of-debt-collection-1469678460" covering the debt collection industry, providing fresh interpretations to the four-decades-old law called the Fair Debt Collection Practices Act, or the FDCPA.

At a public hearing in Sacramento, Calif., where the CFPB unveiled an outline of the rules under consideration, several industry representatives urged the watchdog agency to provide clear guidelines on the use of digital technology, including email and text messaging.

"These new technologies create tremendous opportunities for consumers, debt collectors and debt owners," said Jim Mastriani, president and chief operating officer of Velocity Portfolio Group, a debt collector based in Wall Township, N.J. "However, currently these technologies most often go unused &in favor of traditional telephone and mail communication because of concerns about liability and running afoul of the FDCPA."

Others said many customers much prefer communicating with debt collectors using email or text messages, rather than talking on the phone.

TrueAccord, a San Francisco startup, offers an automated and personalized debt collection service using data, machine learning and behavioral analytics. Ohad Samet, the company's chief executive, said using new technology allows debt collectors to reduce call volume by up to 95%, using methods much less intrusive to consumers.

"We have empirical evidence showing using new technology text, email, social media, digitizing dispute process significantly improves consumer protection, measured by consumer feedback and marked reduction in consumer complaints," Mr. Samet said at the CFPB event.

Venture Deals in Most U.S. Regions Slow to Rebound

By Patience Haggin 
783 words
28 July 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A few megadeals have sent figures for U.S. venture funding back up nationally, but in most regions of the country, that comeback hasn't kicked in yet.

Total venture funding for U.S. startups rebounded nearly 20% from the first quarter, helped by a few billion-dollar deals for tech's biggest names, according to Dow Jones VentureSource. An absence of comparable mega-rounds outside California, coupled with an otherwise rocky investment climate, meant that investment in other regions largely stayed down.

Beyond Silicon Valley and Los Angeles, several parts of the country that rode the funding boom of the past few years have not seen a comeback. The Pacific Northwest, mountain and Southeast regions all saw their weakest quarter in a while.

More on Second-Quarter Deal Flow

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/venture-investment-rises-to-15-78b-1468468862"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/it-fundraising-falls-to-lowest-in-nearly-two-years-1468927807"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/fintech-funding-regains-momentum-1468582201"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/consumer-services-deals-slow-down-1468468861"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/biotech-leads-sharp-decline-in-q2-health-care-venture-investment-1468468863"

Los Angeles-based Snapchat's $1.27 billion round also shook up the usual pecking order. Southern California startups raised $2.46 billion, beating out the mid-Atlantic (typically second only to Northern California) by more than $400 million. The L.A. area's surge even offset a funding decline of more than 75% in the San Diego metropolitan area, where the city's health-care sector took a nose dive.

Among metropolitan areas, Los Angeles and Chicago saw the largest growth spurts versus the first quarter. Total investment more than doubled in those regions.

Investment in the mountain states fell to its lowest number of deals since 2009, and its lowest level by amount invested since 2003.

The region had seen a hot streak in 2014 and 2015, buoyed by the general boom in funding for software and business support services. It attracted a record $810 million in the first quarter of 2015, nearly half of which came from a mega-round that valued Utah data analytics firm Domo at $2 billion. The region's slowdown since the fourth quarter of 2015 has mirrored the national slowdown in funding for business support services.

The mountain region's uptick in 2014 to 2015 may have been more of a string of individual large rounds, rather than a meaningful trend, said Ben Dahl, a partner at Salt Lake City firm Pelion Venture Partners. "Those mega-rounds make some of the aggregate data less helpful, because a lot of the dollars are concentrated in those key deals," Mr. Dahl said.

Funding in the Pacific Northwest was spread more broadly across different industries during its hot streak, but in the second quarter fell in every major sector except biopharmaceuticals. Funding in the Pacific Northwest is down to its lowest point since the first quarter of 2014, both in the amount invested and the number of deals.

Despite the downturn in the Pacific Northwest, Jason Stoffer, a Seattle-based partner at Maveron, said he didn't think investors were losing interest in the region.

"I think what you've seen is a bit of a choppy market for venture funding in general this year," said Jason Stoffer, a Seattle-based partner at Maveron. "I don't think there's a demonstrable difference between Seattle and San Francisco in terms of the sentiment."

Funding in the Southeast (stretching from Virginia to Florida and Arkansas) plummeted more than 60% in the second quarter. The drop is largely owing to the $794 million mega-round that Magic Leap announced in February. When this deal is removed, the region's slowdown actually began in the fourth quarter of 2015, and has now resettled at a level that was normal for the region before its six-quarter hot streak.

The Southeast's hot streak was spread across sectors, and can be read as a series of individual weighty rounds like $300 million for Jacksonville, Fla.-based sports merchandiser Fanatics, $225 million for Charlotte, N.C.-based enterprise payments software AvidXchange, or $62 million for Atlanta-based social media app Yik Yak rather than an wave in any particular sector.

"Venture capital is always a tale of two cities," said Mr. Dahl. "Good companies get funded. And it becomes harder for companies that are more marginal from an investment perspective to get funding." Yet Mr. Dahl said he believed investors would continue to seek out and find promising companies, even outside venture's typical hubs.

"I don't think it's a geographic bias," he said. "It's a quality bias."

Pro Private Markets
FogHorn Systems Raises $12M for Industrial Internet of Things

By Deborah Gage 
453 words
27 July 2016
11:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

The proper name of FogHorn Systems Inc. was incorrectly cited in an earlier version of this article as Fog Systems Inc. 

Corporations are putting a premium on technology that connects industrial sensors to the internet so they can take the pulse of everything from a manufacturing line to an airplane fleet.

One problem, however, is the distance between the data and the computing resources needed to analyze the data, which clogs bandwidth and slows analysis.

FogHorn Systems Inc. is trying to solve this challenge and has raised $12 million from investors whose companies could benefit from its internet-of-things technology.

The investment in the Mountain View, Calif.-based startup includes $2.5 million of converted seed capital and a Series A round led by current investor March Capital and GE Ventures, with Robert Bosch Venture Capital GmbH, Yokogawa Electric Corp . and Darling Ventures participating.

"The cloud is phenomenal for designing products better or predicting when machines fail, but when something is going to break in the next 30 seconds, it's good to be able to spot the anomaly in real time and take action, or trigger an action, like shutting off the machine," said "http://foghorn-systems.com" Chief Executive David King.

Turbines or pumps or jet engines can rapidly generate billions of bytes data, Mr. King said, but streaming it to the cloud to try to make sense of it isn't practical. FogHorn's technology grabs the data as it comes in, cleans it, filters it and applies analytics, such as anomaly detection, before it goes to the cloud.

FogHorn's technology improves on older industrial control systems that don't operate in real time, he said those systems are hard-wired and look for signals, which they send to a human operator who's supposed to do something.

Mr. King says not enough progress has been made even though Silicon Valley tech companies have done some work on industrial computing. Microsoft Corp ., for instance, put Windows servers on factory floors years ago, and Cisco Systems Inc ., which coined the term "fog computing," developed secure ways to get data to the cloud.

FogHorn is especially useful for a company like GE Digital, which employs thousands of data scientists and has machine-learning libraries already developed for its own use, according to Mr. King.

The new money will be used to develop markets for FogHorn in North America, Europe and Asia Pacific with help from the corporate investors GE Digital, Bosch and Yokogawa.

Sumant Mandal and James Montgomery from March Capital Partners are on the board. Also on the board is TM Ravi, co-founder of The Hive, a Palo Alto, Calif.-based Big Data studio that started Foghorn.

A pair of twin brothers have raised $9 million from investors including American Express Ventures to build a chatbot, dubbed Mezi, for shopping.

Mez Mobile Inc., which produces the app, uses a combination of human shoppers and artificial intelligence to help customers book their next flight or buy clothing. In addition to American Express Ventures, Saama Capital, Nexus Ventures and other angels participated in the Series A round.

Mezi is the latest venture from brothers Swapnil Shinde and Snehal Shinde, who co-founded Dhingana Inc . The Indian music-streaming service was acquired by Rdio in March 2014. The Shinde brothers both hold master's in computer science from University of Southern California and have always worked together, with previous positions at Yahoo , Symantec and IBM Software Labs.

Chatbots, or programs that simulate natural conversations, have become popular in recent months with major messaging platforms, such as Facebook Messenger, beginning to support them. But while many chatbots now allow you to shop at one store, Mezi can help you shop at many different stores.

When Messrs. Shinde set out to build Mezi, they initially aimed to build a general virtual assistant. But soon into beta testing they realized they needed to specialize and focused in on shopping. The app launched in December and is available for iOS and over SMS texting.

With American Express Ventures making a strategic investment, the brothers said they would likely have more opportunities to work with the company and use its customer data to build better algorithms in the future. American Express offers a concierge service to its platinum card holders in which its employees assist customers with travel bookings and shopping.

B12 is using machine intelligence and automation to help companies design websites.

The startup, which is incorporated as Unlimited Labs Inc., said on Monday it has raised $12.4 million in a Series A round led by General Catalyst Partners . Breyer Capital, Founder Collective, SV Angel and other individuals participated in the round, which closed in April.

The company has built a platform called Orchestra, which can help its network of freelance web designers find experts.

The New York startup's announcement comes as a wave of startups using artificial intelligence to augment humans are attracting interest from venture capitalists. While some companies are using machine learning to totally replace tasks that were previously accomplished by humans, many others are focusing on ways to keep humans in the equation and make them more efficient. This is particularly common in areas like customer support, marketing or security, where a human touch is still needed.

Nitesh Banta, a former General Catalyst Partners principal, and Adam Marcus , a former Locu Inc . employee, launched B12 because they say human-assisted artificial intelligence is the future of work. The team focused first on building websites because there is a lot of open data they could use to improve their algorithms, and they also think human-assisted AI works best in creative and analytical jobs.

Mr. Banta said B12 differentiates itself from others in the artificial intelligence category because they think the future of their business will always include human workers, but it will just give them the time and freedom to take on more tasks.

"We're focused on helping people accomplish more and elevate the work they do," he said.

B12 charges $2,000 a year or $199 a month for the service. The company has 11 employees.

Transtar Holding Co.

Missed interest payment

S&P Global Ratings cut the ratings of auto parts manufacturer Transtar Holding Co.'s corporate credit rating to D from CCC+ after the company missed an interest payment on $370 million term loan. The drivetrain parts maker, based in Walton, Ohio, is backed by San Francisco private equity firm Friedman Fleischer & Lowe. "Transtar missed the last interest payment on its term loan and we expect that it will enter into a comprehensive debt restructuring support agreement, which may involve capital infusions from its existing lenders and sponsors, following the recent expiration of its forbearance agreement," said S&P Global credit analyst Nishit Madlani. The private-equity-backed company, which employs 2,300 people, was founded by Monte Ahuja in 1975. It had estimated revenue last year of $643.7 million, according to data provided by FactSet.

Answers Corp.

Ratings downgrade

S&P Global Ratings Wednesday cut its corporate credit rating on Internet advertising and data analytics company Answers Corp. to CCC from CCC+, its $40 million revolver due 2019 and $325 million first-lien term loan due 2021 to CCC from CCC+ and its $180 million second-lien term loan due 2022 to CC from CCC-. "The downgrade reflects our expectation of worsening credit metrics through the end of 2016, primarily because of weakness in the company's promoted content business, Answers.com," said S&P Global Ratings credit analyst Kenneth Fleming. Answers Corp. operates in two business lines: Answer Cloud Services (ACS), which was built through a number of acquisitions over the past four years and offers content management and data analytic services for brands and online retailers; and Answers.com, which generates revenue through advertising.

Companies may be included on this list for a variety of reasons. The most common include these events:

 " default - the company defaults on a principal or interest payment

 " covenant problems - the company violates covenants on its debt agreement

 " downgrade - the company's debt is significantly downgraded by a public ratings agency, or a ratings agency indicates the company is vulnerable to default

 " going concern doubt - the company's auditors question whether the company has the ability to continue as a going concern

 " litigation - a substantial monetary judgment resulting from a lawsuit has been entered against the company.

Inclusion of a company in this category is not intended to suggest that it will file for bankruptcy protection, default on its debt or suffer any other financial failure.

Dentsu Buys Business-to-Business Ad Specialist From Pegasus

By Suzanne Vranica

Dentsu Inc.'s Dentsu Aegis Network acquired Gyro, one of the largest independent ad firms that specializes in business-to-business advertising.

Gyro, which was founded in 1999, had annual revenue of roughly $73 million last year and works on behalf of companies such as Hewlett Packard Enterprise Co., Visa Inc., and AFLAC Inc.

Gyro has been majority owned by private-equity firm Pegasus Capital Advisors since 2008.

Financial terms of the deal with Dentsu weren't disclosed.

With its deal for Gyro, Dentsu is seeking to grab a piece of the growing spending on business-to-business marketing and advertising, which is expected to increase 5% this year in the U.S. to $154 billion, according to research firm Outsell.

B2B advertising is when a company advertises its products or services to other companies or to professional people, not to the general public.

"Many global advertisers and many of our global clients are increasing their investment in B2B marketing," said Rob Horler, chief executive officer of Dentsu Aegis Network in the U.S.

Gyro is the latest deal in a long string of acquisitions by the ad giant, which has been expanding aggressively outside of Japan in an effort to diversify its revenue base. Its deal-making went into overdrive after its $5 billion acquisition of U.K.-based Aegis in 2013.

The company derived 54.3% of its business from markets outside of Japan in fiscal 2015, up from 15% in fiscal 2012.

Dentsu signed a record 36 acquisitions in 2015 and its appetite for deal-making is showing no signs of waning. So far this year, the company has gobbled up companies including digital analytics firm Cardinal Path, digital agency Flock and sports marketing firm Alesport Group.

Dentsu said it would fold its recently launched B2B agency Interprise into Gyro.

Christoph Becker, currently chief executive and chief creative officer of Gyro, will keep those titles at the combined entity, while Stuart Giddings, currently global president of Interprise, will become global network president of Gyro. The Interprise name will only be used in Singapore and Hong Kong.

The deal will give Gyro the resources it needs to expand into growing markets such as Latin America and Asia, said Mr. Becker.

Pairing with Dentsu also gives Gyro the ability to offer clients ad buying and planning services, a function Gyro has largely outsourced.

"We now partner with independent partners [for ad buying] and that is not ideal," said Mr. Becker. "This gives us a media powerhouse and propels our offering."

Abreast of the Market
Low-Volatility Funds Offer Peace of Mind

By Aaron Kuriloff 
902 words
25 July 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Investors are pouring billions of dollars into funds that promise to minimize market swings, highlighting the anxiety that prevails after seven years of stock gains.

Low- and minimum-volatility funds are designed to fluctuate less than the market as a whole -- not climbing as high during rallies but not falling as far during selloffs. That kind of risk avoidance has been popular this year. The top-five low-volatility exchange-traded funds added a net $12.5 billion through June 30, even as investors yanked roughly $52 billion from U.S. equity funds, according to estimates from Chicago-based investment researcher Morningstar Inc.

"It helps me sleep well at night," said Narendra Mulani, chief analytics officer at Accenture, who has added to his low-volatility investments this year.

It is another illustration of the rising cost of peace of mind for skittish investors. Proponents of the approach say it helps investors weather market swings, while providing higher returns over the long run. Critics, however, say they have become trendy investments whose benefits could erode if interest rates rise or markets continue to climb.

The stocks within the iShares Edge MSCI Minimum Volatility USA ETF have gained substantially in 2016 to trade at an average of roughly 26 times their trailing 12 months of earnings. That is 30% higher than the iShares Core S&P 500 ETF's P/E of 20.

Such premiums are now common for funds that some investors and analysts said often concentrate in assets such as utility stocks or real-estate investment trusts that have performed well recently but show limited future potential and typically lag behind the market in periods of high confidence or strong growth.

"If you're paying too high a price, you may be taking on the risk you're trying to avoid," said Jim Stephenson, portfolio manager for the Nuveen NWQ Global Equity Income Fund.

Two of the most popular funds have been among the big gainers this year. Investors added about $6.2 billion to the iShares minimum-volatility ETF in the first half of 2016, according to Morningstar, which includes a collection of relatively stable stocks such as General Mills Inc., Johnson & Johnson and AT&T Inc. The PowerShares S&P 500 High Dividend Low Volatility Portfolio ETF, meanwhile, attracted about $1.4 billion to a fund that selects dividend payers, including financial firms such as data-storage giant Iron Mountain Inc., and utilities including Spectra Energy Corp.

The iShares ETF has risen 13% in 2016, while the PowerShares fund has climbed 19%, according to FactSet.

Robert Nestor, head of U.S. iShares smart-beta strategy at BlackRock Inc., credits most of that fund's 2016 gains to a stable run in January and February. When the S&P 500 tumbled more than 10% at the beginning of the year, it dropped less than 5%. During the sharp selloff after the U.K.'s vote to leave the European Union, it gained 2.2%.

The iShares fund also lagged behind both recoveries, rising 9.7% between the February low and mid-April while the S&P 500 gained 13%. Since the

Brexit selloff ended June 27, the fund has gained 6.5%, as the S&P 500 rose 8.7%.

"The whole idea is to give a smoother ride to investors," Mr. Nestor said.

The funds reflect research that suggests investors tend to overpay for stocks that move more than the broader market and underpay for slow-moving shares, even though those do better in the long run by minimizing losses when the market falls, said Nick Kalivas, senior equity product strategist at Invesco PowerShares. So the theory is that sacrificing some gains when the market rallies, to minimize losses when things sour, pays off.

"This is a way to get equity exposure where you can capture some of the upside and mitigate some of that downside," he said.

Some financial advisers said that if people flee to low-volatility strategies in times of worry, they could miss out when stocks rally -- buying high and selling low. A report issued in February by Newport Beach, Calif.-based Research Affiliates also found that some smart-beta strategies have outperformed the market because their popularity has driven up valuations of previously unloved companies unsustainably.

The high-dividend stocks typically held by low-volatility ETFs could also sell off when interest rates rise.

"If interest rates ever start to rise, we could see those stocks do as poorly on a relative basis as they have done well recently," said Eric Nelson, an investment adviser at Servo, a wealth-management firm in Oklahoma City.

Low-volatility strategies are aimed at reducing risk, not chasing returns, and many take measures against overconcentrating in a limited number of sectors, several analysts said. The iShares ETF, for example, contains shares of Newmont Mining Corp., even though mining stocks tend to fluctuate more than the broader market. It acts as a balance to holdings such as utilities, said BlackRock's Mr. Nestor.

Supporters also noted that economic and market conditions may matter more to the performance of low-volatility strategies than valuations. And despite the billions attracted by low-volatility funds, they remain a fraction of the overall stock market.

Abreast of the Market
Investors Paying Up For Peace Of Mind

By Aaron Kuriloff 
917 words
25 July 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Investors are pouring billions of dollars into funds that promise to minimize market swings, highlighting the anxiety that prevails after seven years of stock gains.

So-called low- and minimum-volatility funds are designed to fluctuate less than the market as a whole -- not climbing as high during rallies but not falling as far during selloffs. That kind of risk avoidance has been popular this year. The top-five low-volatility exchange-traded funds added a net $12.5 billion through June 30, even as investors yanked roughly $52 billion from U.S. equity funds, according to estimates from Chicago-based investment researcher Morningstar Inc.

"It helps me sleep well at night," said Narendra Mulani, chief analytics officer at Accenture, who has added to his low-volatility investments this year.

It is the latest illustration of the rising cost of peace of mind for skittish investors. Proponents of the approach say it helps investors weather market swings, while providing higher returns over the long run. Critics, however, say they have become trendy investments whose benefits could erode if interest rates rise or markets continue to climb.

The stocks within the iShares Edge MSCI Minimum Volatility USA ETF have gained substantially in 2016 to trade at an average of roughly 26 times their trailing 12 months of earnings. That is 30% higher than the iShares Core S&P 500 ETF's P/E of 20.

Such premiums are now common for funds that some investors and analysts said often concentrate in assets such as utility stocks or real-estate investment trusts that have performed well recently, but show limited future potential and typically lag behind the market in periods of high confidence or strong growth.

"If you're paying too high a price, you may be taking on the risk you're trying to avoid," said Jim Stephenson, portfolio manager for the Nuveen NWQ Global Equity Income Fund.

Two of the most-popular funds have been among the big gainers this year. Investors added about $6.2 billion to the iShares minimum-volatility ETF in the first half of 2016, according to Morningstar, which includes a collection of relatively stable stocks such as General Mills Inc., Johnson & Johnson and AT&T Inc. The PowerShares S&P 500 High Dividend Low Volatility Portfolio ETF, meanwhile, attracted about $1.4 billion to a fund that selects dividend payers, including financial firms such as data-storage giant Iron Mountain Inc., and utilities including Spectra Energy Corp.

The iShares ETF has risen 13% in 2016, while the PowerShares fund has climbed 19%, according to FactSet.

Robert Nestor, head of U.S. iShares smart-beta strategy at BlackRock Inc., credits most of that fund's 2016 gains to a stable run in January and February. When the S&P 500 tumbled more than 10% at the beginning of the year, it dropped less than 5%. During the sharp selloff after the U.K.'s vote to leave the European Union, it gained 2.2%.

The iShares fund also lagged behind both recoveries, rising 9.7% between the February low and mid-April while the S&P 500 gained 13%. Since the Brexit selloff ended June 27, the fund has gained 6.5%, as the S&P 500 rose 8.7%.

"The whole idea is to give a smoother ride to investors," Mr. Nestor said.

The funds reflect research that suggests investors tend to overpay for stocks that move more than the broader market, and underpay for slow-moving shares, even though those do better in the long run by minimizing losses when the market falls, said Nick Kalivas, senior equity product strategist at Invesco PowerShares. So the theory is that sacrificing some gains when the market rallies, to minimize losses when things sour, pays off.

"This is a way to get equity exposure where you can capture some of the upside and mitigate some of that downside," he said.

Some financial advisers said that if people flee to low-volatility strategies in times of worry, they could miss out when stocks rally -- buying high and selling low. A report issued in February by Newport Beach, Calif.-based Research Affiliates also found that some so-called smart-beta strategies have outperformed the market because their popularity has driven up valuations of previously unloved companies unsustainably.

Low-volatility strategies are aimed at reducing risk, not chasing returns, and many take measures against overconcentrating in a limited number of sectors, several analysts said. The iShares ETF, for example, contains shares of Newmont Mining Corp., even though mining stocks tend to fluctuate more than the broader market. It acts as a balance to holdings such as utilities, said BlackRock's Mr. Nestor.

Supporters also noted that economic and market conditions may matter more to the performance of low-volatility strategies than valuations. And they remain a fraction of the overall stock market.

"If you continue to have sluggish growth and a low-rate environment, people are going to continue to be very interested," said Mr. Kalivas, of Invesco.

Phil DeMuth, a financial adviser at Conservative Wealth Management, in Los Angeles, who advises Mr. Mulani, said he has used the funds because they are comforting to investors. "What we're looking for is a friend in bad times," he said. "I'm really in it for the defensive part."

Abreast of the Market
Investors Clamber for Calm

By Aaron Kuriloff

Such premiums are now common for funds that some investors and analysts said often concentrate in assets such as utility stocks or real-estate investment trusts that have performed well recently, but show limited future potential and typically lag behind the market in periods of high confidence or strong growth.

"If you're paying too high a price, you may be taking on the risk you're trying to avoid," said Jim Stephenson, portfolio manager for the Nuveen NWQ Global Equity Income Fund.

When the S&P 500 tumbled more than 10% at the beginning of the year, it dropped less than 5%. During the sharp selloff after the U.K.'s vote to leave the European Union, it gained 2.2%.

The iShares fund also lagged behind both recoveries, rising 9.7% between the February low and mid-April while the S&P 500 gained 13%. Since the Brexit selloff ended June 27, the fund has gained 6.5%, as the S&P 500 rose 8.7%.

"The whole idea is to give a smoother ride to investors," Mr. Nestor said.

So the theory is that sacrificing some gains when the market rallies, to minimize losses when things sour, pays off.

"This is a way to get equity exposure where you can capture some of the upside and mitigate some of that downside," he said.

Some financial advisers said that if people flee to low-volatility strategies in times of worry, they could miss out when stocks rally -- buying high and selling low.

A report issued in February by Newport Beach, Calif.-based Research Affiliates also found that some so-called smart-beta strategies have outperformed the market because their popularity has driven up valuations of previously unloved companies unsustainably.

The high-dividend stocks typically held by low-volatility ETFs could also sell off when interest rates rise.

"If interest rates ever start to rise, we could see those stocks do as poorly on a relative basis as they have done well recently," said Eric Nelson, an investment adviser at Servo, a wealth-management firm in Oklahoma City.

Low-volatility strategies are aimed at reducing risk, not chasing returns, and many take measures against overconcentrating in a limited number of sectors, several analysts said.

The iShares ETF, for example, contains shares of Newmont Mining Corp., even though mining stocks tend to fluctuate more than the broader market. It acts as a balance to holdings such as utilities, said BlackRock's Mr. Nestor.

Supporters also noted that economic and market conditions may matter more to the performance of low-volatility strategies than valuations. And despite the billions attracted by low-volatility funds, they remain a fraction of the overall stock market.

"If you continue to have sluggish growth and a low-rate environment, people are going to continue to be very interested," said Mr. Kalivas, of Invesco.

Phil DeMuth, a financial adviser at Conservative Wealth Management, in Los Angeles, said he has used the funds because they are comforting to investors. Their climb this year is just a windfall gain, he said.

"What we're looking for is a friend in bad times," he said. "I'm really in it for the defensive part. The other part is nice, but that's not the main feature."

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

Apama Medical Inc., Campbell, Calif., a medical-device company focused on the development of atrial fibrillation technologies, has sold $9 million of a $13 million equity round. In January 2015, the company received $11 million of a planned $17.5 million Series B financing led by Ascension Ventures with participation from investors including Medvance Incubator Partners and Onset Ventures.

Cignifi Inc ., Cambridge, Mass., a big-data startup whose technology predicts a person's credit worthiness based on their call records and other mobile phone usage patterns, has issued two-thirds of a $1.25 million debt offering. Two previous filings going back to 2014 showed the company issuing a total of $5.4 million, mostly in equity. American Express Ventures and Omidyar Network are backers.

Elihuu Inc., Oakland, Calif., which helps connect designers with manufacturers, has issued $1.5 million of a $2.7 million round of equity and rights. A 2014 filing showed the company issuing $220,000 of a $750,000 offering of securities and convertible promissory notes. The company, which does business as BriteHub, is listed in the portfolio of Techstars.

Kindara Inc., Boulder, Colo., which develops mobile applications and connected devices to give women more information about their reproductive health, has sold more than half of a $1 million equity round. nVision Medical Corp., San Bruno, Calif., which develops technology focusing on female health and diagnostic services, has sold $6.5 million of a $12 million equity round. Catalyst Health Ventures counts the company in its portfolio.

NATIONAL NEWS
Better data risk making some people uninsurable; Individual pricing

By Oliver Ralph, Insurance Correspondent 
530 words
22 July 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
02
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

More sophisticated use of data could create an "underclass" of people who cannot afford insurance. According to a report from the Chartered Insurance Institute, consumers could miss out on some types of cover altogether if insurers deem them too risky.

Big data are one of the insurance industry's great hopes for the future. Established insurers and a host of start-ups are investing millions in systems to better understand the information they hold about customers and to collect more. They hope that by better analysing the risks each policyholder faces, they can price products more accurately and advise customers on how to avoid problems.

However, the CII paper warns that using data in this way threatens the concept of pooling risk on which the industry was founded.

"Data is a double-edged sword," said David Thomson, director of policy and public affairs at the CII. "The insurance sector needs to be careful about moving away from pooled risk into individual pricing. They need to think about the broader public interest."

The report says that the concept of pooling risk "underpins the effectiveness of insurance cover". It adds: "Some people may be identified as such high risk to insurers that they are priced out of insurance altogether. Big data could, in effect, create groups of 'uninsurable' people. While in some cases this may be to do with modifiable behaviour, like driving style, it could easily be due to factors that people can't control, such as where they live, age, genetic conditions or health problems."

The issue of genetic data is a particularly contentious one. In theory, genetic data could be useful to insurers when deciding how to price life or health insurance. Because of the ethical questions this poses, an agreement signed in 2000 between the government and the Association of British Insurers stops the industry from using predictive genetic test results. The agreement runs until 2019, although a review is due this year.

"You could price people out of the market for health products. There's a danger insurers will not offer health cover to some people. The government would intervene if people are doing social sorting," said Mr Thomson.

Better use of data in other areas has already forced the government to act. Improved mapping and data analysis have allowed insurers more accurately to assess which homes and businesses run a high risk of flooding. Many people complained that the resulting prices made cover unaffordable for people living in areas at risk.

So the government created Flood Re , a new organisation that aims to lower the cost of insurance for people who live in areas prone to flooding. It started operating in April this year and is funded by a levy on the insurance industry, which in turn passes it on to all home insurance customers. Flood Re does not apply to business insurance.

"Regulators are trying to catch up on this issue," said Mr Thomson. "So there is a huge emphasis on insurers to guard their own reputations and business models. As in banking, algorithms can be good and bad."

MARKETS & INVESTING
Equities remain on a high as ECB leaves monetary policy unchanged; Global overview

By FT reporters 
887 words
22 July 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
19
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Benchmark bond yields start inching lower and single currency wobbles after central bank keeps refinancing rate at zero

Global stocks clung on to eight-month highs as benchmark bond yields began inching lower as the single currency wobbled after the European Central Bank left monetary policy unchanged.

The FTSE All World equity index hovered at its best mark since early November after Wall Street's S&P 500 closed on Wednesday at a fresh high of 2,173. The S&P slid 0.2 per cent going into the New York afternoon to 2,167.69.

The pan-European Stoxx 600, which had rebounded more than 10 per cent from the post-Brexit low hit on June 27, closed 0.1 per cent lower.

The single currency fell 0.1 per cent to $1.1008 and benchmark government bonds yields also moved lower.

German 10-year Bund yields dropped by less than one basis point to 0.02 per cent. US 10-year Treasury yields lost 1bp to 1.57 per cent.

It followed the ECB's decision yesterday to hold policy constant, as expected. It kept its main refinancing rate at zero and the deposit rate at minus 0.4 per cent while maintaining the  80bn level of monthly asset purchases.

In his press conference, president Mario Draghi struck a moderately dovish tone and reiterated he remained willing to use all the stimulatory tools at his disposal if required. But he said the ECB had not discussed changing its easing rules to allow it to expand its asset purchase programme.

However, some analysts thought the ECB was still likely to do more QE.

"The immediate risk of financial market contagion after the UK [Brexit] vote has subsided, an economic slowdown in the eurozone is still likely, pushing inflation down," said Tomas Holinka, economist at Moody's Analytics. "Below-target inflation until 2019 will force the ECB to keep the main refinancing rate at zero in the three-year horizon, while extending the asset purchases."

The ECB's UK and Japanese peers are also expected to loosen policy further in coming weeks. And the trend for more central bank largesse, alongside some generally well-received corporate earnings in Europe and the US , has helped buoy stock markets of late.

Base metal prices held on to gains that took copper to a two-week high, though Brent crude dropped 1.5 per cent to $46.45 a barrel after rallying 1.1 per cent on Wednesday when US data showed a ninth consecutive weekly fall in oil inventories. Dollar-denominated commodities were helped by the buck slipping back from a four-month peak.

The dollar index, which on Wednesday rose to 97.23 on expectations that better US economic data may encourage the Fed to raise interest rates this year, eased 0.2 per cent to 97.01.

Sterling traded flat at $1.3208 - still well below the $1.50 at which it traded when the Brexit polls closed on June 23 - after a report showed UK retail sales fell 0.9 per cent in June. But gilt yields are tracking peers, adding 2bp at 0.85 per cent.

The yen was a standout underperformer, initially weakening on reports that policymakers are considering stimulus measures at the upper end of the previously forecast 10tn-20tn range.

The yen moved through 107 per dollar, its softest in six weeks and a fall that helped the export-sensitive Nikkei 225 rise 0.8 per cent, taking its gains over the last eight sessions to 11.3 per cent.

Nikkei futures later fell back, however, and the yen traded 1 per cent stronger at 105.88 after the BBC aired an interview with BoJ governor Haruhiko Kuroda recorded a month ago in which he said there was "no need and no possibility" for the central bank to embark on a policy of so-called "helicopter money". Tokyo's earlier rally was in keeping with the mood across much of the region.

Hong Kong's Hang Seng index, which tends to track Wall Street quite closely, was up 0.5 per cent, while on the Chinese mainland the Shanghai Composite rose 0.4 per cent and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 added 0.4 per cent.

New Zealand's S&P/NZX 50 rose 0.6 per cent to a new record high as the Kiwi dollar dipped below US$0.70 after the Reserve Bank of New Zealand - which delivered a surprise rate cut in March - said monetary policy would "continue to be accommodative".

Gold moved up over $15 to $1,331 an ounce, welcoming the softer dollar and lingering tensions over Turkey.

The Turkish lira, which fell overnight to a record low of TL3.096 per greenback on worries that a post-failed coup crackdown by President Recep Tayyip Erdogan will exacerbate tensions, recovered 0.4 per cent to TL3.0783.

But the Istanbul stock market shed 4.4 per cent to a five-month low while Ankara's implied 10-year borrowing costs moved above 10 per cent.

Reporting by Jamie Chisholm in London, Joe Rennison in New York and Jennifer Thompson in Hong Kong

Hellman & Friedman will take a $900 million stake in Genesys , a maker of contact-center software that is backed by Permira and Technology Crossover Ventures , according to a news release.

The company, also known as Genesys Telecommunications Laboratories Inc ., said the investment represents an enterprise value of about $3.8 billion. "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0020111019e7ajblwyi" for Genesys at that time, with Technology Crossover Ventures also providing equity, WSJ Pro Private Equity reported at the time.

Genesys is profitable, and the $3.8 billion valuation is about four times the company's revenue and would represent a four-times return on Permira 's initial investment, said two people familiar with the matter.

The company has made a number of add-on acquisitions since Permira 's investment, including two Brazilian technology businesses; social media analytics company Solariat; and CanaPlus Consulting Pte., a maker of contact-center technology based in Singapore.

Founded in 1990, Genesys makes software and provides services for managing call centers and interacting with customers. It has more than 4,700 customers in 120 countries, according to the release.

Hellman & Friedman has a history of making deals with Permira , having acquired Renaissance Learning Inc ., an educational analytics company, from Permira in 2014. Hellman & Friedman will take a $900 million stake in Genesys , a maker of contact-center software that is backed by Permira and Technology Crossover Ventures , according to a news release.

The company, also known as Genesys Telecommunications Laboratories Inc ., said the investment represents an enterprise value of about $3.8 billion. European firm Permira , along with Technology Crossover Ventures and other original investors, will continue to own a majority stake in the Daly City, Calif., company, according to the release.

The valuation represents a significant step up from Permira 's 2012 investment in Genesys via a carve-out acquisition from telecommunications equipment maker Alcatel-Lucent SA . Permira . "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0020111019e7ajblwyi" for Genesys at that time, with Technology Crossover Ventures also providing equity, WSJ Pro Private Equity reported at the time.

Genesys is profitable, and the $3.8 billion valuation is about four times the company's revenue and would represent a four-times return on Permira 's initial investment, said two people familiar with the matter.

The company has made a number of add-on acquisitions since Permira 's investment, including two Brazilian technology businesses; social media analytics company Solariat; and CanaPlus Consulting Pte., a maker of contact-center technology based in Singapore.

Founded in 1990, Genesys makes software and provides services for managing call centers and interacting with customers. It has more than 4,700 customers in 120 countries, according to the release.

Hellman & Friedman has a history of making deals with Permira , having acquired Renaissance Learning Inc ., an educational analytics company, from Permira in 2014. Hellman & Friedman paid $1.1 billion, earning Permira about four times its original investment, WSJ Pro Private Equity "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0020140313ea3dbm2du".

Hellman & Friedman , with offices in New York, San Francisco and London, is currently investing out of its $11.1 billion eighth fund.

Permira invests in the technology, consumer, health-care, financial services and industrials sectors. Other investments include U.K. health-care services company Pantheon Healthcare Group and footwear maker Dr. Martens.

The Palo Alto, Calif.-based Technology Crossover Ventures invests mainly in technology. Earlier this month, the firm invested about $50 million in German eyewear company SuperVista AG.

Sprinklr Raises $105 Million to Grow Social Media Management Software; Funding round values the company at over $1.8 billion

By Jack Marshall 
382 words
21 July 2016
20:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

The chief executive of Sprinklr is Ragy Thomas. An earlier version of this article incorrectly referred to Mr. Thomas as Mr. Ragy. (July 21)

Social media and customer management software company Sprinklr has raised a $105 million funding round at a valuation of over $1.8 billion, the company announced Wednesday morning.

The round, which brings the company's total funding to $239 million, was led by Singapore-based investment firm Temasek. Wellington Management Company LLP and EDBI also participated, along with Sprinklr's existing investors.

Sprinklr's software is designed to help companies manage every aspect of their activity on social media, from listening to consumer feedback to planning, publishing and measuring the content they post to a growing array of digital platforms.

According to CEO Ragy Thomas, Sprinklr will use the money to grow Sprinklr's software platform, and potentially to fund future acquisitions.

"We have ambitious growth numbers; we think we are sitting on incredible growth opportunity, and have the potential to build the next large enterprise software company," Mr. Thomas said.

The company has made three acquisitions in the past 13 months, including social visualization company Postano, audience segmentation firm Booshaka and analytics company NewBrand.

Mr. Thomas said Sprinklr will continue to make acquisitions if and when suitable opportunities present themselves. As the market for social media marketing software matures, he expects those opportunities to continue to arise. According to some industry observers, it's getting harder for marketing and advertising technology companies to raise money.

"We're seeing first-generation social companies hitting their natural growth limit. This funding round gives us the capital to be opportunistic as we see strategic fits," Mr. Thomas said.

Sprinklr's technology now hooks into 23 social networks and platforms and is used by over 1200 brands including Nike, McDonald's, Microsoft, P&G and Samsung, the company said.

According to Mr. Thomas, companies are increasingly seeing "customer experience management" through social media as a competitive advantage.

Sprinklr said it eclipsed $100 million in annual revenue in 2015, and that its $1.83 billion valuation is a reflection of its growth and performance.

Mr. Thomas said taking the company public through an initial public offering is not currently "top of mind."

Morgan Stanley's Wealth Unit Yields Stability for Bank; Loans to clients help drive 12% increase in net interest income; exploring the robo-adviser game

By Anna Prior 
612 words
20 July 2016
17:34
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Morgan Stanley's wealth-management unit muted the effects of volatile markets on revenue during the second quarter, as the bank continues to position the business for profitability and growth as investment advice increasingly goes digital.

Revenue for the New York bank's wealth-management arm in the latest quarter slipped $64 million, or about 2%, from the year-earlier period to $3.81 billion. The unit saw lower commissions and asset-management fees, which fell 14% to $423 million and 4% to $2.08 billion, respectively.

However, net interest income increased by $92 million, or 12%, to $829 million as loans to wealth-management clients grew 19% to a record $69 billion.

"Even though we haven't seen a pickup in retail engagement, [the wealth-management] business continues to provide stability against an uncertain macro backdrop," Chief Financial Officer Jonathan Pruzan said during a conference call Wednesday to discuss earnings.

Profit in Morgan Stanley's wealth arm fell roughly 8% to $516 million from last year, but lower expenses helped pretax profit margin improve from 21% in the first quarter to 23% in the latest quarter. Chief Executive James Gorman previously said the bank has targeted pretax margin for the unit of 23% to 25% for 2017. Overall, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/morgan-stanley-logs-smaller-than-expected-declines-in-profit-revenue-1469012474" but still topped analysts' subdued expectations.

Among other items Morgan Stanley reported along with second-quarter earnings, the bank said its brokerage arm kept its work force basically flat at 15,909.

"Attrition is clearly running low and it's been like that for a while," Mr. Pruzan said on the call. Mr. Gorman added that "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-unveils-retirement-savings-revamp-but-with-a-few-concessions-to-industry-1459936802", which toughens standards for financial advisers and brokers providing advice on retirement accounts, hasn't affected recruitment.

Annualized revenue per broker fell about 2% from last year to $959,000.

Morgan Stanley's brokerage had $12 billion in inflows to fee-based accounts. That helped increase fee-based assets by about $22 billion from the first quarter to $820 billion, which represents about 40% of Morgan Stanley's $2 trillion in client assets.

Meanwhile, the unit is exploring the intersection of technology and investment advice, as executives emphasized the firm's investment in digital initiatives during a discussion of the bank's earnings call.

"Digital is far more than just robo," Mr. Pruzan said on the call. "We think that the right business model and the winning hand here is a combination of advice and [financial advisers] with technology."

The unit's digital strategy, he added, has focused on three main areas including data and analytics, efficiencies around automation, and the various platforms on which clients interact with advisers and the firm.

Traditional wealth-management and brokerage firms have been wrestling with how best to embrace the fast-growing digital investment-advice segment, typically referred to as "robo advisers."

Wells Fargo & Co.'s brokerage and retirement arm has announced it may soon offer its own "http://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargo-considers-robo-service-1436896442", while UBS Group AG's U.S. brokerage said in May that it is "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ubs-teams-with-robo-advice-provider-1463418199" to develop new tools for its brokers.

Morgan Stanley executives didn't discuss any specific robo offerings on Wednesday, but noted the bank has been "investing heavily in this area."

"We believe secular trends are for advice with technology, and that's the business that we are building," Mr. Pruzan said.

Unilever Buys Dollar Shave Club; European consumer- products giant to pay $1 billion for startup in challenge to P&G

By Sharon Terlep 
607 words
20 July 2016
12:29
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

Procter & Gamble's share of the men's razor and blade businesses in the U.S. fell to 59% last year from 71% in 2010, according to Euromonitor An earlier version of this article incorrectly described that market as all of North America.

Four years ago, Michael Dubin launched his company with a hilarious YouTube video poking fun at the pain and expense of shaving. The video went viral and created the Dollar Shave Club. Now Mr. Dubin is selling his startup for a cool $1 billion.

Unilever PLC, the European consumer products giant behind Dove soaps and Axe body sprays, said late Tuesday it was acquiring the Venice, Calif., startup. Terms weren't disclosed, but people familiar with the matter said Unilever is paying $1 billion in cash.

The sale comes about a year after the Dollar Shave Club "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120150622eb6mhrmke" in a $75 million funding round led by Technology Crossover Ventures, according to people familiar with the matter. The company has raised at least $150 million in venture capital and had previously raised funding from investors that also include Forerunner Ventures, Venrock and Pritzker Group Venture Capital.

Dollar Shave Club, which isn't profitable, said it has signed up 3.2 million members for its mail-order service that ships out disposable razors and other grooming products for a flat monthly fee.

It has chipped away at market leader Gillette, which has responded with its own Gillette Shave Club and last year filed a patent-infringement lawsuit.

Mr. Dubin's company has been growing quickly by adding customers as well as new product categories, such as hair gels and most recently body washes. The startup had revenue of $152 million last year and is on track to exceed $200 million in 2016, according to Unilever.

In addition to giving Unilever an entry into the shaving market dominated by U.S. rival Procter & Gamble Co.'s Gillette, Dollar Shave Club's direct-to-consumer business model gives Unilever "unique consumer and data insights," said Kees Kruythoff, president of Unilever North America.

Privately, P&G executives acknowledge the company was caught off guard by success of Dollar Shave Club, which started in 2012. "It was probably on the radar but we weren't necessarily having the right conversation around what might disrupt us," said a person familiar with the company's thinking.

P&G's share of the men's razor and blade businesses in the U.S. fell to 59% last year from 71% in 2010, according to Euromonitor. Dollar Shave Club had 5% of the market last year.

Dollar Shave Club offers to send razor cartridges directly to customers for as little as $1 a month, a model that appealed to the changing shopping habits of consumers fed up with the rising cost of a clean shave.

"The razors were just very cheap, that was the draw," said Kurt Jetta, founder of TABS Group, a retailer and consumer analytics firm.

Mr. Dubin, 37 years old, will remain CEO of Dollar Shave Club. He said Unilever promised Dollar Shave Club autonomy within the company and resources to help fuel more growth. The company will operate as a subsidiary of Unilever. He said Mr. Kruythoff proposed the acquisition after the two executives had spent time discussing a potential Unilever investment in Dollar Shave Club.

"If we can deploy those resources in service of our mission we are that much more," he said in an interview. "It just felt right to me and I really trust the people I met at the Unilever."

Mr. Dubin said he will report to a board that includes top-level Unilever executives.

--Rolfe Winkler contributed to this article.
